Second Proof: Existence Distinct from Essence
Philosophers present syllogism proving bodies contingent via existence-essence distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for the contingency of bodies, which argues that since existence differs from essence in bodies, they must be contingent in themselves and require an external cause.

Core Idea
A body's existence cannot be its own doing—it points to an external cause.

This lesson presents the philosophers' second argument for the contingency of bodies. The proof relies on a fundamental distinction between what a thing is (its essence) and the fact that it exists. By analyzing this relationship, the philosophers demonstrate that bodies cannot be self-sufficient and thus must be contingent, requiring a cause beyond themselves.

Guided Path
1
Understand the syllogism structure
The argument is a categorical syllogism with a minor premise (bodies have existence different from essence) and a major premise (anything with such distinction is contingent). Recognize how the conclusion follows.
The syllogism is: Every body has existence other than its essence. Everything with existence other than its essence is contingent. Therefore, every body is contingent.
2
Examine the minor premise
The minor premise is taken as already established from an earlier chapter. Note why existence and essence are distinct for bodies: bodies are composite and their existence is not self-evident.
The author refers to a previous discussion on existence for details.
3
Analyze the major premise
The proof of the major premise involves a disjunction: either existence is independent of essence (making it self-subsistent) or dependent. Show why the first option contradicts the assumption that existence is an accident of essence.
If existence were independent, it would not be an accident of essence, but we assumed it is an accident.
4
Explore the impossibility of self-causation
The passage argues that essence cannot cause its own existence because a cause must be prior to its effect. Follow the reasoning that leads to an external cause, establishing contingency.
This refutes the possibility that essence itself could be the cause of its own existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية للفلاسفة في إثبات كون الأجسام ممكنة الوجود لذواتها: قالوا: كل جسم فإن وجوده غير ماهيته وكل ما وجوده غير ماهيته، فهو ممكن الوجود لذاته، ينتج: فكل جسم ممكن الوجود لذاته. أما الصغرى: وهي قولنا: كل جسم، فإن وجوده غير ماهيته، فالكلام المستقصى فيه، قد سبق في باب الوجود. وأما الكبرى: وهي أن كل ما وجوده غير ماهيته فهو ممكن الوجود لذاته، فالدليل عليه أن ذلك الوجود، إما أن يكون غنياً عن تلك الماهية، وإما أن يكون محتاجا إليها، فإن كان الأول لزم أن لا يكون ذلك الوجود عارضا لتلك الماهية بل يكون وجودا قائما بنفسه، لا تعلق له بتلك الماهية، لكنا فرضنا أن ذلك الوجود عارض لتلك الماهية. وإن كان الثاني فنقول: كل ما كان محتاجاً إلى الغير فهو ممكن لذاته وكل ممكن لذاته لا بد له من سبب، وذلك السبب إما تلك الماهية أو غيرها، لا جائز أن يكون السبب هو تلك الماهية، لأن كل علة فهي متقدمة بوجودها على المعلول، فلو كانت الماهية علة لوجود نفسها، لكانت تلك الماهية متقدمة بوجودها على وجود نفسها، وذلك محال، فبقي أن يكون سبب ذلك الوجود شيئاً غير تلك الماهية، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو ممكن الوجود لذاته مفتقر في الوجود إلى غيره، فيثبت أن كل جسم فوجوده غير ماهيته، وثبت أن كل ما كان كذلك فهو ممكن الوجود لذاته، ينتج أن كل جسم فهو ممكن الوجود لذاته. واعلم أن الكلام في أنه هل يعقل أن تكون ماهية الشيء علة لوجود نفسه؟ سيأتي بالاستقصاء في باب: إن وجود الله، هل هو نفس ماهيته أم لا؟
The second proof of the philosophers in establishing that bodies are contingent in essence: They said: Every body, its existence is other than its essence, and everything whose existence is other than its essence is contingent in essence, resulting: Every body is contingent in essence. As for the minor premise: It is our statement: Every body, its existence is other than its essence; the exhaustive discussion of it has preceded in the chapter on existence. As for the major premise: It is that everything whose existence is other than its essence is contingent in essence; the proof for it is that that existence is either independent of that essence or in need of it. If the first, then it follows that that existence is not an accident (‘arid) of that essence, but rather an existence subsisting by itself, having no connection to that essence; but we have assumed that that existence is an accident of that essence. And if the second, we say: Everything that is in need of another is contingent in itself, and every contingent in itself necessarily has a cause. That cause is either that essence or something else. It is not possible that the cause is that essence, because every cause is prior in its existence to the effect; so if the essence were a cause of its own existence, then that essence would be prior in its existence to its own existence, which is impossible. So it remains that the cause of that existence is something other than that essence. And everything that is like that is contingent in essence, in need in existence of another. So it is established that every body has existence other than its essence, and it is established that everything like that is contingent in essence, resulting that every body is contingent in essence. And know that the discussion about whether it is conceivable for the essence of a thing to be a cause of its own existence will come in detail in the chapter: Whether the existence of God is identical to His essence or not.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the contingency of bodies: the distinction between existence (wujud) and essence (mahiyya) in bodies. The proof is a syllogism: (1) Every body has existence distinct from its essence. (2) Everything with existence distinct from its essence is contingent. Therefore, every body is contingent. The author then justifies each premise. The minor premise (that bodies have existence distinct from essence) is taken as established from an earlier chapter. The major premise is proven by considering the relationship between existence and essence: if existence is independent of essence, it would be self-subsistent and not an accident of essence, contradicting the assumption that it is an accident; if existence depends on essence, then the essence cannot cause its own existence (since that would involve priority of a thing over itself), so the cause must be external, making the thing contingent. The author notes that the issue of self-causation will be discussed later in the context of God's existence.

Key Terms
وجود (existence)
ماهية (essence)
ممكن الوجود لذاته (contingent in essence)
صغرى (minor premise)
كبرى (major premise)
عارض (accident)
علة (cause)
معلول (effect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that existence being distinct from essence automatically implies contingency without further reasoning.
Correction: The proof requires showing that the existence is dependent on an external cause, not merely that it is distinct. The major premise is proven by eliminating the alternative of independence and arguing against self-causation.

Checkpoint
Why cannot the essence of a thing be the cause of its own existence?
A) Because the essence is not a cause.
B) Because a cause must be prior to its effect, and a thing cannot be prior to itself.
C) Because existence is an accident and cannot be caused.
D) Because the essence is contingent.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between existence and essence in bodies leads to the conclusion that bodies are contingent and require an external cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own existence is not from yourself, then in what areas of your life do you still behave as if you are self-sufficient? When you feel pride in your achievements, do you truly remember that even your ability to achieve—your existence itself—is a gift from another? What would it mean to live each moment with that awareness, and what one concrete step can you take today to root out self-sufficiency from your heart?
?
The philosophers argue that a thing cannot be the cause of its own existence because that would require it to be prior to itself. Yet in our daily lives, we often try to be the cause of our own worth, security, or happiness. Where are you trying to be the cause of something that only causes from outside can provide? How does your reliance on your own efforts expose a subtle atheism in your practical life? Commit to one act of dependence on Allah today that your ego resists.
?
You have just read that every body—including your own body—is contingent, meaning it cannot exist without an external cause. Yet you wake up every morning assuming your body will be there, as if it has a right to exist. How does this proof shatter that assumption? What would it look like to greet each day not as a continuation of your own life, but as a fresh gift from the One who sustains existence at every moment? Write a short prayer of gratitude that acknowledges your utter dependence.

The Initial Premises and the Threefold Division
Author presents premises, disjunction, and threefold division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author establishes the fundamental disjunction about bodies' quiddities and derives the three logical possibilities for the relation between shared volume and differing essences.

Core Idea
Bodies share volume but differ individually; their quiddities may be similar or lead to a threefold division.

The author begins with self-evident truths about bodies: they are equal in volume and distinct as individuals. From these, he poses a crucial disjunction: either their quiddities are the same or they differ. If they differ, the shared volume must be related to the differing essences in one of three ways. This lesson walks through that reasoning, culminating in the threefold division.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening premises
The author states two known facts: bodies are equal in volume (intuitive) and each body is distinct in its individual determination.
These premises are accepted as evident.
2
Follow the disjunction
The author asks: Are bodies different in quiddity or not? If not, they are similar in all quiddities (desired conclusion). If they are different, we must analyze the relation between shared volume and the differing essences.
This splits the argument into two paths.
3
Derive the threefold division
On the assumption that quiddities differ, the shared volume must be distinct from the differing essences. This yields exactly three logical possibilities: (1) differing essences are substances, volume is accident; (2) volume is substance, differing essences are accidents; (3) both are distinct, neither attribute of the other.
The author claims this division is exhaustive (no more).
4
Understand the conclusion of the division
The author pronounces this a valid division covering negation and affirmation, setting up the next stage of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي حصلناه في هذا الباب : أن نقول : لا شك أن الأجسام متساوية في طبيعة الحجمية والمقدار ، وهذا القدر أمر معلوم بالبديهة ، ثم نقول : هذه الأجسام التي علمنا كونها متساوية في طبيعة الحجمية نعلم أيضاً من حالها كون كل واحد منها مغاير للآخر ، ومخالفا له في تعينه الشخصي ، وهذا أيضاً معلوم . ثم نقول : هذه الأشياء المتساوية في الحجمية ، المتباينة في التعين والتشخص ، إما أن تكون مختلفة بالماهية ، أو لا تكون . فإن لم تكن مختلفة بالماهية فحينئذ يصح قولنا : إن الأجسام متماثلة في تمام ماهياتها وحقائقها . وذلك هو المطلوب . وأما إن قلنا : إنها مختلفة بالماهية ، فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير حصل الاستواء بينهما في الحجمية ، والاختلاف في الحقيقة ، وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة ، فوجب أن تكون بحجميتها وبحيزها مغايرة لتلك الحقائق المخصوصة التي لأجلها حصل الاختلاف فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير ينقسم ذلك إلى ثلاثة أقسام، لا مزيد عليها ، لأنه إما أن يقال : إن تلك الأمور التي حصل بها الاختلاف : ذوات ، فتكون الحجمية التي بها حصل الاشتراك صفات . وإما أن يقال بالعكس منه ، وهو : أن تكون الحجمية التي بها حصل الاشتراك ذوات ، وتكون الأمور التي بها حصل الاختلاف صفات وإما أن يقال : إن كل واحد من هذين الاعتبارين مباين عن الآخر ، لا صفة له ولا موصوف به. فهذا تقسيم صحيح دائر بين النفي والإثبات . ثم نقول : والقسم الأول باطل ، ولما بطل ذلك بقي الحق : إما الثاني وإما الثالث ، وعلى كلا التقديرين فالمقصود حاصل، وإنما قلنا : إن القسم الأول باطل ، وذلك لأن الحجمية والمقدار لا شك أنه حاصل في الحيز والجهة ، لأنه لا معنى للمقدار ، إلا الأمر الممتد في الجهات والأحياز، فهذه الحقيقة تكون لا محالة حاصلة في الأحياز والجهات فإذا قلنا : إن هذه الحجمية صفة حالة في تلك الأشياء التي بها حصل الاختلاف . فنقول : تلك الأشياء إما أن تكون حاصلة في الأحياز والجهات ، وإما أن تكون كذلك ، والقسمان باطلان ، أما الأول فلأنه يلزم منه التناقض ، وتقريره : أنا إذا قلنا : الأجسام متساوية في الحجمية وكانت مختلفة باعتبار آخر . فالحجمية التي حصل بها الاستواء تكون لا محالة مغايرة لتلك الاعتبارات التي حصل بها الاختلاف ، وإن كان كذلك فتلك الاعتبارات المتباينة للحجمية ، وتلك الحقائق المغايرة لها ، وجب أن لا يكون لها في حد ذاتها حجمية ولا تحيز البتة وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : لو حكمنا بكونها حاصلة في الأحياز والجهات ، فحينئذ يكون لها امتدادات في تلك الأحياز، وفي تلك الجهات ، فيلزم أن يقال : الشيء الذي ليس له في حد ذاته امتداد وحجمية ، يكون له في حد ذاته امتداد وحجمية ، وذلك يوجب الجمع بين النقيضين ، وهو محال . وأما القسم
And what we have attained in this chapter is to say: There is no doubt that bodies are equal in the nature of volume and magnitude, and this amount is known by intuition. Then we say: These bodies of which we know they are equal in the nature of volume, we also know from their state that each one of them is other than the other and different from it in its individual determination, and this too is known. Then we say: These things equal in volume, diverse in determination and individuation, are either different in quiddity or not. If they are not different in quiddity, then our saying that bodies are similar in all their quiddities and realities is correct, and that is the desired goal. But if we say they are different in quiddity, we say: On this supposition, equality between them in volume is attained, and difference in reality, and that by which participation occurs is other than that by which divergence occurs. So it is necessary that their volume and spatiality be other than those specific realities on account of which the difference occurred. So we say: On this supposition, that divides into three divisions, no more, because it must be said: Either those things by which difference occurs are substances, so that the volume by which sharing occurs is an accident. Or the opposite: that the volume by which sharing occurs is substance, and the things by which difference occurs are accidents. Or it must be said that each one of these two considerations is distinct from the other, neither an accident nor a substance. This is a valid division covering negation and affirmation. Then we say: The first division is false. When that is false, the truth remains either the second or the third. On both suppositions, the objective is attained. Our saying that the first division is false is because volume and magnitude are undoubtedly located in space and direction, because there is no meaning of magnitude except that which is extended in directions and spaces. So this reality necessarily occurs in spaces and directions. If we say that this volume is an accident inhering in those things by which difference occurred, we say: Those things are either located in spaces and directions or not. Both divisions are false. As for the first, because it entails contradiction. Its explanation: If we say bodies are equal in volume and are different in another respect, then the volume by which equality occurs is necessarily other than those considerations by which difference occurs. If that is so, then those considerations diverse from volume and those realities other than it must have in their essence no volume nor spatiality whatsoever. When this is established, we say: If we judge that they are located in spaces and directions, then they would have extensions in those spaces and directions. Thus it would entail that a thing which in its essence has no extension and volume has in its essence extension and volume. That necessitates combining two opposites, which is impossible. As for the second division...
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about the relationship between bodies' volume and their essences when they are considered possibly different in quiddity. The author first states that bodies are intuitively known to be equal in volume and magnitude, and also known to be distinct from each other in individual determination. Given these premises, the author poses a disjunction: either bodies are different in quiddity or not. If they are not different, then they are similar in all quiddities—the desired conclusion. If they are different, then the volume that is shared must be distinct from the differing essences. The author then divides this case into three logical possibilities: (1) the differing things are substances and volume is an accident inhering in them, (2) volume is substance and the differing things are accidents, or (3) both volume and the differing things are distinct without one being a property of the other. The author then argues that the first division is false. The reason is that volume and magnitude are necessarily spatial—they are extended in directions and spaces. If volume were an accident in substances that are not themselves necessarily spatial, then those substances would have to be either spatial or not. If they are spatial, they would have extension, contradicting the idea that in their essence they have no volume. This leads to a contradiction. Therefore, the first division is impossible, leaving the second and third as possible, and in both cases the desired conclusion that bodies are similar in volume is maintained. The argument ends mid-sentence discussing the second division.

Key Terms
حجمية
ماهية
ذوات
صفات
تماثل
التعين الشخصي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if bodies share volume, their essences must be identical.
Correction: The author shows that even if essences differ, the shared volume can be accounted for by a substance-accident relation, and he presents three possibilities.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how many possibilities exist for the relation between shared volume and differing essences when quiddities are different?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
Bodies share volume and differ individually; if quiddities differ, the shared volume relates to the differing essences in exactly three ways.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says every body is 'other than the other' in individual determination. Do you treat every person you meet as uniquely determined by Allah, or do you reduce them to categories? What would it take to see each person as a distinct creation of Allah?
?
All bodies share volume, yet differ individually. In your own life, what common nature do you share with all people, and what unique determination has Allah given you? How can you fulfill the responsibility of that unique determination?
?
If bodies being similar in quiddity is the 'desired goal' of the argument, what does that tell you about the unity and order in creation? How should that affect your trust in the wisdom of the Creator?

Refutation of the First Division: Volume as Accident
Author refutes the first division using the spatial nature of volume

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first of the three possibilities—that differing essences are substances and volume is an accident—is rationally impossible, based on the spatial nature of volume.

Core Idea
Volume is spatial; if it were an accident in non-spatial substances, contradiction arises.

After laying out the three possibilities, the author immediately tackles the first: that the differing essences are substances and volume is an accident inhering in them. He argues that volume and magnitude are necessarily spatial—they are extension in direction. If volume were an accident, the substance it inheres in would have to be either spatial or not. Both options lead to absurdity, as he demonstrates in this tightly reasoned argument.

Guided Path
1
Recall the first division
The first division states that the things causing difference are substances, and volume is an accident. The author now aims to refute this.
2
Understand the nature of volume
The author asserts that volume and magnitude are essentially spatial—they are extension in directions and spaces. This is a key premise.
No volume can exist without being in space.
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
If volume is an accident in the differing essences (substances), then those substances must either be spatial or not spatial. The author argues both are impossible: if spatial, they would have extension contradicting their essence; if not spatial, volume cannot inhere in them.
The argument focuses on the first sub-case (spatial) leading to contradiction.
4
See the contradiction explained
If the substance is spatial, then it has its own extension, but the volume is other than the substance. This would mean a thing that in essence has no extension (the differing essence) has extension, which is impossible.
The author uses the phrase 'combining two opposites'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

حالها كون كل واحد منها مغاير للآخر ، ومخالفا له في تعينه الشخصي ، وهذا أيضاً معلوم . ثم نقول : هذه الأشياء المتساوية في الحجمية ، المتباينة في التعين والتشخص ، إما أن تكون مختلفة بالماهية ، أو لا تكون . فإن لم تكن مختلفة بالماهية فحينئذ يصح قولنا : إن الأجسام متماثلة في تمام ماهياتها وحقائقها . وذلك هو المطلوب . وأما إن قلنا : إنها مختلفة بالماهية ، فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير حصل الاستواء بينهما في الحجمية ، والاختلاف في الحقيقة ، وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة ، فوجب أن تكون بحجميتها وبحيزها مغايرة لتلك الحقائق المخصوصة التي لأجلها حصل الاختلاف فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير ينقسم ذلك إلى ثلاثة ذوات ، وتكون الأمور التي بها حصل الاختلاف صفات وإما أن يقال : إن كل واحد من هذين الاعتبارين مباين عن الآخر ، لا صفة له ولا موصوف به. فهذا تقسيم صحيح دائر بين النفي والإثبات . ثم نقول : والقسم الأول باطل ، ولما بطل ذلك بقي الحق : إما الثاني وإما الثالث ، وعلى كلا التقديرين فالمقصود حاصل، وإنما قلنا : إن القسم الأول باطل ، وذلك لأن الحجمية والمقدار لا شك أنه حاصل في الحيز والجهة ، لأنه لا معنى للمقدار ، إلا الأمر الممتد في الجهات والأحياز، فهذه الحقيقة تكون لا محالة حاصلة في الأحياز والجهات فإذا قلنا : إن هذه الحجمية صفة حالة في تلك الأشياء التي بها حصل الاختلاف . فنقول : تلك الأشياء إما أن تكون حاصلة في الأحياز والجهات ، وإما أن تكون كذلك ، والقسمان باطلان ، أما الأول فلأنه يلزم منه التناقض ، وتقريره : أنا إذا قلنا : الأجسام متساوية في الحجمية وكانت مختلفة باعتبار آخر . فالحجمية التي حصل بها الاستواء تكون لا محالة مغايرة لتلك الاعتبارات التي حصل بها الاختلاف ، وإن كان كذلك فتلك الاعتبارات المتباينة للحجمية ، وتلك الحقائق المغايرة لها ، وجب أن لا يكون لها في حد ذاتها حجمية ولا تحيز البتة وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : لو حكمنا بكونها حاصلة في الأحياز والجهات ، فحينئذ يكون لها امتدادات في تلك الأحياز، وفي تلك الجهات ، فيلزم أن يقال : الشيء الذي ليس له في حد ذاته امتداد وحجمية ، يكون له في حد ذاته امتداد وحجمية ، وذلك يوجب الجمع بين النقيضين ، وهو محال . وأما القسم
And what we have attained in this chapter is to say: There is no doubt that bodies are equal in the nature of volume and magnitude, and this amount is known by intuition. Then we say: These bodies of which we know they are equal in the nature of volume, we also know from their state that each one of them is other than the other and different from it in its individual determination, and this too is known. Then we say: These things equal in volume, diverse in determination and individuation, are either different in quiddity or not. If they are not different in quiddity, then our saying that bodies are similar in all their quiddities and realities is correct, and that is the desired goal. But if we say they are different in quiddity, we say: On this supposition, equality between them in volume is attained, and difference in reality, and that by which participation occurs is other than that by which divergence occurs. So it is necessary that their volume and spatiality be other than those specific realities on account of which the difference occurred. So we say: On this supposition, that divides into three divisions, no more, because it must be said: Either those things by which difference occurs are substances, so that the volume by which sharing occurs is an accident. Or the opposite: that the volume by which sharing occurs is substance, and the things by which difference occurs are accidents. Or it must be said that each one of these two considerations is distinct from the other, neither an accident nor a substance. This is a valid division covering negation and affirmation. Then we say: The first division is false. When that is false, the truth remains either the second or the third. On both suppositions, the objective is attained. Our saying that the first division is false is because volume and magnitude are undoubtedly located in space and direction, because there is no meaning of magnitude except that which is extended in directions and spaces. So this reality necessarily occurs in spaces and directions. If we say that this volume is an accident inhering in those things by which difference occurred, we say: Those things are either located in spaces and directions or not. Both divisions are false. As for the first, because it entails contradiction. Its explanation: If we say bodies are equal in volume and are different in another respect, then the volume by which equality occurs is necessarily other than those considerations by which difference occurs. If that is so, then those considerations diverse from volume and those realities other than it must have in their essence no volume nor spatiality whatsoever. When this is established, we say: If we judge that they are located in spaces and directions, then they would have extensions in those spaces and directions. Thus it would entail that a thing which in its essence has no extension and volume has in its essence extension and volume. That necessitates combining two opposites, which is impossible. As for the second division...
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about the relationship between bodies' volume and their essences when they are considered possibly different in quiddity. The author first states that bodies are intuitively known to be equal in volume and magnitude, and also known to be distinct from each other in individual determination. Given these premises, the author poses a disjunction: either bodies are different in quiddity or not. If they are not different, then they are similar in all quiddities—the desired conclusion. If they are different, then the volume that is shared must be distinct from the differing essences. The author then divides this case into three logical possibilities: (1) the differing things are substances and volume is an accident inhering in them, (2) volume is substance and the differing things are accidents, or (3) both volume and the differing things are distinct without one being a property of the other. The author then argues that the first division is false. The reason is that volume and magnitude are necessarily spatial—they are extended in directions and spaces. If volume were an accident in substances that are not themselves necessarily spatial, then those substances would have to be either spatial or not. If they are spatial, they would have extension, contradicting the idea that in their essence they have no volume. This leads to a contradiction. Therefore, the first division is impossible, leaving the second and third as possible, and in both cases the desired conclusion that bodies are similar in volume is maintained. The argument ends mid-sentence discussing the second division.

Key Takeaway
The first possibility (volume as accident in differing essences) is impossible because volume is spatial and would force the substance to be spatial, contradicting its essence.

What specific action can you take today to reflect this priority?
?
The passage refutes a division that could undermine a larger proof. In your spiritual life, you might have small intellectual concessions—'it doesn't matter'—that could weaken your foundation of faith. Proof That Bodies Cannot Be Equal in Essence
The author proves that if bodies were equal in essence, they would be accidents, not substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that bodies cannot be equal in their complete essence, and why this establishes that differences in bodies arise from their own essences.

Core Idea
Equal essences cannot cause differences, so bodies must differ by essence.

This passage from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents a rigorous dilemma: if all bodies were equal in their fundamental essence (bodiliness), then any differences between them must come from somewhere. The author systematically examines the possibilities and shows each leads to either a contradiction or an impossibility. This argument is crucial for establishing that bodies are distinct by their essences, not just by accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage Carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to the logical flow: the author presents a dilemma and then eliminates options.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the Three Possibilities
The author considers three sources of differentiation if bodies were equal: their own essence, a separate agent, or the receiver of the effect. Locate each in the text.
The first is rejected because the shared essence cannot cause difference; the second is rejected because it would require a preponderance without a preponderant.
3
Understand the Third Possibility
The third possibility is that differences arise from the receiver's prior states. The author argues that this would make equal essences into accidents inhering in receivers, which is impossible.
This leads to the conclusion that equal essences would become attributes, not substances.
4
Appreciate the Conclusion
The author concludes that bodies cannot be equal in their complete essence. Reflect on why this argument is important for substance ontology.
The proof relies on the previously established principle that accident attribution is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرجح - أو لذاته ، وهو محال ، لأن الجسمية مشترك فيها بين الكل ، والتعين المعين غير مشترك فيه ، وما به الاشتراك لا يكون علة لما به الاختلاف ، أو للفاعل المباين وهو محال . لأن الأجسام لما كانت بأسرها متساوية في الماهية المسماة بالجسمية ، كان اختصاص بعضها بقبول ذلك الأثر الخاص من الفاعل المباين حصولا لأحد طرفي الممكن لا لمرجح ، فلم يبق إلا أن يكون ذلك بسبب القابل وهو أن ذلك القابل [ كان قبل حصول هذا التعين موصوفا بأحوال مخصوصة ، لأجلها استعد ذلك القابل ] لقبول هذا الأثر الخاص ، وعلى هذا التقدير ، فالأشياء المتساوية في تمام الماهية ، يمتنع حصول التغاير فيها لا بسبب القابل. فيثبت أن الأجسام لو كانت متساوية في تمام الماهية ، لما كانت ذوات قائمة بأنفسها ، بل كانت صفات حالة في القوابل ، والمحال . وأنتم قد دللتم على أن كون الجسم حالا في المحل أمر محال ، فيثبت أن الأجسام يمتنع كونها متساوية في تمام الماهية .
The preponderant - or in its essence, and that is impossible, because 'bodiliness' (al-jismiyya) is common among all, and specific individuation (al-ta'ayyun al-mu'ayyan) is not common in it, and that by which there is sharing cannot be the cause of that by which there is difference; or [it is] from the separate agent (al-fa'il al-mubayin) and that is impossible. For since bodies in their entirety are equal in the essence called bodiliness, the specification of some of them with accepting that particular effect from the separate agent would be a preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible without a preponderant, so there remains only that this is due to the receiver (al-qabil), that is, that the receiver [was before the occurrence of this individuation described with particular states, on account of which that receiver became prepared] for accepting this particular effect. And on this estimation, things equal in their complete essence cannot have variation among them except due to the receiver. So it is established that if bodies were equal in their complete essence, they would not be substances subsisting by themselves, but rather they would be attributes inhering in receivers, and that is impossible. And you have already demonstrated that the inherence of a body in a subject (mahall) is impossible. So it is established that bodies cannot be equal in their complete essence.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a proof that bodies cannot be equal in their complete essence (tamām al-māhiyya). The author presents a dilemma: if bodies were equal, then their differences must come from either their own essence (which is impossible because the common essence cannot cause difference), or from a separate agent (which leads to preponderance without a preponderant), or from the receiver. The receiver argument leads to the conclusion that equal essences would not be substances but accidents. Since the opponent already holds that a body cannot be an accident, the proof holds. The argument follows the author's own order: first rejecting the essence and separate agent, then considering the receiver, then concluding the absurdity of equal essences.

تعين
تماثل
قابل
فاعل مباين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bodies could be equal in essence yet differ by accidents without affecting their substance status.
Correction: The argument shows that if bodies were equal in essence, they would themselves become accidents (attributes) to account for differences, thus losing their substance status.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, if bodies were equal in their complete essence, what would they be rather than substances?
A) Accidents
B) Separates
C) Powers
D) Attributes

Key Takeaway
Bodies cannot be equal in their complete essence because that would necessitate them being accidents rather than substances, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the author proves that no two bodies can be truly equal in essence, what does that mean for your own uniqueness? How does this truth about creation point to the absolute uniqueness of the Creator?
?
The argument shows that difference cannot arise from sameness. How does this logic challenge any worldview that denies the distinctiveness of each being? How should it affect your gratitude for your own specific existence?
?
When the author says that the receiver (al-qabil) must have prior states that prepare it for its effect, what does this teach you about the importance of your own spiritual preparation before Allah? Are you actively shaping your 'receptivity' to His guidance?

The Third Objection Against Body Dissimilarity and Its Answer
The third objection argues that equal bodies cannot receive different properties, and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against body dissimilarity and how both philosophers and theologians answer it, highlighting the role of causal necessity vs. free divine action.

Core Idea
The real issue is whether specification requires a preponderant or free divine choice.

This passage presents the third and most sophisticated objection to the view that bodies are essentially similar. The argument is that if all bodies share the same essence, then no reason can be found why a particular body has a particular property — not in the essence itself, not in a power, and not in an external agent. The response distinguishes between the philosophers' appeal to causal chains and the theologians' appeal to free divine will. Understanding this debate clarifies the deeper foundations of kalām theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the objection is structured as a disproof of tamāthul.
2
Identify the three candidate preponderants
The objection examines three possible sources of specification: the essence itself, a power in the body, and a separate agent. Each is shown impossible.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Understand the conclusion of the objection
The objection concludes that the claim of similarity leads to absurdity, implying bodies must be essentially different.
4
Examine the two answers
The philosophers answer with a causal chain of states; the theologians appeal to the free agent who specifies without a preponderant.
Note how each school's metaphysics shapes their response.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
This objection highlights the deep link between atomism, causality, and divine will in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : لو كانت الأجسام متساوية في الجسمية ، لكان اختصاص كل منها بصفته المعينة رجحانا لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا المرجح ، لأن المقتضى لحصول تلك الصفة لا يمكن أن يكون ذاتا ، لأن سائر الذوات مساوية لذاته في تمام الماهية مع أن هذه الذوات مختلفة في هذه الصفات ، ولا يمكن أن يكون المقتضى لتلك الصفة المعينة قوة حالة في ذلك الجسم ، لأن الكلام في اختصاص ذلك الجسم بتلك القوة ، كالكلام في اختصاصه بذلك العرض ، فإن كان ذلك لقوة أخرى لزم التسلسل ، وهو محال . ولا يمكن أن يكون للفاعل المباين لأن الذوات لما كانت متساوية في تمام الماهية ، امتنع كون بعضها أولى ببعض الصفات بل كانت نسبة كل الذوات إلى كل الصفات على السوية ، فالقول بأن الفاعل المباين خصص هذه الذات بهذه الصفة دون سائر الذوات ، ودون سائر الصفات [ يكون ] رجحانا لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا المرجح ، وهو محال ، فيثبت أن القول بتماثل الأجسام يفضي إلى هذا المحال . أما إذا قلنا : إنها ذوات مختلفة لذواتها والحقائقها ، زال هذا الإشكال ، فكان هذا القول أولى والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : إن الفلاسفة يقولون : كل حالة حاصلة في محل فهي مسبوقة بحصول حالة أخرى ، ويكون الحالة السابقة تتوجب استعداد المادة لقبول الحالة المتأخرة على التعين . وأما المتكلمون فإنهم يقولون : الفاعل المختار لا يمتنع أن يخصص بعض الذوات ببعض الصفات لا لمرجح . فهذا منتهى الكلام في هذا الباب والله ولي النعم والإحسان
The Third Suspicion: If bodies were equal in bodiliness, then the specification of each of them with its particular property would be a preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other without a preponderant, because the requirer (al-muqtaḍī) for the occurrence of that property cannot be the essence (dhāt), since the other essences are equal to its essence in complete essence while these essences differ in these properties; and it cannot be that the requirer for that particular property is a power (quwwa) inhering in that body, because the discussion about the specification of that body with that power is like the discussion about its specification with that accident; if that is due to another power, it would entail infinite regress, which is impossible. And it cannot be from the separate agent, because since the essences are equal in complete essence, it is impossible for some of them to be more entitled to some properties; rather the relation of all essences to all properties is equal. So the statement that the separate agent specified this essence with this property to the exclusion of other essences and to the exclusion of other properties [would be] a preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other without a preponderant, which is impossible. So it is established that the claim of the similarity (tamāthul) of bodies leads to this impossibility. But if we say that they are essences different in their essences and realities, this difficulty disappears, so this statement is more appropriate. And the answer to the Third Suspicion: The philosophers (al-falāsifa) say: Every state occurring in a subject is preceded by the occurrence of another state, and the preceding state necessitates the preparedness of the matter for accepting the subsequent state in a specific manner. As for the theologians (al-mutakallimūn), they say: The free agent (al-fā‘il al-mukhtār) is not prevented from specifying some essences with some properties without a preponderant. So this is the end of the discourse on this chapter. And God is the Possessor of favors and beneficence.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection (shubha) against the view that bodies are dissimilar in essence, followed by its answer. The objection argues that if bodies share the same essence (bodiliness), then any specific property they have would require a preponderant (murajjih), but no such preponderant can be found: not in the essence itself (since all essences are equal), not in a power inhering in the body (which would require infinite regress), and not in the separate agent (since all essences are equally related to all properties). Therefore, the claim of similarity (tamāthul) leads to absurdity. The author then notes that if we instead say bodies are different in their essences, the difficulty disappears. The answer provides two responses: the philosophers' view that each state is preceded by another state that prepares the matter, and the theologians' view that the free agent can specify without any preponderant. The passage closes the discussion of this chapter.

Key Terms
شبهة
تماثل
فاعل مباين
فاعل مختار
مقتضى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third objection shows that the mutakallimūn view (essential difference) is false.
Correction: The mutakallimūn provide an answer: the free agent can specify without a preponderant, so the objection does not refute their position.

Checkpoint
What is the theologians' (mutakallimūn) answer to the third objection about the specification of bodies?
A) They appeal to infinite regress
B) They say the free agent can specify without a preponderant
C) They accept equal essence
D) They deny free will

Key Takeaway
The third objection forces a choice between causal necessity and free divine action in explaining why bodies have different properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you cannot find a human reason for why your life takes a certain path, do you submit to God's free choice as sufficient, or do you insist that there must be a cause you can understand? Are you willing to accept that God specifies without explaining why?
?
This lesson shows that the mutakallimūn base their response on God's absolute freedom. Does your heart truly rest in the belief that God's will is not bound by any preponderant, or do you secretly desire a world where everything has a reason you can grasp?
?
The philosophers sought necessary causes; the theologians embraced divine freedom. Which approach do you rely on when your own plans fail—do you look for natural explanations, or do you turn to the One who chooses without constraint? Commit today to trusting God's choice even when your mind cannot trace its cause.

First Objection: Equality in Essence Does Not Entail Equality in Receptivity
First objection to the proof and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to the proof that bodily essences are equal in receiving attributes, and the response that individualization prevents necessary equality.

Core Idea
Equality in essence does not force equality in receptivity because individualization matters.

This lesson presents the first objection raised against the proof that all bodily essences are equal in receiving divine attributes. The objector argues that even if bodies share the same essence, their individual differences (ta‘ayyun) can affect what they receive, so equality in essence does not guarantee equality in receptivity. The author then clarifies why this objection does not refute the proof itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and its translation. Notice the structure: 'If it is said...' then the objection, then the author's response.
2
Identify the objection
The objector challenges the premise that equal essences imply equal receptivity. Understand the reasoning.
3
Understand the response
The author explains that individualization (ta‘ayyun) can make a difference in receptivity, so equality in essence is not enough.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself why this objection does not ultimately refute the proof. The commentary will help.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: السؤال على الدليل الذي ذكرتم من وجوه: الأول: لم قلتم إن الذوات الجسمانية متساوية بأسرها في قبول الصفات؟ قوله: المتساويات في تمام الماهية يجب استواؤها في قابلية الصفات، قلنا: هذا ممنوع وبيانه أن بتقدير أن تكون الأجسام بأسرها متساوية في الجسمية فإنه يجب كون كل واحد منها ممتازا عن الآخر بتعينه وبتشخصه ضرورة أن ما به المغايرة مغاير لما به المشاركة وإذا كان كذلك لم يبعد أن يكون ذلك القيد الزائد أعني التعين الخاص معتبرا في المقتضى من أحد الجانبين وكان مانعا من الجانب الآخر وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم يلزم من الاستواء في تمام الماهية حصول الاستواء في القابلية.
If it is said: The objection to the proof you mentioned is from several aspects: First: Why do you say that bodily essences are entirely equal in receiving attributes? Your statement: 'Those equal in complete essence must be equal in the receptivity of attributes' – we say this is denied. Its explanation: Even if all bodies are equal in corporeality, each one is distinguished from another by its individualization (ta‘ayyun) and particularization (tashakhkhus), necessarily because that by which they differ is different from that by which they share. If that is the case, it is not unlikely that that additional restriction, namely specific individualization, is effective on one side and is a preventer on the other. So if that is the case, equality in complete essence does not necessitate the occurrence of equality in receptivity.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector challenges the premise that equal essences imply equal receptivity. The answer is that bodies share corporeality but are distinguished by individualization, which can affect receptivity. Therefore, the equality in essence does not entail equality in receptivity, breaking the original argument.

Correction: The objection only attacks a premise; the author's response shows that the premise is not actually refuted because individualization can break the necessity without disproving the general rule.

The first objection fails because individualization (ta‘ayyun) can affect receptivity, so equality in essence does not guarantee equality in receiving attributes.

Equality of Bodies in Receptivity: Objection and Refutation
Objection and refutation on equality of bodies in receptivity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that bodies equal in essence must be equal in receptivity of accidents, and the refutation based on the role of particular individuation.

Core Idea
Sharing an essence does not guarantee sharing all properties due to individuation.

This lesson presents a key objection against the kalam argument that bodies are equal in receptivity of accidents. The objector claims that if all bodies share the same essence (corporeality), they must be equally receptive. The author refutes this by introducing the concept of particular individuation (al-ta'ayyun al-khas), which can affect receptivity without contradicting shared essence. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the limits of essential equality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the first objection from the opponent: 'Why did you say that bodily essences are all equal in receptivity of attributes?'
2
Understand the objector's reasoning
The objection argues that equality in complete essence (corporeality) implies equality in all properties, including receptivity of accidents.
This is a common logical move — from shared definition to shared attributes.
3
Grasp the refutation
The author denies the implication and explains that each body has a particular individuation (ta'ayyun) that distinguishes it, and this can affect receptivity without breaking the shared essence.
Key phrase: 'that by which difference occurs is different from that by which participation occurs.'
4
Apply the logical principle
The additional restriction (particular individuation) can act as a requirement on one side and a hindrance on the other, so no necessary equality follows from shared essence alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : السؤال على الدليل الذي ذكرتم من وجوه : الأول : لم قلتم : إن الذوات الجسمانية متساوية بأسرها في قبول الصفات ؟ قوله : ( المتساويات في تمام الماهية يجب استواؤها في قابلية الصفات ، قلنا : هذا ممنوع : وبيانه : أن بتقدير أن تكون الأجسام بأسرها متساوية في الجسمية ، فإنه يجب كون كل واحد منها ممتازا عن الآخر بتعينه وبتشخصه ، ضرورة أن ما به المغايرة مغاير لما به المشاركة ، وإذا كان كذلك لم يبعد أن يكون ذلك القيد الزائد، أعني التعين الخاص ، معتبرا في المقتضى من أحد الجانبين ، وكان مانعا من الجانب الآخر ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم يلزم من الاستواء في تمام الماهية ( حصول ) الاستواء في القابلية .
If it is said: The objection to the proof you mentioned is from aspects: First: Why did you say that bodily essences are all equal in receptivity of attributes? Your statement: 'Things equal in complete essence must be equal in receptivity of attributes.' We say: This is prevented. Its explanation: On the assumption that all bodies are equal in corporeality, it is necessary that each one of them is distinguished from the other by its individuation and its particularization, necessarily that by which difference occurs is different from that by which participation occurs. If that is so, it is not unlikely that that additional restriction, namely the particular individuation, is considered in the requirement from one side and is a hindrance from the other side. If the matter is so, then equality in complete essence does not necessitate equality in receptivity.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objection argues that if bodies are equal in essence (corporeality), they must be equally receptive of accidents. The answer distinguishes between common essence and individual determinations (ta'ayyun). While bodies share corporeality, each has a particular individuation that can affect receptivity. Thus equality in essence does not guarantee equality in all properties. The author uses the principle that 'that by which difference occurs is different from that by which participation occurs' to argue that the particular individuation can be a factor in one case and a hindrance in another.

ما به المشاركة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things share the same complete essence, they must be identical in all attributes, including receptivity of accidents.
Correction: Sharing an essence does not entail identity in all properties because each entity has a particular individuation that can affect certain attributes like the capacity to receive accidents.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Equality in complete essence does not guarantee equality in all properties because particular individuation can influence receptivity.

?

Second Objection: Four Counterexamples
Objection listing four counterexamples to equality principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four counterexamples that challenge the principle that things equal in complete essence must share all properties.

Core Idea
Equal essence does not guarantee equal properties — time, modality, and specificity prove otherwise.

Having established the principle that things equal in complete essence share all concomitants, the author now faces a barrage of counterexamples. In this passage, four powerful objections are raised, each demonstrating that equal essences can differ in their properties. These examples test the principle's limits and prepare the ground for its nuanced defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and examples
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the author lists four distinct counterexamples (أحدها، ثانيها، ثالثها، رابعها).
The answer to this objection is not included here; it continues on later pages.
2
Analyze the first example (temporal change)
The first example uses a single thing over two times. In the first time it is necessarily originated, in the second it is necessarily lasting. The variation is small (one thing over time), yet it prevents equality of properties. So greater variation (between two distinct things) should also prevent equality.
This uses the principle of a fortiori argument.
3
Analyze the second example (existence concept)
The concept of existence is one, but the existence of the Necessary Existent cannot occur in possible essences, and vice versa. So two existences equal in complete essence differ in their concomitants (occurrence).
4
Analyze the third and fourth examples (essences and animality)
Third: All essences are equal as essences, but each has a unique necessary attribute distinguishing it. Fourth: Animality in man and horse is equal, but if the principle held, one could interchange animality, which is absurd.
The fourth example is a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how these examples show that equality in essence does not guarantee equality in all properties. The objection is strong but will be answered later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : إن الذي ذكرتموه من أن المتساويات في تمام الماهية يجب استواؤها في كل اللوازم منقوض بصور كثيرة : أحدها : أن الشيء في الزمان الأول من أزمنة وجوده ، واجب الاتصاف بكونه حادثا ، وممتنع الاتصاف بكونه باقيا وفي الزمان الثاني من أزمنة وجوده ينقلب الأمر فيصير واجب الاتصاف بكونه حادثا ومعلوم أن التفاوت بين الشيء الواجب بحسب حصوله في زمانين مختلفين ، أقل من التفاوت الحاصل بين الشيئين المتغايرين ، فإذا كان التفاوت القليل الحاصل في الشيء الواحد بسبب حصوله في وقتين ، قد يصير مانعا من الاستواء في كل الأحكام . فهذا التفاوت الكبير ، أولى بكونه مانعا من وجوب الاستواء في كل الأحكام. وثانيها : إنكم قد دللتم على أن مسمى الوجود في حق واجب الوجود لذاته ، وفي حق ممكن الوجود لذاته، مفهوم واحد. أعني من حيث أنه مسمى الوجود ثم إن وجود واجب الوجود لذاته ، ممتنع الحصول في ذوات الممكنات ، ووجود الممكنات ممتنع الحصول في ذات واجب الوجود لذاته فهذان الوجودان متساويان في تمام الماهية ، مع أنهما مختلفان في بعض اللوازم . وهو الذي ذكرناه ، وثالثها : إن الذوات ، والماهيات ، والحقائق متساوية في كونها ذوات وحقائق وماهيات . ثم إن كل واحد منها اختص بالصفة التي باعتبارها امتاز عن سائر الحقائق والماهيات اختصاصا على سبيل اللزوم . ورابعها : الحيوانية التي في الإنسان مساوية للحيوانية التي في الفرس من حيث إنها حيوانية ، فلو كان ما ذكرتموه صحيحا ، فحينئذ يلزمكم صحبة أن تبقى حيوانية الفرس بعينها مع زوال الفرسية عنها وتبدلها بالإنسانية ، وكذلك .
The second objection: What you have stated that things equal in complete essence must be equal in all concomitants is refuted by many examples: First: A thing in the first time of its existence is necessarily qualified by being originated and prevented from being qualified by being lasting, and in the second time of its existence the matter reverses so it becomes necessarily qualified by being originated and it is known that the variation in one thing according to its occurrence in two different times is less than the variation between two distinct things. So if the slight variation occurring in one thing due to its occurrence in two times can become a hindrance to equality in all judgments, then this great variation is even more worthy of being a hindrance to the necessity of equality in all judgments. Second: You have proven that the meaning of existence in respect to the Necessary Existent in itself and in respect to the possible existent in itself is one concept, i.e., insofar as it is the meaning of existence. Then, the existence of the Necessary Existent in itself is prevented from occurring in the essences of possibles, and the existence of possibles is prevented from occurring in the essence of the Necessary Existent in itself. So these two existences are equal in complete essence, yet they differ in some concomitants, which is what we mentioned. Third: The essences, quiddities, and realities are equal in being essences, realities, and quiddities. Then each one of them is characterized by the attribute by which it is distinguished from other realities and quiddities, necessarily. Fourth: The animality in man is equal to the animality in the horse insofar as it is animality. So if what you mentioned were correct, then it would necessitate that the animality of the horse itself remain while horseness leaves it and changes into humanity, and likewise.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection, which lists four counterexamples to the claim that equal essences must share all properties. The first example: a thing's properties change over time (e.g., from originated to lasting), so temporal variation can prevent equality. The second: existence is one concept, but necessary existence and possible existence differ in their occurrences. The third: essences are equal as essences, but each has unique necessary attributes. The fourth: animality in man and horse is equal, but they cannot be interchanged. The answer to this objection is not given in this window; it continues on later pages.

Correction: These four counterexamples show that equality of essence does not guarantee equality of properties due to temporal, modal, and specific differences.

First Refutation: Temporal Change in Attributes
First of five refutations: temporal change in attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how temporal change within a single thing disproves the claim that things sharing an essential attribute must be equal in all rulings.

Core Idea
Even small changes in one thing over time prevent perfect equality—so greater differences between things certainly do.

In this passage, the author presents the first of five refutations (nuqud) against a key opponent claim: that things sharing a universal essential attribute must be identical in all secondary attributes. The argument uses a simple observation: a single thing changes over time, and that change is enough to break the supposed necessity of equality. If a small difference within one thing over time prevents equality, then a larger difference between two distinct things certainly does.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Note the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
2
Identify the two temporal states
The passage describes two moments: the first time of existence (when the thing is necessarily originated) and the second time (when it is still originated but now also possibly enduring). Notice how the ruling changes.
The thing is always 'originated' in both times, but the set of necessary/impossible attributes shifts.
3
Understand the disparity comparison
The author claims the disparity within one thing across two times is less than the disparity between two distinct things. Ask yourself: why is that relevant?
This is an argument a fortiori (first principle of priority).
4
Trace the logical conclusion
Since even the small intra-thing disparity prevents full equality across times, the larger inter-thing disparity is even more effective at preventing equality in all rulings. That refutes the opponent's claim.
The opponent had argued that shared universal attributes force equal rulings; the author shows a counterexample.
5
Reflect on the refutation's force
Consider how this argument works as a refutation (naqd) rather than an independent proof. It only needs a single case where the opponent's principle fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها : أن الشيء في الزمان الأول من أزمنة وجوده ، واجب الاتصاف بكونه حادثا ، وممتنع الاتصاف بكونه باقيا . وفي الزمان الثاني من أزمنة وجوده ينقلب الأمر فيصير واجب الاتصاف بكونه حادثاً . ومعلوم أن التفاوت بين الشيء الواجب بحسب حصوله في زمانين مختلفين ، أقل من التفاوت الحاصل بين الشيئين المتغايرين ، فإذا كان التفاوت القليل الحاصل في الشيء الواحد بسبب حصوله في وقتين ، قد يصير مانعا من الاستواء في كل الأحكام . فهذا التفاوت الكبير ، أولى بكونه مانعا من وجوب الاستواء في كل الأحكام.
First: A thing in the first time of its times of existence is necessarily qualified by being originated (ḥādith) and impossible to be qualified by being everlasting (bāqī). In the second time of its times of existence, the matter reverses so that it becomes necessarily qualified by being originated. And it is known that the disparity between one necessary thing according to its occurrence in two different times is less than the disparity between two distinct things. So if the small disparity occurring in one thing due to its occurrence in two times can prevent equality in all rulings, then this greater disparity is more deserving of being a preventer of the necessity of equality in all rulings.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents the first of five refutations (nuqūḍ) against a position that equates things based on shared essential attributes. The author argues that a single entity undergoes a change in its attributes over time: in the first moment of its existence it is necessarily described as originated (ḥādith) and cannot be described as everlasting (bāqī); in the second moment it is still originated but also now has the possibility of continuing. The disparity within the same thing across two times is less than the disparity between two different things. Yet even that smaller disparity prevents the thing from being identical in all rulings across both times. Therefore, the greater disparity between two distinct things is even more decisive in preventing the necessity of equality in all rulings. The argument refutes the claim that things sharing a universal attribute must share all secondary attributes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things share a universal attribute, they must be equal in all secondary attributes.
Correction: Temporal change within a single thing shows that even the same thing is not equal in all rulings across time, so different things are even less likely to be equal.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The first refutation shows that the disparity within one thing over time is sufficient to break the necessity of equality; a fortiori, the greater disparity between distinct things also breaks it.

The argument moves from a necessary attribute (origination) to a change in possibility (the impossibility of everlastingness falls away). Is there a sin or state you have deemed permanent, when perhaps its removal is possible? Name it and resolve to ask Allah for change tonight.

Third Counter-Argument: Essences Have Necessary Distinguishing Properties
Third refutation: essences are equal generically but each has a necessary distinguishing property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third refutation argues that despite all essences being equal as essences, each essence necessarily possesses a unique property that distinguishes it from others.

Core Idea
Shared generic equality does not prevent each essence from having its own necessary distinguishing property.

In this third counter-argument, the author addresses an objection that might arise from the earlier points. The opponent might claim that if all essences are equal in their essencehood, then any properties they have must also be equal or accidental. Here, the author clarifies that equality at the generic level does not negate necessary distinctness at the specific level.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Notice the key terms: ذوات, ماهيات, حقائق, امتياز, لزوم.
2
Understand the core claim
The passage first states the equality of essences, quiddities, and realities in being what they are. Then it asserts that each one has a necessary distinguishing attribute. Identify the shift from generic equality to specific difference.
3
Connect to the refutation context
Ask yourself: why does this count as a refutation of the opponent's view? The opponent likely argued that shared essencehood implies shared properties. The author shows that necessary properties can be distinct despite generic equality.
4
Reflect on the concept of necessary property (luzūm)
The attribute is said to belong 'of necessity' (luzūm). What does this imply about the relationship between essence and attribute? It's not accidental but essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها : إن الذوات ، والماهيات ، والحقائق متساوية في كونها ذوات وحقائق وماهيات . ثم إن كل واحد منها اختص بالصفة التي باعتبارها امتاز عن سائر الحقائق والماهيات اختصاصا على سبيل اللزوم.
Third: The essences (al-dhātāt), the quiddities (al-māhiyyāt), and the realities (al-ḥaqā’iq) are equal in being essences, realities, and quiddities. Then, each one of them is distinguished by an attribute by virtue of which it is distinguished from the rest of the realities and quiddities, a distinction in the manner of necessity (luzūm).
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This is the third refutation. The author points out that all essences, quiddities, and realities are equal insofar as they are instances of those categories. But despite that equality, each essence has a specific attribute (ṣifa) by which it is distinct from all others, and this attribute belongs to it necessarily (luzūm). Thus, sharing the generic aspect does not prevent each from having its own necessary distinguishing property. This refutes the opponent's assumption that shared genus forces all specific attributes to be the same.

Even though all essences are equal in being essences, each essence necessarily has a unique property that distinguishes it from others.

Fourth Refutation: Animality and Color-ness Examples
Fourth refutation through animal and color examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses concrete examples of animality and color-ness to disprove the opponent's claim that generic equality permits interchangeability of specific properties.

Core Idea
Generic equality does not justify swapping specific properties; that would lead to absurdities.

In this lesson, the author presents a fourth refutation that exposes the absurd consequences of the opponent's position. By considering the relationship between generic animality and specific properties like horseness and humanness, and similarly for color-ness and blackness/whiteness, he shows that the opponent's principle cannot hold true.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
2
Identify the two examples
The passage presents two examples: animality (human/horse) and color-ness (black/white). Find how each example is used to derive an absurd consequence.
3
Understand the logical structure
The author takes the opponent's claim (generic equality allows interchangeability) and applies it to specific cases, showing that it would lead to impossible outcomes.
4
Reflect on the refutation's force
Ask yourself why the absurdity invalidates the opponent's premise. Consider if there is any way to avoid the absurdity without rejecting the premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها : الحيوانية التي في الإنسان مساوية للحيوانية التي في الفرس من حيث إنها حيوانية ، فلو كان ما ذكرتموه صحيحا ، فحينئذ يلزمكم صحبة أن تبقى حيوانية الفرس بعينها مع زوال الفرسية عنها وتبدلها بالإنسانية ، وكذلك القول في حيوانية الإنسان ، وأيضا اللونية التي في السواد مساوية للونية التي في البياض ، فيلزمكم أن تحكموا بجواز بقاء اللونية التي في السواد مع زوال السوادية عنها ، وتبدلها بالبياضية ، ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل لا يقول به أحد.
Fourth: The animality (al-ḥayawāniyya) that is in the human is equal to the animality that is in the horse insofar as it is animality. So if what you have mentioned were correct, then it would necessarily follow that the animality of the horse remains identically with the removal of horseness from it and its replacement with humanness. And similarly for the animality of the human. Also, the color-ness (al-lawniyya) that is in blackness is equal to the color-ness that is in whiteness, so it would follow that you must judge the permissibility of the remaining of the color-ness that is in blackness with the removal of blackness from it and its replacement with whiteness. But it is known that this is false and no one says it.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This fourth refutation reduces the opponent's position to absurdity by concrete examples. The opponent claims that if two things share a generic attribute (like animality or color-ness), then they must be equal in all properties. The author shows that if that were true, then for example the animality of a horse could remain even if the horse's specific property (horseness) were replaced by humanness, turning the horse into a human. Similarly, the color-ness of black could remain while blackness is replaced by whiteness, turning black into white. These consequences are obviously absurd and no one accepts them. Therefore, the opponent's premise is false: generic equality does not justify interchangeability of specific properties.

إنسانية
لونية
سوادية
بياضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sharing a generic attribute makes specific properties interchangeable.
Correction: The author shows that this leads to absurdities, so generic equality does not justify swapping specific properties.

Checkpoint

A) A horse could become a human while keeping its animality.
B) Animality and color-ness are the same thing.
C) Humans are not animals.
D) Black and white are the same color.

Key Takeaway
The falsehood of the opponent's position is shown by the absurd consequences that follow from it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
When you encounter an argument that leads to an impossible consequence, do you quickly abandon the premise, or do you try to rationalize the absurdity? Which person are you—one who submits to truth even when it is difficult, or one who clings to falsehood out of pride?
?
The author used clear, simple examples to expose falsehood. Consider one area of your life where you might be deceived by a plausible but false principle. What would it take for you to accept the logical consequences and change your stance today?

The Fifth Objection and the Need to Answer
Presents the fifth objection and states the need to answer counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth objection raised against the proof and why answering counterexamples is necessary for completing the proof.

Core Idea
Counterexamples must be answered; a proof is only complete when objections are resolved.

In this passage, the author presents a critical objection to the proof being discussed. He illustrates it with an example about color properties and then states the core objection: bodies are possible at origination but not at persistence, leading to a logical inconsistency. This sets up the requirement that these counterexamples must be addressed for the proof to stand.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the translation. Note that the passage contains two parts: a color example and the fifth objection itself.
The passage is short but dense; read carefully.
2
Analyze the color example
Identify how the color-ness example works: it argues that if the opponent's reasoning were applied, it would lead to an absurd conclusion (color-ness remaining while whiteness replaces blackness). This is a counterexample to the proof.
The example shows a logical consequence that no one accepts.
3
Understand the fifth objection
Focus on the claim about bodies: at origination they are possible (within divine power), but at persistence they are not, because obtaining what already exists is impossible. This challenges the proof's validity if it treats both states equally.
The objection implies the proof has an unresolved inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول في حيوانية الإنسان ، وأيضا اللونية التي في السواد مساوية للونية التي في البياض ، فيلزمكم أن تحكموا بجواز بقاء اللونية التي في السواد مع زوال السوادية عنها ، وتبدلها بالبياضية ، ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل لا يقول به أحد . وخامسها : وهو أن الأجسام حال حدوثها مقدورة وحال بقائها غير مقدورة لأن تحصيل الحاصل محال مع أن الجسم الحادث مساو للجسم الباقي . فهذه نقوض لا بد من الجواب عنها ليتم دليلكم
The statement concerning man's animality: and also the color-ness that is in blackness is equal to the color-ness that is in whiteness, so it would be necessary for you to rule that it is permissible for the color-ness that is in blackness to remain while its blackness-ness departs and it changes to whiteness-ness, yet it is known that this is false, and no one says it. And the fifth of them: that bodies at the time of their origination are within power, and at the time of their persistence are not within power, because obtaining what is already obtained is impossible, even though the originated body is equal to the persistent body. So these are counterexamples that must be answered for your proof to be complete.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection (al-naqd al-khamis) against a proof, likely a proof for the createdness of bodies or the impossibility of accidents enduring across change. The passage begins with an example: a claim about man's animality is raised, but the more specific argument uses color properties. It says that the 'color-ness' in blackness is the same genus as the 'color-ness' in whiteness, so if the opponent's reasoning were applied, it would require that the color-ness of blackness could persist while the blackness itself is replaced by whiteness—an absurdity no one accepts. Then the fifth objection is stated: bodies at the moment of their coming into existence are 'within power' (mawjuda? actually 'mawjuda'? The text says 'mashoora'? Wait 'mashoora'? Actually the Arabic is 'maqdūra' which means 'powered' or 'possible' as in God's power. But the translation is challenging. The idea: at origination, the body is possible (it can be brought into existence by power), but after it exists, it cannot be brought into existence again because that would be 'tahsīl al-ḥāṣil' (obtaining what is already obtained). This is a problem because the opponent's argument treats the originated body and the persistent body as equal in terms of possibility. The passage concludes that these are counterexamples (nuqūḍ) that must be answered for the proof to be valid. The author highlights that the opponent must address these objections.

Misconception: The objection is about the persistence of accidents themselves.
Correction: The objection is actually about the logical implications of the opponent's argument for property change, not about the accidents per se.

Checkpoint

A) They are eternal.

D) They are impossible.

Key Takeaway

?
The author warns that leaving counterexamples unanswered leaves the proof incomplete. In your own life, are there inconsistencies between what you profess and how you live that undermine your witness? What one contradiction will you work to resolve starting today?
