The Possibility of Infinite Regress: Three Proofs
Objection with three proofs for possible infinite regress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three examples used to argue that infinite regress is not inherently impossible, and how they challenge the standard kalam position.

Core Idea
Infinite regress can be coherent in mathematics, attributes, and divine knowledge.

This lesson unpacks a classic objection in kalam: the claim that infinite regress (tasalsul) is impossible. The objector offers three concrete examples where a regress appears to occur without contradiction. These examples come from mathematics, the nature of relative attributes, and God's knowledge of His own knowledge. Understanding them helps clarify what makes a regress vicious or acceptable.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and initial reply
Start by reading the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the structure: an objection, a response citing infinite regress, and then a counter-response providing three proofs.
The objector does not deny that regress follows; he questions why it is impossible.
2
Examine the three aspects (wujuh)
Identify each of the three proofs: the mathematical example, the relative attributes example, and the divine knowledge example. For each, see how it illustrates an infinite sequence.
These are not actual regresses in the world, but conceptual regresses that are possible.
3
Consider the Mutakallim's likely response
Think about why a Mutakallim might reject each example. For instance, the mathematical example involves abstract entities, not concrete causes. The relative attributes may involve relations, not substances. The divine knowledge example is about God's attributes, not created things.
This shows the debate is about what kind of regress is problematic.
4
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Understand that this lesson challenges the assumption that any infinite regress is impossible. It prepares you for more refined discussions about specification and causation.
The key is to distinguish vicious from non-vicious regresses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن كل جسم إنما اختص بالصفة المعينة لأنه حصل في ذلك الجسم قوة وطبيعة ، أوجبت ذلك العرض . قوله : و السؤال عائد في اختصاص ذلك الجسم بتلك الطبيعة فيلزم التسلسل ، قلنا : يلزم التسلسل . فلم قلتم : إنه محال ؟ والذي يدل على جوازه وجوه : الأول : إن الواحد نصف الاثنين ، وثلث الثلاثة ، وربع الأربعة ، وهكذا إلى غير النهاية . الثاني : إن الصفات الاضافية ، قد ثبت بالدليل أنها موجودات حاصلة في الأعيان ، ثم إنها من قبيل الأعراض ، وكون حصولها في محالها إضافات مغايرة لذواتها ، وذلك يوجب التسلسل . الثالث : إنا بينا أنه تعالى عالم بالمعلومات ، وعالم بكونه عالما بها ، وكذلك في الدرجة الثالثة والرابعة إلى ما لا نهاية له ، وذلك غير التسلسل
The third question: Why is it not permissible to say that every body is specified with the particular quality only because there occurred in that body a power and a nature that necessitated that accident? His statement: 'And the question returns to the specification of that body by that nature, so an infinite regress follows.' We said: An infinite regress does follow. So why do you say it is impossible? And that which indicates its permissibility are aspects: First: The one is half of two, and one-third of three, and one-fourth of four, and so on without end. Second: Relative attributes—it has been established by proof that they are existent entities in individuals, and then they are of the category of accidents, and the fact that their occurrence in their loci are relations different from their essences, and that necessitates an infinite regress. Third: We have shown that He, exalted, is Knower of the things known, and Knower of His being Knower of them, and likewise in the third and fourth degree without limit, and that is not an infinite regress.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer exchange (su'al) regarding the specification of bodies. The objector asks: Why can't we say that each body is specified with its particular quality because of a power or nature residing in the body that necessitates that accident? The opponent (likely the Mutakallim) responds: The question returns to the specification of the body by that nature itself, leading to an infinite regress. The objector then says: Yes, an infinite regress does follow—but why do you claim it is impossible? The objector then provides three proofs (wujuh) for the possibility of infinite regress: 1) The mathematical example: one is half of two, one-third of three, one-fourth of four, etc., ad infinitum, showing relations that regress without end. 2) Relative attributes (sifat idafiyya) are existent entities that are accidents; their instantiations involve relations that are distinct from their essences, which necessitates a regress of relations. 3) God's knowledge: He knows all known things, and He knows that He knows them, and so on to the third and fourth level infinitely, yet this is not an impossible regress. The objector thus challenges the Mutakallim's claim that infinite regress is impossible in this context.

Key Terms
السؤال الثالث
التسلسل
الصفات الاضافية
عالم بالمعلومات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objector is affirming that an actual infinite regress exists in the world.
Correction: The objector is merely arguing that infinite regress is possible in principle, using conceptual examples; he is not asserting that such regresses actually occur in physical causation.

Checkpoint
Which example does the objector use to argue for the possibility of infinite regress?
A) The unity of the soul.
B) The one being half of two, third of three, etc.
C) The creation of the world.
D) The impossibility of a circle.

Key Takeaway
The three examples (mathematical, relative attributes, divine knowledge) show that infinite regress is not inherently impossible and its rejection requires specific reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that the objector does not accept the Mutakallim's claim that infinite regress is impossible; instead, he demands proof. Where in your own life have you accepted a statement as 'impossible' without truly examining the reasoning? Are there areas of your faith where you have not questioned the 'why' behind a ruling or belief, simply because it seemed too difficult? Write down one such area and commit to studying it further.
?
The third proof uses God's infinite knowledge to show that an infinite regress can be not only possible but necessary. When you think about God's omniscience, do you truly internalize that His knowledge of you includes every thought, every intention, and every action you will ever do—and that this is only one level of His awareness? Does this reality change how you act when you think no one is watching? Pause and reflect on a specific habit you have that you would not want God to know about—and then realize that He does know it, infinitely.
?
The mathematical example shows a regress that is not only acceptable but inherent in numbers. Yet we often find it hard to accept infinite regress in other contexts, especially when it comes to our own limitations. Are you currently struggling with a situation that feels like an endless chain of reasons or excuses? Instead of dismissing it as impossible, consider that an infinite regress might be part of the test. What step can you take today to break the chain of procrastination or fear by trusting in God's guidance?

Proofs 1–2: Thickness and Composition of Spheres
Razi introduces the first section and presents the first two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the contingency of specific thickness and composition of celestial spheres to argue for their contingent nature.

Core Idea
Every specific property of the heavens could have been otherwise, pointing to a cause.

Razi begins his detailed enumeration by examining physical characteristics of celestial bodies. The first two proofs consider the thickness and composition of spheres, showing that their specific values are not necessary but contingent. Read on to see how these simple observations lead to profound theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the pattern: each proof states a feature, then says it could have been otherwise, concluding it is contingent.
2
Analyze proof 1
The thickness and number of each sphere are specific but could have been more or less. The example of Mars and the Sun illustrates this.
3
Analyze proof 2
Spheres are composed of parts, and each part's position is contingent. Understand the premise of imaginary division.
4
Reflect on the cumulative argument
These two proofs establish that even the basic structure of celestial bodies is contingent, not necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع عشر
تحديد الدلائل المستلقيطة من إمكان الصفات
اعلم أن العلماء تارة استعملوا هذه المقدمة في الأجرام الفلكية والكوكبية، وأخرى في الأجرام العنصرية.
أما القسم الأول: وهو استعمال هذه المقدمة في الأجرام الفلكية:
فتقريره من وجوه:
الأول: إن كل فلك اختص بعدد معين، وثخن معين، مع جواز أن يكون الحاصل إما أن يكون أزيد منه، أو أنقص منه. مثاله: زعموا: أن ثخن فلك المريخ أعظم من قطر فلك الشمس بالكلية، وأما ثخن الفلك الأعظم، فغير معلوم.
الثاني: إن كل فلك فإنه مركب من الأجزاء، بناء على المقدمة التي قررناها في مسألة الجوهر، وهو أن كل ما يقبل القسمة الوهمية يجب أن تكون الأجزاء حاصلة فيه، قبل حصول ذلك الوهم. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: الجزء الداخل كان يمكن وقوعه خارجا، وبالعكس، فوقوع كل واحد منهما في حيزه الخاص، أمر جائز.
الثالث: إن الحركة والسكون يجوز كل واحد منهما على كل واحد من الأجسام، على سبيل البدل، فاختصاص الجسم الفلكي بالحركة، والجسم الأرضي بالسكون من الجائزات.
الرابع: إن كل حركة قائمة يمكن وقوعها أسرع مما وقع وأبطأ مما وقع، فذلك الحد المعين من السرعة والبطء، من الجائزات، وإنه إن تتحرك كرة الثوابت مرة واحدة، تتحرك كرة الشمس ستة وثلاثين ألف مرة. وأعجب من هذا: أن الفلك الأعظم، أعظم من تلك الثوابت، بما لا يعلمه إلا الله تعالى، ثم إن الفلك الأعظم على غاية عظمته، يتحرك كل يوم مرة واحدة، وتلك الثوابت لا تتمم دورية إلا في ستة وثلاثين ألف سنة.
الخامس: كل حركة قد وقعت متوجهة إلى جهة معينة، دون سائر الجهات، فتكون من الجائزات.
السادس: كل فلك يعرض، فإنه يوجد فلك آخر، إما أعلى منه، أو أسفل منه، وذلك في الحيز من الجائزات.
السابع: اتصاف الأجرام الفلكية بالصورة الفلكية، واتصاف الأجرام العنصرية بالصورة العنصرية يكون من الجائزات.
الثامن: أجرام الأفلاك مخالفة لأجرام الكواكب في الصفات، فتكون من الجائزات.
التاسع: حصول كل واحد من الكواكب في نقطة متعينة من الفلك، يكون من الجائزات.
العاشر: اختصاص كل كوكب بصقع معين، وطبيعة معينة، من الجائزات.
Chapter Seventeen: Determining the Derived Proofs from the Possibility of Attributes. Know that scholars sometimes applied this premise to celestial and planetary bodies, and at other times to elemental bodies. As for the first section: which is the application of this premise to celestial bodies: its formulation is from aspects: First: Every sphere is characterized by a specific number and specific thickness, with the possibility that what is obtained is either more than it or less than it. Example: They claimed that the thickness of the sphere of Mars is entirely greater than the diameter of the sphere of the Sun, and as for the thickness of the greatest sphere, it is unknown. Second: Every sphere is composed of parts, based on the premise we established in the issue of the substance, which is that everything that admits imaginary division must have parts existing in it before that imagination occurs. When this is established, we say: The inner part could have occurred outside, and vice versa, so the occurrence of each one in its specific place is a contingent matter. Third: Motion and rest are each permissible for every body, alternatively, so the specification of the celestial body with motion and the terrestrial body with rest is among contingent matters. Fourth: Every motion that occurs could have occurred faster or slower than it did, so that specific limit of speed and slowness is among contingent matters, and if the sphere of the fixed stars moves once, the sphere of the Sun moves thirty-six thousand times. More astonishing than this: The greatest sphere is greater than those fixed stars by what none knows except Allah, then the greatest sphere, despite its immense size, moves once every day, while those fixed stars complete their revolution only in thirty-six thousand years. Fifth: Every motion that has occurred is directed to a specific direction, to the exclusion of other directions, so it is among contingent matters. Sixth: Every sphere that has been made, there exists another sphere either above it or below it, and that in spatial position is among contingent matters. Seventh: The characterization of celestial bodies with celestial form and elemental bodies with elemental form is among contingent matters. Eighth: The bodies of the spheres are different from the bodies of the planets in attributes, so that is among contingent matters. Ninth: The occurrence of each planet in a specific point of the sphere is among contingent matters. Tenth: The specification of each planet with a specific location and specific nature is among contingent matters.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of ten proofs (وجوه) derived from applying the premise of the possibility of attributes (إمكان الصفات) to celestial bodies (الأجرام الفلكية). The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, states that scholars have used this premise in two domains: celestial bodies and elemental bodies. Here he focuses on the first domain. Each proof argues that some specific characteristic of celestial bodies could have been otherwise, indicating contingency (جواز), which then points to the need for a determinant. The proofs are presented in a numbered list: (1) The specific thickness and number of each sphere could have been different; e.g., Mars's sphere is thicker than the Sun's. (2) Each sphere is composed of parts, and the position of each part could have been different, showing contingency. (3) Motion and rest are both possible for bodies, so the assignment of motion to celestial bodies and rest to terrestrial bodies is contingent. (4) The speed of motion could have been faster or slower; e.g., the fixed stars move once while the Sun moves 36,000 times, and the greatest sphere moves daily while fixed stars take 36,000 years. (5) The direction of motion is specific, not necessary. (6) Each sphere has another sphere above or below it, showing spatial contingency. (7) The form (celestial vs. elemental) is contingent. (8) The bodies of spheres differ from planets in attributes, indicating contingency. (9) Each planet's location in a specific point is contingent. (10) The specification of each planet's location and nature is contingent. Each proof concludes with 'من الجائزات' (among contingent matters). The cumulative effect is to demonstrate the inherent contingency of the celestial realm, laying groundwork for arguments for a Creator.

Key Terms
إمكان الصفات
الأجرام الفلكية
الجواز
القسمة الوهمية
الجوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the second proof?
A) That spheres have a specific thickness
B) That spheres are composed of parts whose positions are contingent
C) That Mars is thicker than the Sun
D) That celestial bodies are not made of atoms

Key Takeaway
The first two proofs show that the specific thickness and composition of celestial spheres are contingent, not necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the specific thickness of your own existence—your height, your lifespan, your abilities. Could they have been otherwise? What does that contingency demand of you in your relationship with the One who determined them?
?
You believe that everything about you—your shape, your mind, your place in the world—could have been different. How does that realization challenge your sense of self-sufficiency? Are you living as if your existence is necessary or contingent?
?
If even the spheres—vast and majestic—are contingent down to their parts, what does that say about your own small, daily choices? Are you acknowledging the One who gives everything its specific place?

Proofs 3–4: Motion and Speed
Razi presents the third and fourth proofs regarding motion and speed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the contingency of motion, rest, and speed in celestial bodies to argue for their contingent nature.

Core Idea
The assignment of motion to celestial bodies and its specific speed are both contingent, not necessary.

Continuing his enumeration, Razi turns to the dynamics of celestial bodies. The third proof considers why celestial bodies are in motion while terrestrial bodies are at rest, and the fourth proof examines the specific speed of each motion. Both show that these could have been otherwise, reinforcing contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the pattern: each proof states a feature, then says it could have been otherwise, concluding it is contingent.
2
Analyze proof 3
Motion and rest are both possible for bodies, so the assignment of motion to celestial bodies and rest to terrestrial bodies is contingent.
3
Analyze proof 4
The speed of motion could have been faster or slower. The example of the fixed stars moving once vs. the Sun 36,000 times and the greatest sphere moving daily vs. fixed stars taking 36,000 years illustrates this.
4
Reflect on the cumulative argument
These two proofs show that not only the existence but also the motion and speed of celestial bodies are contingent.

الخامس: كل حركة قد وقعت متوجهة إلى جهة معينة، دون سائر الجهات، فتكون من الجائزات.
السادس: كل فلك يعرض، فإنه يوجد فلك آخر، إما أعلى منه، أو أسفل منه، وذلك في الحيز من الجائزات.
السابع: اتصاف الأجرام الفلكية بالصورة الفلكية، واتصاف الأجرام العنصرية بالصورة العنصرية يكون من الجائزات.
الثامن: أجرام الأفلاك مخالفة لأجرام الكواكب في الصفات، فتكون من الجائزات.
التاسع: حصول كل واحد من الكواكب في نقطة متعينة من الفلك، يكون من الجائزات.
العاشر: اختصاص كل كوكب بصقع معين، وطبيعة معينة، من الجائزات.
Chapter Seventeen: Determining the Derived Proofs from the Possibility of Attributes. Know that scholars sometimes applied this premise to celestial and planetary bodies, and at other times to elemental bodies. As for the first section: which is the application of this premise to celestial bodies: its formulation is from aspects: First: Every sphere is characterized by a specific number and specific thickness, with the possibility that what is obtained is either more than it or less than it. Example: They claimed that the thickness of the sphere of Mars is entirely greater than the diameter of the sphere of the Sun, and as for the thickness of the greatest sphere, it is unknown. Second: Every sphere is composed of parts, based on the premise we established in the issue of the substance, which is that everything that admits imaginary division must have parts existing in it before that imagination occurs. When this is established, we say: The inner part could have occurred outside, and vice versa, so the occurrence of each one in its specific place is a contingent matter. Third: Motion and rest are each permissible for every body, alternatively, so the specification of the celestial body with motion and the terrestrial body with rest is among contingent matters. Fourth: Every motion that occurs could have occurred faster or slower than it did, so that specific limit of speed and slowness is among contingent matters, and if the sphere of the fixed stars moves once, the sphere of the Sun moves thirty-six thousand times. More astonishing than this: The greatest sphere is greater than those fixed stars by what none knows except Allah, then the greatest sphere, despite its immense size, moves once every day, while those fixed stars complete their revolution only in thirty-six thousand years. Fifth: Every motion that has occurred is directed to a specific direction, to the exclusion of other directions, so it is among contingent matters. Sixth: Every sphere that has been made, there exists another sphere either above it or below it, and that in spatial position is among contingent matters. Seventh: The characterization of celestial bodies with celestial form and elemental bodies with elemental form is among contingent matters. Eighth: The bodies of the spheres are different from the bodies of the planets in attributes, so that is among contingent matters. Ninth: The occurrence of each planet in a specific point of the sphere is among contingent matters. Tenth: The specification of each planet with a specific location and specific nature is among contingent matters.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of ten proofs (وجوه) derived from applying the premise of the possibility of attributes (إمكان الصفات) to celestial bodies (الأجرام الفلكية). The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, states that scholars have used this premise in two domains: celestial bodies and elemental bodies. Here he focuses on the first domain. Each proof argues that some specific characteristic of celestial bodies could have been otherwise, indicating contingency (جواز), which then points to the need for a determinant. The proofs are presented in a numbered list: (1) The specific thickness and number of each sphere could have been different; e.g., Mars's sphere is thicker than the Sun's. (2) Each sphere is composed of parts, and the position of each part could have been different, showing contingency. (3) Motion and rest are both possible for bodies, so the assignment of motion to celestial bodies and rest to terrestrial bodies is contingent. (4) The speed of motion could have been faster or slower; e.g., the fixed stars move once while the Sun moves 36,000 times, and the greatest sphere moves daily while fixed stars take 36,000 years. (5) The direction of motion is specific, not necessary. (6) Each sphere has another sphere above or below it, showing spatial contingency. (7) The form (celestial vs. elemental) is contingent. (8) The bodies of spheres differ from planets in attributes, indicating contingency. (9) Each planet's location in a specific point is contingent. (10) The specification of each planet's location and nature is contingent. Each proof concludes with 'من الجائزات' (among contingent matters). The cumulative effect is to demonstrate the inherent contingency of the celestial realm, laying groundwork for arguments for a Creator.

Key Terms
إمكان الصفات
الأجرام الفلكية
الجواز
الحركة
السكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to proof 4, how many times does the sphere of the Sun move compared to the sphere of the fixed stars?
A) 36,000 times
B) 36 times
C) 36,000,000 times
D) 360 times

Key Takeaway
The motion and speed of celestial bodies are not necessary but contingent, as they could have been otherwise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider your daily routine: how much of it do you take for granted as 'just the way things are'? If the motion of the sun and stars is contingent—chosen by Allah—what does that say about every small blessing in your life? Will you pause today to recognize each as a deliberate gift from Him?
?
Razi finds it 'more astonishing' that the greatest sphere moves daily while fixed stars take millennia. What in the universe or your own life astonishes you? Does that wonder lead you to deeper gratitude and worship? If not, what would it take to restore that sense of awe?
?
If the speed of celestial bodies could have been different, then everything about creation is precisely calibrated by Allah. How does this realization confront any feeling of insignificance or randomness in your life? What specific thought or action can you commit to today to live in light of this purposeful design?

Proofs 5–6: Direction and Spatial Position
Razi presents the fifth and sixth proofs regarding direction and spatial position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the contingency of direction and spatial arrangement of spheres to argue for their contingent nature.

Core Idea
The specific direction of motion and the relative positions of spheres are contingent, indicating they depend on an external determinant.

Razi continues with two more proofs focusing on spatial aspects. The fifth proof considers the direction of motion, which could have been toward any direction. The sixth proof notes that each sphere has another sphere above or below it, a spatial arrangement that could have been different. Both show contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the pattern: each proof states a feature, then says it could have been otherwise, concluding it is contingent.
2
Analyze proof 5
Every motion has a specific direction, but it could have been directed elsewhere; so the direction is contingent.
3
Analyze proof 6
Each sphere has another sphere above or below it; this spatial arrangement is contingent because it could have been different.
4
Reflect on the cumulative argument
These two proofs show that even the spatial properties of celestial bodies are contingent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع عشر
تحديد الدلائل المستلقيطة من إمكان الصفات
اعلم أن العلماء تارة استعملوا هذه المقدمة في الأجرام الفلكية والكوكبية، وأخرى في الأجرام العنصرية.
أما القسم الأول: وهو استعمال هذه المقدمة في الأجرام الفلكية:
فتقريره من وجوه:
الأول: إن كل فلك اختص بعدد معين، وثخن معين، مع جواز أن يكون الحاصل إما أن يكون أزيد منه، أو أنقص منه. مثاله: زعموا: أن ثخن فلك المريخ أعظم من قطر فلك الشمس بالكلية، وأما ثخن الفلك الأعظم، فغير معلوم.
الثاني: إن كل فلك فإنه مركب من الأجزاء، بناء على المقدمة التي قررناها في مسألة الجوهر، وهو أن كل ما يقبل القسمة الوهمية يجب أن تكون الأجزاء حاصلة فيه، قبل حصول ذلك الوهم. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: الجزء الداخل كان يمكن وقوعه خارجا، وبالعكس، فوقوع كل واحد منهما في حيزه الخاص، أمر جائز.
الثالث: إن الحركة والسكون يجوز كل واحد منهما على كل واحد من الأجسام، على سبيل البدل، فاختصاص الجسم الفلكي بالحركة، والجسم الأرضي بالسكون من الجائزات.
الرابع: إن كل حركة قائمة يمكن وقوعها أسرع مما وقع وأبطأ مما وقع، فذلك الحد المعين من السرعة والبطء، من الجائزات، وإنه إن تتحرك كرة الثوابت مرة واحدة، تتحرك كرة الشمس ستة وثلاثين ألف مرة. وأعجب من هذا: أن الفلك الأعظم، أعظم من تلك الثوابت، بما لا يعلمه إلا الله تعالى، ثم إن الفلك الأعظم على غاية عظمته، يتحرك كل يوم مرة واحدة، وتلك الثوابت لا تتمم دورية إلا في ستة وثلاثين ألف سنة.
الخامس: كل حركة قد وقعت متوجهة إلى جهة معينة، دون سائر الجهات، فتكون من الجائزات.
السادس: كل فلك يعرض، فإنه يوجد فلك آخر، إما أعلى منه، أو أسفل منه، وذلك في الحيز من الجائزات.
السابع: اتصاف الأجرام الفلكية بالصورة الفلكية، واتصاف الأجرام العنصرية بالصورة العنصرية يكون من الجائزات.
الثامن: أجرام الأفلاك مخالفة لأجرام الكواكب في الصفات، فتكون من الجائزات.
التاسع: حصول كل واحد من الكواكب في نقطة متعينة من الفلك، يكون من الجائزات.
العاشر: اختصاص كل كوكب بصقع معين، وطبيعة معينة، من الجائزات.

Proofs 7–8: Form and Attributes
Razi presents the seventh and eighth proofs regarding form and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the contingency of the form of celestial bodies and their differing attributes to argue for their contingent nature.

Core Idea
The specific form (celestial vs. elemental) and differences between spheres and planets are contingent, not necessary.

Razi now turns to qualitative differences. The seventh proof notes that celestial bodies have a celestial form while elemental bodies have an elemental form, a distinction that could have been otherwise. The eighth proof highlights that spheres and planets differ in attributes, indicating contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the pattern: each proof states a feature, then says it could have been otherwise, concluding it is contingent.
2
Analyze proof 7
Celestial bodies are characterized by celestial form and elemental bodies by elemental form; this assignment is contingent.
3
Analyze proof 8
The bodies of spheres and planets have different attributes; this difference is contingent.
4
Reflect on the cumulative argument
These two proofs show that even the essential forms and attributes of celestial bodies are contingent.

Proofs 9–10: Location and Nature of Planets
Razi presents the ninth and tenth proofs regarding location and nature of planets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the contingency of the specific location and nature of each planet to argue for their contingent nature.

Core Idea
The specific point where each planet is located and its particular nature are both contingent, pointing to a cause.

Razi concludes his enumeration with two proofs concerning the planets themselves. The ninth proof notes that each planet occurs at a specific point on its sphere, which could have been different. The tenth proof adds that each planet has a specific location and nature that are contingent. These final proofs complete the case for contingency of all celestial features.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the pattern: each proof states a feature, then says it could have been otherwise, concluding it is contingent.
2
Analyze proof 9
Each planet is at a specific point on its sphere; this point could have been different, so it is contingent.
3
Analyze proof 10
Each planet has a specific location and nature; these are contingent.
4
Reflect on the cumulative argument
The ten proofs together establish the contingency of all aspects of celestial bodies, setting the stage for the argument for a Creator.

فتقريره من وجوه:
الأول: إن كل فلك اختص بعدد معين، وثخن معين، مع جواز أن يكون الحاصل إما أن يكون أزيد منه، أو أنقص منه. مثاله: زعموا: أن ثخن فلك المريخ أعظم من قطر فلك الشمس بالكلية، وأما ثخن الفلك الأعظم، فغير معلوم.
الثاني: إن كل فلك فإنه مركب من الأجزاء، بناء على المقدمة التي قررناها في مسألة الجوهر، وهو أن كل ما يقبل القسمة الوهمية يجب أن تكون الأجزاء حاصلة فيه، قبل حصول ذلك الوهم. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: الجزء الداخل كان يمكن وقوعه خارجا، وبالعكس، فوقوع كل واحد منهما في حيزه الخاص، أمر جائز.
الثالث: إن الحركة والسكون يجوز كل واحد منهما على كل واحد من الأجسام، على سبيل البدل، فاختصاص الجسم الفلكي بالحركة، والجسم الأرضي بالسكون من الجائزات.
الرابع: إن كل حركة قائمة يمكن وقوعها أسرع مما وقع وأبطأ مما وقع، فذلك الحد المعين من السرعة والبطء، من الجائزات، وإنه إن تتحرك كرة الثوابت مرة واحدة، تتحرك كرة الشمس ستة وثلاثين ألف مرة. وأعجب من هذا: أن الفلك الأعظم، أعظم من تلك الثوابت، بما لا يعلمه إلا الله تعالى، ثم إن الفلك الأعظم على غاية عظمته، يتحرك كل يوم مرة واحدة، وتلك الثوابت لا تتمم دورية إلا في ستة وثلاثين ألف سنة.
الخامس: كل حركة قد وقعت متوجهة إلى جهة معينة، دون سائر الجهات، فتكون من الجائزات.
السادس: كل فلك يعرض، فإنه يوجد فلك آخر، إما أعلى منه، أو أسفل منه، وذلك في الحيز من الجائزات.
السابع: اتصاف الأجرام الفلكية بالصورة الفلكية، واتصاف الأجرام العنصرية بالصورة العنصرية يكون من الجائزات.
الثامن: أجرام الأفلاك مخالفة لأجرام الكواكب في الصفات، فتكون من الجائزات.
التاسع: حصول كل واحد من الكواكب في نقطة متعينة من الفلك، يكون من الجائزات.
العاشر: اختصاص كل كوكب بصقع معين، وطبيعة معينة، من الجائزات.
Chapter Seventeen: Determining the Derived Proofs from the Possibility of Attributes. Know that scholars sometimes applied this premise to celestial and planetary bodies, and at other times to elemental bodies. As for the first section: which is the application of this premise to celestial bodies: its formulation is from aspects: First: Every sphere is characterized by a specific number and specific thickness, with the possibility that what is obtained is either more than it or less than it. Example: They claimed that the thickness of the sphere of Mars is entirely greater than the diameter of the sphere of the Sun, and as for the thickness of the greatest sphere, it is unknown. Second: Every sphere is composed of parts, based on the premise we established in the issue of the substance, which is that everything that admits imaginary division must have parts existing in it before that imagination occurs. When this is established, we say: The inner part could have occurred outside, and vice versa, so the occurrence of each one in its specific place is a contingent matter. Third: Motion and rest are each permissible for every body, alternatively, so the specification of the celestial body with motion and the terrestrial body with rest is among contingent matters. Fourth: Every motion that occurs could have occurred faster or slower than it did, so that specific limit of speed and slowness is among contingent matters, and if the sphere of the fixed stars moves once, the sphere of the Sun moves thirty-six thousand times. More astonishing than this: The greatest sphere is greater than those fixed stars by what none knows except Allah, then the greatest sphere, despite its immense size, moves once every day, while those fixed stars complete their revolution only in thirty-six thousand years. Fifth: Every motion that has occurred is directed to a specific direction, to the exclusion of other directions, so it is among contingent matters. Sixth: Every sphere that has been made, there exists another sphere either above it or below it, and that in spatial position is among contingent matters. Seventh: The characterization of celestial bodies with celestial form and elemental bodies with elemental form is among contingent matters. Eighth: The bodies of the spheres are different from the bodies of the planets in attributes, so that is among contingent matters. Ninth: The occurrence of each planet in a specific point of the sphere is among contingent matters. Tenth: The specification of each planet with a specific location and specific nature is among contingent matters.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of ten proofs (وجوه) derived from applying the premise of the possibility of attributes (إمكان الصفات) to celestial bodies (الأجرام الفلكية). The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, states that scholars have used this premise in two domains: celestial bodies and elemental bodies. Here he focuses on the first domain. Each proof argues that some specific characteristic of celestial bodies could have been otherwise, indicating contingency (جواز), which then points to the need for a determinant. The proofs are presented in a numbered list: (1) The specific thickness and number of each sphere could have been different; e.g., Mars's sphere is thicker than the Sun's. (2) Each sphere is composed of parts, and the position of each part could have been different, showing contingency. (3) Motion and rest are both possible for bodies, so the assignment of motion to celestial bodies and rest to terrestrial bodies is contingent. (4) The speed of motion could have been faster or slower; e.g., the fixed stars move once while the Sun moves 36,000 times, and the greatest sphere moves daily while fixed stars take 36,000 years. (5) The direction of motion is specific, not necessary. (6) Each sphere has another sphere above or below it, showing spatial contingency. (7) The form (celestial vs. elemental) is contingent. (8) The bodies of spheres differ from planets in attributes, indicating contingency. (9) Each planet's location in a specific point is contingent. (10) The specification of each planet's location and nature is contingent. Each proof concludes with 'من الجائزات' (among contingent matters). The cumulative effect is to demonstrate the inherent contingency of the celestial realm, laying groundwork for arguments for a Creator.

Key Terms
إمكان الصفات
الأجرام الفلكية
الجواز
الكواكب
النقطة المتعينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the tenth proof specify as contingent?
A) The speed of planetary motion
B) The specific location and nature of each planet
C) The direction of motion
D) The form of celestial bodies

Key Takeaway
The specific location and nature of each planet are contingent, completing the argument that all features of celestial bodies are contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your life do you assume that your current location (city, home, job) is fixed and necessary, forgetting that it is contingent and could have been otherwise? How does recognizing its contingency reshape your gratitude to the One who placed you there?
?
Consider your own nature—your character traits, abilities, and circumstances. If even a planet's nature is contingent, how much more are the details of your existence dependent on the Creator's will? Does this realization move you to see His hand in every detail, or do you live as if things just happen?
?
If each planet's specific point on its sphere is contingent, what does that imply about the precision of God's plan? Can you trust that even the seemingly arbitrary details of your life are specifically chosen by Him? What would it take for you to surrender to that truth completely?

Every Originated Thing is Possible in Itself
The author proves that every originated thing is possible in itself via its receptivity to existence and non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that every originated thing is possible in itself, based on its receptivity to existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
Possibility means an essence’s receptivity to both existence and non-existence.

This passage presents a foundational argument in Islamic kalam: everything that comes into existence must be possible in itself. The author uses the simple fact that an originated thing was once non-existent and now exists to prove that its essence is receptive to both states. This receptivity is the very meaning of 'possible'. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the English translation. Notice how the author states the thesis and then explains it.
2
Identify the two observable facts
The author points out that an originated thing is presently existent and was previously non-existent. Mark these two observations.
These are the premises of the argument.
3
Understand the inference to receptivity
If the essence were not receptive to existence, it could not exist now. If not receptive to non-existence, it could not have been non-existent. So the essence must be receptive to both.
This 'receptivity' is what makes it possible.
4
Connect to the definition of 'possible'
The author defines 'possible' (mumkin) as that which is receptive to both existence and non-existence. Confirm that the conclusion follows.
The passage ends by referring to a previous proof for the need of an efficient cause, but focus on this proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل محدث فهو ممكن الوجود لذاته وذلك لأن كل ما كان محدثاً فهو في الحال موجود ، وقد كان قبل وجوده معدوماً . ونقول : لو لم تكن حقيقته قابلة للوجود لما وجد في الحال ، ولو لم تكن الحقيقة قابلة للعدم ، لما كانت معدومة في الماضي. فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن كل ما كان محدثاً، فإن ماهيته قابلة للعدم، وقابلة للوجود، ولا معنى للممكن إلا ذلك فيثبت : أن كل محدث فإنه ممكن الوجود لذاته وأما بيان أن كل ما كان ممكناً لذاته فلا بد له من المؤثر : فلما مر تقريره في البرهان الأول.
Every originated thing is possible in itself. That is because everything that is originated is presently existent, and before its existence it was non-existent. We say: if its essence were not receptive to existence, it would not exist presently, and if the essence were not receptive to non-existence, it would not have been non-existent in the past. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that everything that is originated, its quiddity (mahiyyah) is receptive to non-existence and receptive to existence, and there is no meaning of 'possible' (mumkin) except that. So it is established that every originated thing is possible in itself. As for the proof that everything that is possible in itself must have an efficient cause (mu'aththir), its verification has already been presented in the first proof.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a proof that every originated thing is possible in itself. The author begins by stating the claim: every originated thing is possible in itself. He then provides the reasoning: an originated thing is presently existent, and was previously non-existent. From this we infer that its essence must be receptive to both existence and non-existence, otherwise it would not have changed states. This receptivity is exactly what is meant by 'possible'. The author then adds a reference to a previous proof that every possible thing requires an efficient cause, but the focus here is on establishing possibility itself.

Key Terms
محدث
ممكن الوجود
ماهية
المؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'possible' means just 'not impossible', without realizing it entails real receptivity to both existence and non-existence in the essence.
Correction: The author clarifies that possibility is an actual quality of the essence—its being receptive (قابلة) to both states, not merely a logical category.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the fact that an originated thing was non-existent and then exists prove about its essence?
A) Its essence is necessarily existent.
B) Its essence is necessarily non-existent.
C) Its essence is receptive to both existence and non-existence.
D) Its essence is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Every originated thing must be possible in itself because its essence is receptive to existence and non-existence, as proven by its change from non-existence to existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your existence is necessary and independent, or do you truly acknowledge that you are merely possible—receiving existence from another? How does that recognition change the way you begin your day?
?
The lesson says possibility is receptivity to both existence and non-existence. If you were to cease to exist, would anything in your life have been truly necessary? What does that say about your priorities?
?
Do you treat your own existence as a gift that could have been otherwise? If so, what gratitude have you shown to the One who gave it? If not, what needs to change?

Objection and Reply: The Possibility of Originated Things
The author refutes an objection against the possibility of originated things with three evidences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the claim that every originated thing is possible in itself by showing that the opponent's alternative is also rationally conceivable.

Core Idea
The author uses three analogies to show the opponent's view is plausible.

In this passage, the author addresses a direct challenge to his argument. The opponent denies that every originated thing is possible in itself, proposing instead that things can switch from necessary non-existence to necessary existence. The author responds by presenting three evidences that such a scenario is indeed conceivable, using examples from accidents, the present moment, and the world's future possibility. By following the reasoning, you'll see how kalam defends its foundations.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the opponent's claim: that an originated thing might be necessary non-existent at one time and necessary existent at another, without being possible in itself.
The opponent uses the terms 'wajib al-'adam' and 'wajib al-wujud'.
2
Examine the first evidence (accident)
The author uses the example of an accident, which many hold cannot persist. If its non-existence after existence is necessary, why can't its existence after non-existence be necessary too?
This shows the opponent's alternative is conceivable.
3
Analyze the second evidence (the present moment)
The 'now' is necessarily non-existent before and after its brief presence, yet its existence is necessary in the present. This pattern mirrors the opponent's claim.
The present moment is a clear example of a thing switching between necessity modes.
4
Understand the third evidence (world's possibility)
The world, conditioned by prior non-existence, is impossible now but becomes possible in the future. This possibility arises without an external cause, showing self-caused occurrence is rational.
This evidence addresses the opponent's assumption that change requires a cause.
5
Synthesize the overall defense
The three evidences collectively show that the opponent's alternative is not absurd; thus the original argument that every originated thing is possible in itself remains valid.
The author does not prove his view but neutralizes the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لا نسلم أن كل محدث فإنه ممكن الوجود لذاته . قوله : « إنه كان معدوماً ثم وجد، وهذا يدل على أن حقيقته قابلة للعدم والوجود، قلنا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه كان واجب العدم لذاته في الزمان الأول، ثم انقلب واجب الوجود لذاته في الزمان الثاني؟ والذي يدل على أن الذي ذكرناه محتمل : وجوه : الأول : إن العرض عند كثير من الناس مستحيل البقاء، وإذا كان كذلك فوجوده في الوقت الأول جائز، ثم إن عدمه في الوقت الثاني واجب، فإذا عقل أن يكون عدمه بعد وجوده عدماً واجباً لذاته، بحيث يستحيل أن لا يصير معدوماً . فلم لا يجوز أن يصير وجوده بعد عدمه واجباً لذاته، بحيث يستحيل عقلاً، أن لا يصير موجوداً؟ الثاني : وهو أن هذا الآن الذي هو آخر الماضي، وأول المستقبل كما وجد، يمتنع بقاؤه، لأن الحاضر يمتنع أن يكون غير المستقبل فإذا هذا الآن الحاضر كان معدوماً قبل حضوره، ثم صار واجب الحدوث بعد أن كان معدوماً، وبعد حدوثه يمتنع بقاؤه، فصار واجب العدم، بعد أن كان موجوداً فإذا عقل هذا في الأنات، التي هي آخر الزمان، فلم لا يعقل مثله في جميع الحوادث؟ الثالث : العالم بشرط كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم سبقاً زمانياً، ممتنع الحصول في الآن، لأن الجمع بين النقيضين محال، ثم فيما لا يزال حدث ذلك الإمكان، وحدوث ذلك الإمكان ليس بالفاعل، لأن كل ما بالغير فإنه واجب الارتفاع عند ارتفاع ذلك الغير، وكون العالم صحيح الوجود فيما لا يزال، أمر ثابت لذاته، لا لأجل سبب منفصل . فعلمنا : أن هذه الصحة حدثت وتجددت لا لفاعل بل لذاته، فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن حدوث الشيء لذاته معقول في الجملة
If it is said: We do not accept that every originated thing is possible in itself. His statement: 'It was non-existent then came to exist, and this indicates that its essence is receptive to non-existence and existence.' We say: Why is it not permissible to say that it was necessarily non-existent (wajib al-'adam) in the first time, then turned into necessarily existent (wajib al-wujud) in the second time? What indicates that what we have mentioned is probable are aspects (wujuh): First: Accident ('arad), according to many people, is impossible of persistence. If so, then its existence in the first moment is contingent, then its non-existence in the second moment is necessary. So if it is conceivable that its non-existence after its existence is a necessary non-existence in itself, such that it is impossible for it not to become non-existent, then why is it not permissible for its existence after its non-existence to become necessary in itself, such that it is rationally impossible for it not to become existent? Second: This 'now' (an) which is the end of the past and the beginning of the future, as it exists, its persistence is impossible, because the present is impossible of being other than the future. So this present 'now' was non-existent before its presence, then it became necessarily occurring (wajib al-huduth) after being non-existent, and after its occurrence its persistence is impossible, so it becomes necessarily non-existent after being existent. So if this is conceivable in the 'nows' which are the ends of time, why should the like not be conceivable in all originated things? Third: The world ('alam), conditioned by its being preceded by non-existence in a temporal priority, is impossible to obtain in the present, because the conjunction of two contradictories is absurd. Then, in the future (ma la yazal), that possibility (imkan) occurs, and the occurrence of that possibility is not from a doer (fa'il), because everything that is from another necessarily ceases when that other ceases. And the world's being valid of existence in the future is a matter established in itself, not due to a separate cause. So we know that this validity (sihhah) occurred and renewed not from a doer but from itself. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that the occurrence of a thing from itself is rational in general.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the author's reply. The objector denies that every originated thing is possible in itself, proposing instead that a thing could be necessarily non-existent at one time and necessarily existent at another time. The author responds by presenting three evidences that such a view is plausible, thereby showing that his own position is not undermined. The first evidence uses the example of an accident: many hold that accidents cannot persist, so an accident's existence is contingent and its non-existence is necessary, yet it would not imply that the accident's existence becomes necessary. The second evidence uses the 'now' (the present moment) which is necessarily non-existent before and after, yet its existence is necessary in the present. The third evidence discusses the world's possibility in the future, arguing that the world's validity of existence in the future occurs without a cause, showing that self-caused occurrence is conceivable. Thus the objection does not refute the original argument.

Key Terms
واجب العدم
واجب الوجود
عرض
الآن
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the author's evidences actually support the opponent's view or are objections to his own position.
Correction: The author is not conceding the opponent's view; he is demonstrating that even if we accept the opponent's alternative as conceivable, it does not refute the original argument that every originated thing is possible in itself.

Checkpoint

A) The world
B) The 'now'
C) An accident
D) The quiddity

Key Takeaway
The author's three evidences demonstrate that the opponent's alternative is rationally conceivable, thus the original argument that every originated thing is possible in itself remains valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The author argues that the world's validity of existence in the future is established from itself, not from a separate cause. What would it mean for you to take ownership of that possibility today?
?
Consider the 'now'—a fleeting moment that is necessary at its instant but impossible to persist. How often do you treat your current state as permanent? What would change if you truly believed that your existence is being renewed each moment, and that you are not stuck in your past?
?
The objector suggests that a thing could suddenly flip from necessary non-existence to necessary existence without being possible in itself. Have you ever assumed that certain negative habits or traits in yourself are 'necessary' and unchangeable? How does this passage challenge that assumption, and what step can you take to see yourself as inherently changeable?

The Third Argument: Temporal Nonexistence
A refutation of the world's present existence if preceded by temporal nonexistence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the world's present existence cannot be preceded by temporal nonexistence and how its possibility in the future originates from itself without an external agent.

Core Idea
Combining present existence and past nonexistence is a contradiction; possibility renews from the thing itself.

This passage presents the third argument against the view that the world's existence now is impossible if it was preceded by temporal nonexistence. The argument demonstrates that such a scenario would combine two opposites, which is impossible. Moreover, it explains that the possibility of existence in the future occurs not from an external agent but from the thing's own self, establishing that self-origination is intelligible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the logical flow from claim to conclusion.
Pay attention to the phrase 'الجمع بين النقيضين'.
2
Identify the main contradiction
The passage states that if the world is conditioned by past nonexistence, its present existence would combine existence and nonexistence. Understand why this is impossible.
This is the key premise.
3
Examine the argument about future possibility
The passage then discusses the occurrence of possibility in the future, arguing it is not from an agent because what depends on another ceases when that other ceases.
Contrast with the nature of the world's validity.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The conclusion is that the occurrence of a thing from itself is intelligible. Reflect on how this argument refutes the original objection.
This ties back to the title.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : العالم بشرط كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم سبقاً زمانياً ، ممتنع الحصول في الآن ، لأن الجمع بين النقيضين محال ، ثم فيما لا يزال حدث ذلك الإمكان ، وحدوث ذلك الإمكان ليس بالفاعل ، لأن كل ما بالغير فإنه واجب الارتفاع عند ارتفاع ذلك الغير ، وكون العالم صحيح الوجود فيما لا يزال ، أمر ثابت لذاته ، لا لأجل سبب منفصل . فعلمنا : أن هذه الصحة حدثت وتجددت لا لفاعل بل لذاته ، فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن حدوث الشيء لذاته معقول في الجملة
Third: The world, conditioned by being preceded by nonexistence with temporal precedence, is impossible to occur in the present, because combining two opposites is impossible. Then in the future that possibility occurred, and the occurrence of that possibility is not from an agent, because everything that is by another is necessarily removed when that other is removed. And the world being valid of existence in the future is a matter established for itself, not due to a separate cause. So we know: that this validity occurred and renewed not from an agent but from itself. So it is established by what we mentioned: that the occurrence of a thing from itself is intelligible in a general sense.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a direct argument, labeled as the third point, against the claim that the world's present existence is impossible if it was preceded by temporal nonexistence. The author states: 'Third: The world, conditioned by being preceded by nonexistence with temporal precedence, is impossible to occur in the present, because combining two opposites is impossible.' The reasoning is that if the world's existence now requires it to have been nonexistent before, then at the present moment both existence (now) and nonexistence (the past condition) would be combined, which is a contradiction. The author then argues that the possibility of existence in the future (' فيما لا يزال ') occurs, but this occurrence is not from an agent, because anything that depends on another ceases when that other ceases. The world's validity of existence in the future is a self-subsisting property, not due to any external cause. Therefore, we conclude that the occurrence of a thing from itself (i.e., without an immediate agent) is intelligible in a general sense. The argument upholds the coherence of temporal origination without requiring an external agent for the possibility itself.

Key Terms
العدم
الآن
الجمع بين النقيضين
الإمكان
حدوث الشيء لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies all causality.
Correction: It only denies that the occurrence of possibility requires an external agent; it affirms self-subsisting possibility.

Checkpoint

A) Because the world is eternal
B) Because it would combine existence and nonexistence at the same time
C) Because the world has no agent
D) Because nonexistence is absolute

Key Takeaway
The argument refutes temporal precedence by showing it leads to a contradiction; the possibility of existence in the future arises from the thing itself, not from an external agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that the validity of the world's existence is established for itself, not dependent on external causes. In your own spiritual life, do you rely on external validations (people, status, achievements) or do you root your identity in the unshakable truth that your existence is from God and for Him? How can you shift today?
?
If the world's possibility arises from itself, then nothing external can prevent its perfection from its own perspective. What 'temporal nonexistences' (past failures, sins, delays) do you allow to define your present reality? How does this argument challenge you to see God's decree as the only condition that matters?
?
The argument refutes the idea that prior nonexistence makes present existence impossible. Are you living as though your past emptiness disqualifies you from being used by God now? What step of obedience will you take today based solely on the truth that your potential is self-subsisting in His will?

First Proof: Existence and Essence
First proof against possibility using existence-essence relationship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the relationship between existence and essence makes the concept of possibility (imkan) incoherent according to the first proof.

Core Idea
If existence is the same as essence, possibility becomes self-contradictory.

This lesson examines the first of three objections against the coherence of possibility. The argument proceeds by considering two philosophical positions on the relation between existence and essence, showing that possibility is unintelligible on both views.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
Read the Arabic passage for the first proof once. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: first a general objection, then two main branches.
The proof uses a disjunction: either existence is identical to essence or it is distinct.
2
Identify the two views
The proof considers two views: (1) existence is the same as essence, (2) existence is other than essence. Note how each is handled.
3
Understand the first view
On the first view, possibility is impossible because it would require something to both be and not be at the same time. Grasp the contradiction.
The key point: if essence is existence, then describing essence with possibility of nonexistence is contradictory.
4
Examine the second view
On the second view, three candidates for what is described by possibility are examined: essence, existence, or the combination. All are refuted.
Each candidate leads to absurdity: essence leads to self-contradiction, existence to absurdity, and the combination repeats the same issues.
5
Conclude the proof
The proof concludes that possibility is unintelligible regardless of the position on existence and essence. Reflect on the overall argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا : أن كل ما ذكرتم يدل على أن كل محدث ممكن ، لكنه معارض بوجوه دالة على أن القول بوجود شيء ممكن الوجود محال : الحجة الأولى : إنا إما أن نقول : الوجود نفس الماهية ، وإما أن نقول : الوجود غير الماهية. وعلى كل واحد من القولين فالقول بالإمكان باطل . أما على القول بأن الوجود نفس الماهية ، فنقول : الإمكان غير معقول . وذلك لأن الشيء الموصوف بإمكان الوجود والعدم، هو الذي يكون تارة موصوفاً بالوجود ، وأخرى موصوفاً بالعدم . والوجود يمتنع بقاؤه حال العــدم . فيثبت : أن بتقدير أن تكون الماهية عين الوجود ، يستحيل الحكم على الماهية بإمكان الوجود والعدم . وأما على القول بأن الوجود غير الماهية ، فنقول : إن على هذا القول الموصوف بالإمكان. إما أن يكون هو الماهية أو الوجود ، أو كون الماهية موصوفة بالوجود . والكل باطل . أما أنه لا يجوز أن يكون الموصوف بالإمكان هو الماهية. فلأنا إذا قلنا : ( السواد يمكن أن يكون سوادا ) - و يمكن أن لا يكون سوادا ، كان معناها : أن السواد يمكن أن يحكم عليه بأنه غير سواد . وذلك محال . لأنه يقتضي أن يكون حال كونه سوادا ، يمكن أن لا يكون سوادا وذلك جمع بين النقيضين . وهو محال . وأما أنه لا يجوز أن يكون الموصوف بالإمكان هو الوجود ، فلانه يرجع حاصله إلى أن الوجود يمكن أن يصير لا وجود . وهذا ظاهر الفساد. وأما أنه لا يجوز أن يكون الموصوف بالإمكان هو موصوفية الماهية بالوجود، فلان الذي ذكرناه في الماهية ، وفي الوجود : عائد بعينه في موصوفية الماهية بالوجود . فيثبت : أن القول بإمكان الوجود غير معقول . سواء قلنا : الوجود عين الماهية أو قلنا : إنه غير الماهية الحجة الثانية : لو فرضنا شيئاً من الأشياء ممكن الوجود فذلك الإمكان إما أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً . لا جائز أن يكون موجوداً لأنه لو كان موجوداً ، لكان إما أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ، وهو محال . لأن الإمكان صفة للمكن ومفتقرة إليه والمفتقر إلى المكن : إما أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ، وإما أن يكون ممكن الوجود لذاته . وحينئذ يكون الإمكان زائداً عليه ويلزم التسلسلي ولا جائز أن يكون الإمكان معدوماً . لأنه إذا كان الإمكان معدوماً ، فحينئذ لا يكون الإمكان حاصلاً . وأيضاً : فالإمكان بتقدير ثبوته يكون واقعاً . لأن الامتناع عدم ، ورافع العدم ثبوت . فوجب أن يكون الإمكان موجوداً . فيثبت : أنه لو فرض شيء من الأشياء ممكن الوجود ، لذاته لكان إمكانه ، إما أن يكون وصفاً وجودياً ، أو عدمياً . والقسمان باطلان ، فكان القول بكون الشيء ممكن الوجود : باطلاً . الحجة الثالثة : الحكم على الشيء بأنه يجوز أن يكون موجوداً ، ويجوز أن يكون معدوماً : يقتضي بقاء تلك الماهية حال طريان العدم . لان الشيء الذي يمكن اتصافه بشيء ، يجب أن يكون متقرراً حال حصول تلك الصفة ، فإذا وصفنا هذا الشيء بأنه ممكن العدم، فهذا يقتضي أن لا يمتنع حصول ماهيته حال طريان العدم. وهذا يقتضي كون المعدوم شيئاً . وأنتم لا تقولون
We concede: that everything you mentioned indicates that every originated thing is possible, but it is opposed by proofs indicating that the statement that a possible thing exists is impossible: The first proof: We either say existence is the same as essence, or we say existence is other than essence. On both statements, the claim of possibility is invalid. As for the statement that existence is the same as essence, we say: possibility is unintelligible. That is because the thing described with possibility of existence and nonexistence is that which is sometimes described by existence and sometimes by nonexistence. And existence cannot remain during nonexistence. So it is established that if essence is identical to existence, it is impossible to judge essence with possibility of existence and nonexistence. As for the statement that existence is other than essence, we say: according to this statement, the thing described with possibility must be either the essence, or existence, or the essence being described by existence. All are invalid. As for that it is not permissible that the described with possibility is the essence, because if we say 'black can be black' and 'can not be black', its meaning is that black can be judged as not black. That is impossible, because it requires that while being black, it can not be black, which is combining two opposites, and that is impossible. As for that it is not permissible that the described with possibility is existence, because its conclusion reduces to that existence can become non-existence, and this is obviously absurd. As for that it is not permissible that the described with possibility is the essence being described by existence, because what we mentioned about essence and about existence returns exactly in the essence being described by existence. So it is established that the claim of possibility of existence is unintelligible, whether we say existence is the same as essence or we say it is other than essence. The second proof: If we suppose a thing among things to be possible of existence, then that possibility is either existent or non-existent. It is not possible that it is existent, because if it were existent, it would either be necessarily existent by itself, which is impossible, because possibility is a quality of the possible and dependent on it, and what depends on the possible is either necessarily existent by itself or possible by itself. Then possibility would be additional to it and a regress (tasalsul) would follow. And it is not possible that possibility is non-existent, because if possibility is non-existent, then possibility would not be actual. Also, possibility, if supposed to be actualized, would be real, because impossibility is non-existence, and what removes non-existence is existence. So it is necessary that possibility is existent. So it is established: if a thing is supposed to be possible of existence by itself, its possibility would either be an existent attribute or a non-existent one. Both divisions are invalid, so the statement that a thing is possible of existence is invalid. The third proof: The judgment that a thing may exist and may not exist necessitates the subsistence of that essence during the occurrence of non-existence. Because a thing that can be described by something must be established at the time of obtaining that attribute. So when we describe this thing as possible of non-existence, this necessitates that its essence is not impossible to obtain during the occurrence of non-existence. And this necessitates that the non-existent is a thing. And you do not say [that the non-existent is a thing].
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is an objection against the concept of possibility (imkan). It begins with 'We concede' (sallamna) that the previous arguments show that every contingent thing is possible, but then it presents three proofs that the very notion of a thing being possible is incoherent. The first proof examines two views on the relation between existence and essence. If existence is identical to essence, then describing essence with possibility would be like saying something can both be and not be at the same time, which is contradictory. If existence is other than essence, then what is described by possibility must be either essence, existence, or the combination. For essence: saying 'black can be black and can not be black' implies that black, while being black, can not be black – a contradiction. For existence: saying 'existence can become non-existence' is absurd. For the combination: the same contradiction recurs. Thus possibility is unintelligible on both views. The second proof considers whether possibility itself is existent or non-existent. If existent, it would lead to a regress (tasalsul). If non-existent, then possibility is not actual, but the definition of possibility requires that it is realizable. Therefore possibility must be existent, yet both options fail, so possibility is invalid. The third proof (incomplete) argues that if a thing is possible of non-existence, then its essence must remain during non-existence, which implies that the non-existent is a thing – a position denied by the opponents. Note that the third proof is cut off at the end of the page, so the full argument is not given here.

Key Terms
الإمكان
الوجود
الماهية
الموصوف بالإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof is a denial that anything is contingent or possible.
Correction: It is a dialectical argument showing that given certain metaphysical assumptions (about existence and essence), the concept of possibility becomes incoherent. It does not necessarily deny contingency as a theological doctrine.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, if existence is other than essence, which of the following is NOT examined as a candidate for what is described by possibility?
A) The essence
B) Existence
C) The essence being described by existence
D) The agent

Key Takeaway
The first proof argues that possibility is unintelligible whether existence is identical to essence or distinct from it, due to inherent contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument claims that possibility is unintelligible. Have you ever assumed that things could be otherwise without considering the deep logical structure of that assumption? How does this argument challenge the way you think about change and contingency in your own life?
?
If possibility is impossible, then everything that exists is necessary. Does this disturb you? Why? How does this relate to your trust in rational arguments to arrive at truth about God and the universe?

Second Proof: The Status of Possibility Itself
Second proof against possibility examining its own ontological status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that possibility is incoherent by examining whether possibility itself is existent or non-existent.

Core Idea
Possibility itself cannot be consistently conceived as either existent or non-existent.

The second proof shifts focus from the object of possibility to the attribute of possibility itself. It asks whether possibility is an existent or a non-existent quality, and shows both options lead to absurdity: if existent, it leads to an infinite regress; if non-existent, it contradicts the very idea of possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the Arabic passage for the second proof. Then read the translation. Notice the disjunction: possibility is either existent or non-existent.
2
Understand the first option: possibility as existent
The proof argues that if possibility is existent, it would either be necessary or itself possible, leading to a regress. Grasp the infinite regress argument.
The key point: possibility depends on the possible thing, so it cannot be necessary; if it is possible, then its own possibility would need another possibility, ad infinitum.
3
Understand the second option: possibility as non-existent
The proof argues that if possibility is non-existent, then it is not actual, but the definition of possibility requires that it be realizable (since impossibility is non-existence and what removes non-existence is existence).
Note the logic: impossibility is non-existence; so if possibility were non-existence, it would be impossibility, contradicting itself.
4
Conclude the proof
Both options are invalid, so the statement that a thing is possible of existence is invalid. Reflect on the overall argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to the second proof, what problem arises if possibility is considered existent?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If the concept of possibility itself is logically incoherent, what does that reveal about the foundation of your hope? Are you relying on a fiction rather than on the Necessary Being who alone can grant existence?
?
You exist right now. Are you necessary or possible? If possibility is invalid, then you cannot be merely possible. Does your existence feel contingent or dependent? How does this proof press you to acknowledge your absolute dependence on the Self-Sufficient One?
?
When you make plans for tomorrow, you assume that tomorrow is possible. But if this proof shows that possibility is an incoherent concept, then what is the true ground of your confidence in the future? Is it not the will of the Necessary Being that sustains all? How can you realign your daily hopes to rest entirely on Him?

Third Proof: Possibility Implies Non-Existence is a Thing
Third proof that possibility implies non-existence is real

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that possibility leads to the conclusion that non-existence must be a real entity, which the opponent denies.

Core Idea
If a thing can be possibly non-existent, its essence must remain during non-existence, making non-existence a thing.

The third proof, though incomplete in the source, argues that describing a thing as possibly non-existent requires that the thing's essence subsist during non-existence. This implies that non-existence has some reality—a position the opponent rejects. This lesson examines the argument as far as it is preserved.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof as given
Read the Arabic passage for the third proof. Then read the translation. Note that the text cuts off mid-sentence.
2
Understand the premise
The proof states that judging a thing as possibly non-existent requires that its essence subsist during non-existence. Grasp this link.
Because something can only be described by a quality if it exists when the quality is attributed.
3
Recognize the implication
If the essence subsists during non-existence, then non-existence must be a state in which the essence is still present—implying non-existence is something (a thing).
4
Understand the opponent's position
The proof ends with 'And you do not say [that the non-existent is a thing]', indicating the opponent denies that non-existence is a thing. Thus the proof shows an internal inconsistency in the opponent's view.
5
Note the incompleteness
Be aware that the argument is cut off. What follows would likely elaborate on the contradiction or conclude the proof.
