سلمنا : أن كل ما ذكرتم يدل على أن كل محدث ممكن ، لكنه معارض بوجوه دالة على أن القول بوجود شيء ممكن الوجود محال : الحجة الأولى : إنا إما أن نقول : الوجود نفس الماهية ، وإما أن نقول : الوجود غير الماهية. وعلى كل واحد من القولين فالقول بالإمكان باطل . أما على القول بأن الوجود نفس الماهية ، فنقول : الإمكان غير معقول . وذلك لأن الشيء الموصوف بإمكان الوجود والعدم، هو الذي يكون تارة موصوفاً بالوجود ، وأخرى موصوفاً بالعدم . والوجود يمتنع بقاؤه حال العــدم . فيثبت : أن بتقدير أن تكون الماهية عين الوجود ، يستحيل الحكم على الماهية بإمكان الوجود والعدم . وأما على القول بأن الوجود غير الماهية ، فنقول : إن على هذا القول الموصوف بالإمكان. إما أن يكون هو الماهية أو الوجود ، أو كون الماهية موصوفة بالوجود . والكل باطل . أما أنه لا يجوز أن يكون الموصوف بالإمكان هو الماهية. فلأنا إذا قلنا : ( السواد يمكن أن يكون سوادا ) - و يمكن أن لا يكون سوادا ، كان معناها : أن السواد يمكن أن يحكم عليه بأنه غير سواد . وذلك محال . لأنه يقتضي أن يكون حال كونه سوادا ، يمكن أن لا يكون سوادا وذلك جمع بين النقيضين . وهو محال . وأما أنه لا يجوز أن يكون الموصوف بالإمكان هو الوجود ، فلانه يرجع حاصله إلى أن الوجود يمكن أن يصير لا وجود . وهذا ظاهر الفساد. وأما أنه لا يجوز أن يكون الموصوف بالإمكان هو موصوفية الماهية بالوجود، فلان الذي ذكرناه في الماهية ، وفي الوجود : عائد بعينه في موصوفية الماهية بالوجود . فيثبت : أن القول بإمكان الوجود غير معقول . سواء قلنا : الوجود عين الماهية أو قلنا : إنه غير الماهية الحجة الثانية : لو فرضنا شيئاً من الأشياء ممكن الوجود فذلك الإمكان إما أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً . لا جائز أن يكون موجوداً لأنه لو كان موجوداً ، لكان إما أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ، وهو محال . لأن الإمكان صفة للمكن ومفتقرة إليه والمفتقر إلى المكن : إما أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ، وإما أن يكون ممكن الوجود لذاته . وحينئذ يكون الإمكان زائداً عليه ويلزم التسلسلي ولا جائز أن يكون الإمكان معدوماً . لأنه إذا كان الإمكان معدوماً ، فحينئذ لا يكون الإمكان حاصلاً . وأيضاً : فالإمكان بتقدير ثبوته يكون واقعاً . لأن الامتناع عدم ، ورافع العدم ثبوت . فوجب أن يكون الإمكان موجوداً . فيثبت : أنه لو فرض شيء من الأشياء ممكن الوجود ، لذاته لكان إمكانه ، إما أن يكون وصفاً وجودياً ، أو عدمياً . والقسمان باطلان ، فكان القول بكون الشيء ممكن الوجود : باطلاً . الحجة الثالثة : الحكم على الشيء بأنه يجوز أن يكون موجوداً ، ويجوز أن يكون معدوماً : يقتضي بقاء تلك الماهية حال طريان العدم . لان الشيء الذي يمكن اتصافه بشيء ، يجب أن يكون متقرراً حال حصول تلك الصفة ، فإذا وصفنا هذا الشيء بأنه ممكن العدم، فهذا يقتضي أن لا يمتنع حصول ماهيته حال طريان العدم. وهذا يقتضي كون المعدوم شيئاً . وأنتم لا تقولون
We concede: that everything you mentioned indicates that every originated thing is possible, but it is opposed by proofs indicating that the statement that a possible thing exists is impossible: The first proof: We either say existence is the same as essence, or we say existence is other than essence. On both statements, the claim of possibility is invalid. As for the statement that existence is the same as essence, we say: possibility is unintelligible. That is because the thing described with possibility of existence and nonexistence is that which is sometimes described by existence and sometimes by nonexistence. And existence cannot remain during nonexistence. So it is established that if essence is identical to existence, it is impossible to judge essence with possibility of existence and nonexistence. As for the statement that existence is other than essence, we say: according to this statement, the thing described with possibility must be either the essence, or existence, or the essence being described by existence. All are invalid. As for that it is not permissible that the described with possibility is the essence, because if we say 'black can be black' and 'can not be black', its meaning is that black can be judged as not black. That is impossible, because it requires that while being black, it can not be black, which is combining two opposites, and that is impossible. As for that it is not permissible that the described with possibility is existence, because its conclusion reduces to that existence can become non-existence, and this is obviously absurd. As for that it is not permissible that the described with possibility is the essence being described by existence, because what we mentioned about essence and about existence returns exactly in the essence being described by existence. So it is established that the claim of possibility of existence is unintelligible, whether we say existence is the same as essence or we say it is other than essence. The second proof: If we suppose a thing among things to be possible of existence, then that possibility is either existent or non-existent. It is not possible that it is existent, because if it were existent, it would either be necessarily existent by itself, which is impossible, because possibility is a quality of the possible and dependent on it, and what depends on the possible is either necessarily existent by itself or possible by itself. Then possibility would be additional to it and a regress (tasalsul) would follow. And it is not possible that possibility is non-existent, because if possibility is non-existent, then possibility would not be actual. Also, possibility, if supposed to be actualized, would be real, because impossibility is non-existence, and what removes non-existence is existence. So it is necessary that possibility is existent. So it is established: if a thing is supposed to be possible of existence by itself, its possibility would either be an existent attribute or a non-existent one. Both divisions are invalid, so the statement that a thing is possible of existence is invalid. The third proof: The judgment that a thing may exist and may not exist necessitates the subsistence of that essence during the occurrence of non-existence. Because a thing that can be described by something must be established at the time of obtaining that attribute. So when we describe this thing as possible of non-existence, this necessitates that its essence is not impossible to obtain during the occurrence of non-existence. And this necessitates that the non-existent is a thing. And you do not say [that the non-existent is a thing].
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is an objection against the concept of possibility (imkan). It begins with 'We concede' (sallamna) that the previous arguments show that every contingent thing is possible, but then it presents three proofs that the very notion of a thing being possible is incoherent. The first proof examines two views on the relation between existence and essence. If existence is identical to essence, then describing essence with possibility would be like saying something can both be and not be at the same time, which is contradictory. If existence is other than essence, then what is described by possibility must be either essence, existence, or the combination. For essence: saying 'black can be black and can not be black' implies that black, while being black, can not be black – a contradiction. For existence: saying 'existence can become non-existence' is absurd. For the combination: the same contradiction recurs. Thus possibility is unintelligible on both views. The second proof considers whether possibility itself is existent or non-existent. If existent, it would lead to a regress (tasalsul). If non-existent, then possibility is not actual, but the definition of possibility requires that it is realizable. Therefore possibility must be existent, yet both options fail, so possibility is invalid. The third proof (incomplete) argues that if a thing is possible of non-existence, then its essence must remain during non-existence, which implies that the non-existent is a thing – a position denied by the opponents. Note that the third proof is cut off at the end of the page, so the full argument is not given here.

الماهية
العدم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third proof is a complete argument that denies possibility entirely.
Correction: The proof is incomplete, and it aims to show an inconsistency in the opponent's view, not necessarily a direct denial of possibility.

Checkpoint
What does the third proof claim that possibility of non-existence necessitates?
A) That non-existence is impossible
B) That the essence remains during non-existence
C) That possibility is eternal
D) That existence and non-existence are the same

Key Takeaway
The third proof argues that possibility of non-existence requires the essence to subsist during non-existence, implying non-existence is a thing—a conclusion the opponent denies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of something you deeply love but that is possible to lose—a relationship, a health, a possession. Does the fact that its non-existence is a real possibility change how you hold it in your heart? Are you clinging to it as if its absence is nothing, or do you live with the gravity of its possible non-existence? Write down one concrete way you will acknowledge the reality of that possibility today, without fear but with gratitude.
?
The proof forces you to admit that non-existence is not just emptiness but a state that can 'contain' an essence. If your own non-existence before birth was a real state, what does that say about the gift of your existence now? Are you treating your life as a fragile, borrowed reality, or as something you deserve? Pause and ask Allah to make you grateful for the very fact that you are, knowing that your non-existence was once a thing.
?
You likely deny the reality of things that are absent—past moments, lost opportunities, even your own death. But this lesson says non-existence is a thing. How does that press on you when you think of your own inevitable non-existence? Will you live today as though your existence is a temporary state loaned to you, or will you ignore its end? Commit to one action today that reflects your belief that your non-existence is a real, coming state.

The Second Proof Against Possibility as a Real Attribute
Sanusi presents the second proof that possibility cannot be a real attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why possibility cannot be a real attribute by analyzing the second proof that reduces the concept of 'possible existence' to absurdity.

Core Idea
Possibility cannot be a real attribute—either its existence or non-existence leads to contradiction.

This lesson presents the second proof from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah against the notion that possibility (imkan) is a real attribute inherent in things. The argument examines two hypotheses: that possibility exists or that it does not. Both are shown to be untenable, demonstrating that the concept of 'possible existence' is internally contradictory. Follow the logical steps carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read and translate the passage
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: the argument is divided into two main branches.
The passage uses conditional reasoning: 'if possibility is existent... if it is non-existent...'.
2
Analyze the first branch
Examine the first branch: if possibility is existent, it must be either necessary or possible. Discover why both options lead to absurdity.
Dependency on the possible prevents necessity; possibility leads to infinite regress.
3
Analyze the second branch
Examine the second branch: if possibility is non-existent, then it is not real. Also, note the additional argument from the nature of impossibility.
Impossibility is non-existence, so its opposite (possibility) must be existence.
4
Draw the conclusion
Combine the results: both divisions are false, so the claim that something is 'possibly existent' is false. Understand the implication: possibility cannot be a real attribute.
This refutes the idea of an intermediate state between existence and non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : لو فرضنا شيئاً من الأشياء ممكن الوجود فذلك الإمكان إما أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً . لا جائز أن يكون موجوداً لأنه لو كان موجوداً ، لكان إما أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ، وهو محال . لأن الإمكان صفة للمكن ومفتقرة إليه والمفتقر إلى المكن : إما أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ، وإما أن يكون ممكن الوجود لذاته . وحينئذ يكون الإمكان زائداً عليه ويلزم التسلسل ولا جائز أن يكون الإمكان معدوماً . لأنه إذا كان الإمكان معدوماً ، فحينئذ لا يكون الإمكان حاصلاً . وأيضاً : فالإمكان بتقدير ثبوته يكون واقعاً . لأن الامتناع عدم ، ورافع العدم ثبوت . فوجب أن يكون الإمكان موجوداً . فيثبت : أنه لو فرض شيء من الأشياء ممكن الوجود لذاته لكان إمكانه ، إما أن يكون وصفاً وجودياً ، أو عدمياً . والقسمان باطلان ، فكان القول بكون الشيء ممكن الوجود : باطلاً
The second proof: If we suppose something among things to be possibly existent, then that possibility is either existent or non-existent. It is not possible that it is existent, because if it were existent, it would either be necessary of existence by itself, which is impossible. For possibility is an attribute of the possible and depends on it, and that which depends on the possible is either necessary of existence by itself or possible of existence by itself. Then possibility would be additional to it and entail infinite regress. And it is not possible that possibility is non-existent. Because if possibility is non-existent, then possibility is not attained. Also, possibility, if it is assumed to be established, would be actual, because impossibility is non-existence, and what removes non-existence is existence. So it must be that possibility is existent. Thus it is established: if something among things is supposed to be possibly existent by itself, then its possibility is either an existential attribute or a non-existential attribute. And both divisions are false, so the statement that something is possibly existent is false.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against the reality of possibility (imkan) as a real attribute. The argument proceeds by considering whether possibility (imkan) itself is existent or non-existent. If it is existent, it must be either necessary by itself or possible. If it is necessary by itself, that is impossible because possibility is dependent on the possible (mumkin); dependency contradicts necessity. If it is possible by itself, then it requires another possibility, leading to infinite regress. If possibility is non-existent, then it is not real, contradicting the claim that something is possible. Additionally, since impossibility ('adam) is non-existence, its opposite must be existence, so possibility must be existent. Therefore, possibility cannot be either a real existent or a mere non-existence, so the notion of a thing being 'possible existence' is unfounded. This refutes the concept of intermediate states between existence and non-existence.

Key Terms
إمكان
ممكن
واجب الوجود
صفة وجودية
صفة عدمية
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'possible' is a property of things; the argument shows it leads to contradictions.
Correction: The proof demonstrates that 'possible' cannot be a real attribute because it would entail either dependence on the possible (contradicting necessity) or an infinite chain. Therefore, possibility is not a real attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what happens if possibility is assumed to be existent?
A) It would be a valid attribute.
B) It would be necessary of existence by itself or possible, both leading to contradiction.
C) It would be non-existent.
D) It would be a property of the essence.

Key Takeaway
The concept of 'possible existence' as a real attribute is self-contradictory; both its existence and non-existence lead to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act as if your existence or your choices are in a neutral 'possible' state, independent of Allah's constant creation? If there is no real intermediate state, then every moment you exist is by Allah's direct will. How would your daily decisions change if you truly internalized that?
?
The proof shows that claiming something is 'just possible' leads to contradictions. In your life, do you hold onto the idea that certain outcomes are merely 'possible' without entrusting them fully to Allah? What would it mean to abandon that illusion and see all existence as either from Allah or nothing?
?
Consider a specific sin you struggle with. Do you think of it as 'just possible' to fall into, as if its occurrence is a neutral possibility? The proof destroys that neutrality. How does knowing that your action either is (by Allah's will) or is not (by His withholding) affect your sense of responsibility and your reliance on Him?

Fourth Proof: Possibility of Future Events Is Impossible
Argues that possibility regarding future events is impossible due to truth conditions of future propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the logical analysis of contradictory statements about the future to prove that possibility (imkan) regarding future events is impossible.

Core Idea
Future events must have a determinate truth value, so genuine possibility is impossible.

In this proof, the author extends the impossibility of possibility from past and present to the future. Using the example of two contradictory statements about Zayd's action tomorrow, he argues that logical principles force them to have fixed truth values, ruling out genuine indeterminacy. This lesson will walk through the four cases considered and show why only one conclusion remains.

Guided Path
1
Observe the temporal division
The author divides possibility into past, present, and future. The first two are immediately impossible because one side has already occurred.
2
Understand the contradictory statements
Consider two statements about the future: 'Zayd will go to market tomorrow' and its negation. They are contradictories.
Contradictories cannot both be true or both false.
3
Examine the four logical cases
The author lists four possibilities: both true, both false, one specifically true/false, one non-specifically true/false. Identify why the first two are impossible.
4
Reject the non-specific case
The non-specific case would mean a proposition is both true and false, which is impossible.
5
Conclude with the required result
The only remaining option is that one statement is specifically true and the other specifically false. This shows future events are determinate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن الإمكان إما أن يحصل بالنسبة إلى الماضي أو الحاضر أو المستقبل . والقسمان الأولان، لأن في هذين القسمين، أحد الطرفين قد وجد، وحصل . ومع حصول أحد الطرفين كان الطرف الثاني ممتنع الحصول . وأما المستقبل فنقول : حصول الإمكان بالنسبة إلى المستقبل محال أيضاً . ويدل عليه : إنا إذا قلنا : « زيد يخرج إلى السوق غداً » « زيد لا يخرج إلى السوق غداً » فإما أن يكونا صادقين معاً. وهو محال . لاستحالة الجمع بين النقيضين . وإما أن يكونا كاذبين معاً . وهو محال . لامتناع ارتفاع النقيضين معاً . ولما بطل هذان القسمان، بقي أن يقال : إما أن أحدهما صادق بعينه، والآخر كاذب بعينه، وإما أن يكون أحدهم لا على التعيين صادقاً، ويكون الآخر لا على التعيين كاذباً . والقسم الثاني باطل . لأن كون القضية صادقة وكاذبة : محال . فلم يبق إلا أن يقال: أحدهما صادق بعينه، والآخر كاذب بعينه. وهذا هو المطلوب.
The fourth proof: Indeed, possibility either occurs in relation to the past or the present or the future. The first two divisions are impossible because in these two divisions one of the two sides has already occurred and come to be. And with the occurrence of one side, the other side becomes impossible of occurrence. As for the future, we say: the occurrence of possibility in relation to the future is also impossible. And evidence for that: when we say 'Zayd will go to the market tomorrow' and 'Zayd will not go to the market tomorrow', then either both are true together, which is impossible because of the impossibility of combining two contradictories; or both are false together, which is impossible because of the impossibility of both contradictories being false. And when these two divisions are invalidated, it remains to be said: either one of them is specifically true and the other specifically false, or one of them is non-specifically true and the other non-specifically false. The second division is false because a proposition being both true and false is impossible. So nothing remains except to say: one of them is specifically true and the other specifically false. And this is what is required.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof that possibility (imkan) cannot be real. It divides possibility into three temporal categories: past, present, and future. For past and present, possibility is impossible because one outcome has already occurred, making the opposite impossible. For the future, the argument uses the example of two contradictory statements about Zayd's action tomorrow. If possibility were real, both statements would be neither true nor false, but the argument shows that they must have definite truth values. Four cases are considered: both true (impossible), both false (impossible), one true specifically and the other false (the only coherent option), or one true nonspecifically and the other false nonspecifically (meaning indeterminacy, which is impossible because a proposition cannot be simultaneously true and false). The only remaining possibility is that one of the two contradictory statements is specifically true and the other false. This conclusion (al-matlub) demonstrates that future events are determined, not genuinely possible in an indeterminate sense. Thus, possibility as a real attribute does not apply to the future either.

ماضي

كذب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Future events are genuinely possible in an indeterminate sense.
Correction: Logical principles force a determinate truth value for future propositions, so genuine possibility is impossible.

Checkpoint
In the fourth proof, what is the conclusion about the two contradictory statements about the future?
A) Both are false.
B) Both are true.
C) One is specifically true and the other specifically false.
D) One is true and the other false, but not specifically.

Key Takeaway
The logical analysis of future contradictory statements proves that future events must have a determinate truth value, thus possibility (imkan) regarding the future is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every future event, including your actions tomorrow, already has a determinate truth value, does that undermine your sense of free will? How do you reconcile this with the daily choices you make? Are you willing to accept that your decisions are part of a predetermined truth, or do you resist this conclusion? Examine your heart: do you truly feel that your choices are real, or is that feeling an illusion? Commit to reflecting on this tension between determinism and personal responsibility in your own life.
?
Consider the statement 'Zayd will go to the market tomorrow.' The proof says it is either specifically true or specifically false. Now apply this to a decision you are currently facing. If the truth is already determined, what does that mean for your worry and planning? Can you trust that whatever outcome occurs is already known and permitted by Allah? How does this affect your anxiety about the future? Will you choose to submit to this reality or continue to struggle against it?
?
The proof shows that possibility is impossible for future events. Does this mean that your hopes and duas are meaningless? Or does it mean that your actions are already accounted for in Allah's knowledge? How does this understanding change the way you pray and strive? Are you willing to act with full sincerity knowing that the outcome is already determined? Ponder: do you live as if the future is open or as if it is already written? What would change if you truly internalized this proof?

Objections Against Imkan as a Real Attribute
Objections against imkan as a real attribute and conclusion it is mental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objections raised against the concept of imkan (possibility) being a real attribute, and why the opponents conclude that imkan is only a mental consideration.

Core Idea
Imkan cannot be a real attribute; it's just a mental hesitation.

The author begins by presenting the arguments of those who deny that imkan (possibility) is a real attribute existing in external things. Two main objections are discussed: one based on the impossibility of describing immutable essences or prior existence, and another based on future contingents leading to determinism. These arguments aim to show that what we call 'possibility' is nothing but a state of uncertainty in the mind.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection
Read from 'وأيضاً به' to 'امتنع القول بكون الإمكان صفة للوجود'. This objection argues that imkan cannot describe essence (mahiyya) because essences are unchanging, nor existence (wujud) because possibility occurs before existence.
Notice the dilemma: imkan must attach to either mahiyya or wujud, but both are impossible.
2
Read the fourth proof
Read from 'الحجة الرابعة' to 'فهذا الإمكان حاصل في الأذهان. فأما في الأعيان، فإنه ممتنع الحصول'. This longer argument shows that possibility relative to the future leads to a contradiction unless one side is determined, making it necessary or impossible.
The argument uses the example of Zayd going to the market tomorrow.
3
Identify the conclusion
Note that the passage concludes that imkan is only in the mind (al-adhhan), not in external entities (al-a'yan). This is stated clearly: 'فهذا الإمكان حاصل في الأذهان. فأما في الأعيان، فإنه ممتنع الحصول'.
4
Reflect on the reasoning
Consider how the arguments undermine the idea of genuine contingency. Think about why the opponents think imkan must be mental: because if it were real, it would force determinism.
This connects to the learning objective.

وأيضاً به : القائلون بأن المعدوم شيء لا يمكنهم القول بالإمكان . وذلك لان على هذا المذهب إما أن يكون وصفاً للماهية أو للوجود، ولا جائز أن يكون وصفاً للماهية، لأن الماهيات يستحيل عليها الانقلاب والتغير . فلا نعقل كون الإمكان وصفاً لها، ولا جائز أن يكون وصفاً للوجود، لأن الإمكان حاصل قبل الحدوث. فالموصوف بذلك الإمكان، يجب أن يكون حاصلاً قبل حصول الوجود والوجود يمتنع كونه حاصلاً قبل نفسه . وإذا كان كذلك، امتنع القول بكون الإمكان صفة للوجود . الحجة الرابعة : إن الإمكان إما أن يحصل بالنسبة إلى الماضي أو الحاضر أو المستقبل . والقسمان الأولان، لأن في هذين القسمين، أحد الطرفين قد وجد، وحصل . ومع حصول أحد الطرفين كان الطرف الثاني ممتنع الحصول . وأما المستقبل فنقول : حصول الإمكان بالنسبة إلى المستقبل محال أيضاً . ويدل عليه : إنا إذا قلنا : « زيد يخرج إلى السوق غداً » ـ « زيد لا يخرج إلى السوق غداً »، فإما أن يكونا صادقين معاً. وهو محال . لاستحالة الجمع بين النقيضين . وإما أن يكونا كاذبين معاً . وهو محال . لامتناع ارتفاع النقيضين معاً . ولما بطل هذان القسمان، بقي أن يقال : إما أن أحدهما صادق بعينه، كاذب بعينه، وإما أن يكون أحدهم لا على التعيين صادقاً، ويكون الثاني لا على التعيين كاذباً . والقسم الثاني باطل . لأن كون القضية صادقة وكاذبة : صفة حقيقية. والصفة الحقيقية الموجودة في الأعيان لابد لها من محل موجود في الأعيان . وكل ما كان موجوداً في الأعيان، فهو متعين في نفسه . فإن كل ما كان موجوداً في نفسه، فهو من حيث إنه هو : متعين فيثبت أن كل ما كان موجوداً في الأعيان فهو متعين في نفسه، فوجب أن يقال أن كل ما لا يكون متعيناً في نفسه فإنه لا يكون موجوداً في الأعيان، وما لا يكون موجوداً في الأعيان امتنع كونه محلاً، لصفة موجودة في الأعيان . وقد بينا : أن كون الخبر في نفسه مطابقاً أو كونه غير مطابق للمخبر في نفسه : أمر ثابت في نفس الوجود فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن قول من يقول : إن الصادق أحدهما لا بعينه باطل . ولما بطل هذا ثبت : أن أحدهما في نفس الأمر صادق بعينه، وأن الثاني في نفس الأمر كاذب بعينه . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك، كان الطرف المطابق للجانب الصادق، يكون واجب الحصول . والطرف المطابق للجانب الكاذب يكون ممتنع الوقوع . فيثبت : أن الشيء في نفسه لا يكون ممكن الوجود البتة، بل يكون إما واجب الحصول، أو ممتنع الحصول . إلا أن العقل لما كان لا يعرف الجانب الواقع، والجانب الممتنع : بقي متوقفاً متردداً . فهذا الإمكان حاصل في الأذهان. فأما في الأعيان، فإنه ممتنع الحصول . فهذه جملة الوجوه الدالة على نفي الإمكان. وهو آخر الكلام في السؤال. والجواب : إن مرادنا من لفظ الإمكان، هو كون الشيء بحيث يجوز أن يستمر على ما كان عليه قبل ذلك ويجوز أن لا يبقى على ما كان عليه قبل ذلك . وإذا ظهر مرادنا من لفظ الإمكان، زالت الشبهات المذكورة . لأنا نعلم أن الإنسان الجالس لا يمتنع بقاؤه على الجلوس، ولا يمتنع زوال ذلك الجلوس . والعلم به ضروري . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : الذي حدث بعد العدم الأزلي :
And also by it: those who say that the non-existent is a thing cannot assert possibility. That is because according to this doctrine, either it is a description of the essence or of existence, and it is not permissible for it to be a description of the essence, because essences are impossible to change and alter. So we do not conceive possibility as a description of them, and it is not permissible for it to be a description of existence, because possibility occurs before coming-into-being. So that which is described by that possibility must exist before the occurrence of existence, and existence is impossible to be before itself. And if it is so, the assertion that possibility is an attribute of existence is impossible. The fourth proof: that possibility either occurs relative to the past, present, or future. The first two divisions, because in these two divisions, one of the two sides has already occurred and come to be. And with the occurrence of one side, the second side becomes impossible of occurrence. As for the future, we say: the occurrence of possibility relative to the future is also impossible. And it is indicated by: when we say, 'Zayd goes to the market tomorrow' — 'Zayd does not go to the market tomorrow', then either both are true together, which is impossible because of the impossibility of combining two contradictories; or both are false together, which is impossible because of the impossibility of removing both contradictories together. And when these two divisions are invalid, it remains to be said: either one of them is determinately true and determinately false, or one of them is true without determination and the second is false without determination. And the second division is invalid, because a proposition being true and false is a real attribute. And a real attribute existing in external entities necessarily has a locus existing in external entities. And everything that exists in external entities is determinate (muta`ayyin) in itself. Indeed, everything that exists in itself is, inasmuch as it is itself, determinate. Thus it is established that everything that exists in external entities is determinate in itself, so it must be said that everything that is not determinate in itself does not exist in external entities, and what does not exist in external entities is impossible to be a locus for an attribute existing in external entities. And we have shown that the report's being in itself corresponding or its being non-corresponding to the reported-in-itself is a matter established in the very existence. So it is established by what we mentioned that the statement of one who says that the true one is one of them without specification is false. And when this is falsified, it is established that one of them in actual fact is determinately true, and the second in actual fact is determinately false. And when the matter is so, the side corresponding to the truthful side is necessary of occurrence, and the side corresponding to the false side is impossible of occurring. So it is established that a thing in itself is not at all possible of existence, but rather it is either necessary of occurrence or impossible of occurrence. Except that when the intellect does not know the actual side and the impossible side, it remains suspended and hesitant. So this possibility (imkan) occurs in minds. As for in external entities, it is impossible of occurrence. This is the sum of the proofs indicating the negation of possibility. That is the end of the discussion in the objection. And the answer: what we mean by the term 'imkan' is that a thing is such that it may continue in its former state and may not remain in its former state. And when what we mean by the term 'imkan' becomes clear, the mentioned doubts vanish. For we know that a sitting person is not prevented from remaining seated, nor is he prevented from ceasing that sitting. And knowledge of this is necessary. If you have understood this, we say: that which came into being after eternal non-existence:
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument-and-response section. First, the author presents objections raised by opponents against the concept of imkan (possibility) as a real attribute. The first objection is that imkan cannot be an attribute of the essence (mahiyya) because essences are immutable, nor of existence because existence cannot precede itself. The second, called 'the fourth proof', argues that possibility relative to the future leads to a paradox: if two contradictory future statements are both possible, they cannot be both true or both false, so one must be determinately true and the other false, which would make the true one necessary and the false one impossible, thereby denying contingency. The author then concludes that imkan is only a mental concept, not an external reality. Finally, the author gives his answer: by 'imkan' he means the thing's capacity to either continue in its current state or not, which is a self-evident notion. This definition resolves the previous objections because it does not posit imkan as a real attribute in external entities. The passage ends mid-sentence as it continues beyond the page.

صفة حقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that imkan (possibility) is a real positive attribute existing in things, like color or shape.
Correction: The objections show that imkan cannot be an attribute of essence or existence, and the future-contingent argument proves that any real attribute would be determinate and thus eliminate contingency. Therefore, imkan is only a mental concept.

Checkpoint
Why does the first objection argue that imkan cannot be an attribute of existence (wujud)?
A) Because existence is not a real thing
B) Because possibility occurs before existence, and existence cannot precede itself
C) Because existence is unchanging
D) Because the non-existent is not a thing

Key Takeaway
The objections demonstrate that imkan cannot be an external real attribute; it is merely a mental hesitation about which of two determined outcomes will occur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a major decision and feel uncertain about the outcome, this lesson says that in reality the outcome is already determined as either necessary or impossible. How does that change the way you approach your next big choice? Will you trust that God’s knowledge has already settled it, and focus on doing your best without anxiety? What one concrete step can you take this week to submit to that truth?
?
The author argues that our 'possibility' is just a mental hesitation covering our ignorance. Think of a past situation where you worried about two possible outcomes. Now that you know which actually happened, do you see that the other was truly impossible? How does that realization challenge your tendency to fear uncertain futures? Commit to reminding yourself daily that in God's plan, only what must happen will happen.
?
If imkan is only in the mind, then your feeling of 'maybe' does not reflect reality. How does this affect your view of free will? Are your choices truly yours, or are they just the unfolding of what is necessary? This lesson pushes you to reconcile your subjective experience of choice with objective determinism. How will you live differently knowing that your hesitation is not from external uncertainty but from a mind that does not yet know God's decree? Describe one way you will practice trust in God's decree today.

The Author's Definition of Imkan and Its Resolution
Author redefines imkan as a capacity, resolving earlier objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's positive definition of imkan as a thing's capacity to continue or not, and how this definition resolves the earlier objections by treating imkan as a self-evident notion rather than a real attribute.

Core Idea
Imkan is simply the capacity to persist or change – a self-evident notion.

After presenting the objections, the author now provides his own definition of imkan. He clarifies that by 'imkan' he means the thing's ability to either remain in its current state or not. This humble definition avoids the pitfalls of treating imkan as a real attribute and resolves the earlier arguments, as it is based on necessary knowledge. The passage ends with the beginning of the next argument about things coming into being after eternal non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's definition
Read from 'والجواب' to 'والعلم به ضروري'. The author states his definition of imkan: a thing's capacity to either continue in its former state or not. He says this is known by necessity.
Notice the contrast: earlier objections treated imkan as a metaphysical attribute; the author redefines it as an observable capacity.
2
See how it resolves doubts
The author claims that once his meaning of imkan is clear, the previous doubts disappear. He gives the example of a sitting person: we know necessarily that he can remain seated or stand up.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this definition avoids the earlier objections. It does not posit imkan as an attribute of essence or existence, nor does it involve future contingents in the same way. It is a simple, everyday notion.
This connects to the learning objective.
4
Note the transitional sentence
The passage ends with 'إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : الذي حدث بعد العدم الأزلي :' which introduces the next topic: things that come into being after eternal non-existence. This shows how the discussion continues.
The lesson ends here; the next part is covered elsewhere.

Rational Judgments
advanced
6 min
Possibility is Only in the Mind
Razi denies possibility as real, based on determinacy of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Fakhr al-Din al-Razi argues that possibility (imkan) is a purely mental concept and not a real property of things in themselves.

Core Idea
Everything real is determined; thus nothing in reality is merely possible.

Razi begins by establishing a metaphysical principle: a real attribute must inhere in a real, determinate subject. He then applies this to the truth-value of reports, showing that truth and falsehood are fixed realities. This leads to the conclusion that in external existence, every thing is either necessary or impossible; possibility only arises from the intellect's ignorance of which side is actual. In this lesson, we will trace this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the overall flow. Notice Razi's compressed style.
The passage is dense; focus on the logic, not every nuance initially.
2
Identify the main premise
Find the claim that a real attribute needs a real subject, and that every real thing is determined. How does Razi use this to deny undetermined truth?
This is the core of the argument.
3
Trace the conclusion about possibility
See how Razi moves from determinate truth to the necessity/impossibility of outcomes, concluding that possibility is only mental.
Ask yourself: why can't possibility be a real mode?
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider what this means for the concept of contingency. If nothing is possible in external reality, how do we explain change and choice?
Razi will address this in the next lesson with his definition of imkan.

صفة حقيقية. والصفة الحقيقية الموجودة في الأعيان لابد لها من محل موجود في الأعيان . وكل ما كان موجوداً في الأعيان ، فهو متعين في نفسه . فإن كل ما كان موجوداً في نفسه ، فهو من حيث إنه هو : متعين فيثبت : أن كل ما كان موجوداً في الأعيان فهو متعين في نفسه فوجب أن يقال أن كل مالا يكون متعيناً في نفسه فإنه لا يكون موجوداً في الأعيان ، وما لا يكون موجوداً في الأعيان امتنع كونه محلاً ، لصفة موجودة في الأعيان . وقد بينا : أن كون الخبر في نفسه مطابقاً أو كونه غير مطابق للمخبر في نفسه : أمر ثابت في نفس الوجود فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن قول من يقول : إن الصادق أحدهما لا بعينه باطل . ولما بطل هذا ثبت : أن أحدهما في نفس الأمر صادق بعينه ، وأن الثاني في نفس الأمر كاذب بعينه . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، كان الطرف المطابق للجانب الصادق ، يكون واجب الحصول . والطرف المطابق للجانب الكاذب يكون ممتنع الوقوع . فيثبت : أن الشيء في نفسه لا يكون ممكن الوجود البتة ، بل يكون إما واجب الحصول، أو ممتنع الحصول . إلا أن العقل لما كان لا يعرف الجانب الواقع ، والجانب الممتنع : بقي متوقفاً متردداً . فهذا الإمكان حاصل في الأذهان. فأما في الأعيان ، فإنه ممتنع الحصول . فهذه جملة الوجوه الدالة على نفي الإمكان. وهو آخر الكلام في السؤال. والجواب : إن مرادنا من لفظ الإمكان ، هو كون الشيء بحيث يجوز أن يستمر على ما كان عليه قبل ذلك ويجوز أن لا يبقى على ما كان عليه قبل ذلك . وإذا ظهر مرادنا من لفظ الإمكان ، زالت الشبهات المذكورة . لأنا نعلم أن الإنسان الجالس لا يمتنع بقاؤه على الجلوس ، ولا يمتنع زوال ذلك الجلوس . والعلم به ضروري . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : الذي حدث بعد العدم الأزلي : فإن العلم الضروري حاصل بأنه كان يجوز بقاؤه على ذلك العدم الأصلي وكان يجوز تبدل ذلك العدم بالوجود ، ولما تساوى الأمران، امتنع رجحان أحدهما على الآخر ، لا لمرجح . فهذا هو المراد من هذا الدليل . وعند الوقوف عليه يظهر أن السؤالات المذكورة ساقطة .
A real attribute. And the real attribute existing in external things necessarily has a subject existing in external things. And everything existing in external things is determined in itself. For everything existing in itself, inasmuch as it is itself, is determined. So it is established that everything existing in external things is determined in itself, so it must be said that whatever is not determined in itself is not existing in external things, and what is not existing in external things is impossible to be a subject for an attribute existing in external things. And we have shown that the report being in itself corresponding or being non-corresponding to the reported thing in itself is a matter fixed in existence itself. So it is established by what we have mentioned that the statement of one who says that the truthful one is one of them without specification is false. And when this is false, it is established that one of them is in reality truthful specifically, and the second is in reality false specifically. And whenever the matter is thus, the side corresponding to the truthful side is necessary of occurrence, and the side corresponding to the false side is impossible of occurrence. So it is established that a thing in itself is not possible of existence at all, but is either necessary of occurrence or impossible of occurrence. Except that since the intellect does not know the actual side and the impossible side, it remains suspended and hesitant. So this possibility occurs in minds. As for external things, it is impossible of occurrence. This is the summary of the proofs indicating the negation of possibility. And that is the end of the discussion on the objection. And the answer: Our intended meaning of the term 'possibility' is that the thing is such that it is permissible that it continues as it was before and permissible that it does not remain as it was before. And when our intended meaning of the term 'possibility' becomes clear, the mentioned doubts disappear. For we know that the sitting person's remaining upon sitting is not impossible, nor is the removal of that sitting impossible. And knowledge of it is necessary. If you have understood this, we say: That which occurred after eternal non-existence: necessary knowledge is obtained that it was permissible for it to remain upon that original non-existence and permissible for that non-existence to change into existence, and when the two matters were equal, preponderance of one over the other is impossible without a preponderator. This is the intended meaning of this proof. And upon understanding it, it becomes clear that the mentioned objections are invalid.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical argument about the nature of possibility (imkan) in relation to existence and attributes. The author begins by asserting that a real attribute (sifa haqiqiyya) existing in external things (a'yan) must have a subject (mahall) that also exists in external things. He then argues that everything existing in external things is determined (mut'ayyan) in itself, meaning it has a specific identity. Consequently, anything not determined in itself cannot exist in external things nor serve as a subject for a real attribute. This establishes a metaphysical principle: existence in reals entails determination.

Next, the author applies this to the truth value of reports (khabar). He argues that a report's correspondence or non-correspondence to its object is a fixed fact in existence. Therefore, to say 'the truthful one is one of them without specification' is false; in reality, one is specifically truthful and the other specifically false. Since truth and falsehood are determinate, what corresponds to the truthful side is necessarily existent (wajib al-husul), and what corresponds to the false side is impossible (mumtani' al-wuqu'). Hence, nothing in reality is merely possible (mumkin); everything is either necessary or impossible. The appearance of possibility arises only from the intellect's ignorance of which side is actual, so possibility is a mental concept (hāṣil fī al-adhhān), not a real mode.

After concluding the refutation, the author presents a response (jawab) to clarify the intended meaning of 'imkan'. He defines it as a thing's capacity to either continue as it is or change from its current state. This removes previous doubts: we necessarily know that a sitting person can remain sitting or stand up. Similarly, regarding something that originated after eternal non-existence (al-'adam al-azali), we know it could have remained non-existent or become existent, and without a preponderant (murajjih), one outcome cannot occur over the other. This is the proof's real import; once understood, the objections are invalid.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility (imkan) is a real ontological state of things in themselves, independent of the mind.
Correction: Razi argues that in external reality, every thing is either necessary or impossible. What we call 'possible' is only a mental judgment due to ignorance of the actual determinate outcome.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the statement 'the truthful one is one of them without specification' false?
A) Because both reports could be false.
B) Because truth and falsehood are determinate in reality, and every real thing is determined.
C) Because the intellect cannot know which one is true.
D) Because the concept of 'one of them' is self-contradictory.

Key Takeaway
In external reality, nothing is merely possible; everything is either necessary or impossible, and possibility is only a mental construct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that in external reality, nothing is merely possible; every outcome is either necessary or impossible. Think of a situation in your life where you are uncertain about the future—a job offer, a medical result, a relationship. Are you willing to admit that your uncertainty is purely due to your ignorance, and that in reality, one outcome is already fixed as necessary and the other as impossible? How would embracing this truth change the way you worry or plan? Will you commit to trusting Allah's decree even when your intellect hesitates?
?
The passage states that the intellect remains suspended because it does not know which side is actual. How often do you mistake your own lack of knowledge for real ambiguity in Allah's creation? Can you identify a specific belief or action in your life that assumes that things could have been otherwise—that there is genuine possibility rather than a determined reality? What would it take for you to live as if every moment is exactly what Allah has willed, not merely possible?
?
If possibility is only in the mind, then every event that occurs—even those that seem random or optional—is actually necessary. Does this thought comfort you or trouble you? Why? Consider your own choices: do you truly believe that your decisions are real and meaningful, even if they are determined? How does this lesson challenge your sense of responsibility and your reliance on Allah?

Defining Imkan as Capacity for Change
Razi defines imkan as capacity for persistence or change and applies it to the proof of a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defines possibility (imkan) as the capacity for a thing to persist or change, and how this definition resolves objections against the existence of possibility.

Core Idea
Imkan is the known capacity for a thing to continue or alter its state.

After arguing that possibility is only mental, Razi now clarifies what he actually means by 'imkan' in theological discourse. He defines it not as a real mode of being, but as a thing's ability to remain as it is or to change. This definition is known by necessity, as seen in the example of a sitting person. Razi then applies this to the creation of the world: something that came into existence after eternal non-existence could have remained non-existent or become existent, and without a preponderant (murajjih), neither outcome would occur. This shows that imkan is a real capacity, not a mental illusion. In this lesson, we will unpack this definition and its role in the proof of a creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer section
Read from 'والجواب' to the end. Notice how Razi explicitly states his intended meaning of imkan.
This is the positive definition after the negative argument.
2
Grasp the definition
Focus on the phrase: 'كون الشيء بحيث يجوز أن يستمر على ما كان عليه قبل ذلك ويجوز أن لا يبقى على ما كان عليه قبل ذلك'. This is the core definition.
Imkan is about a thing's relationship to its own states, not a separate property.
3
Understand the example
Razi gives the example of a sitting person: we know necessarily that he can remain sitting or stand up. This shows imkan is known by necessity.
The example grounds the definition in common experience.
4
Apply to creation
See how Razi applies this to something that came into being after eternal non-existence: it could have remained non-existent or become existent. The need for a preponderant (murajjih) proves a creator.
This is the famous argument from possibility (imkan) for God's existence.
5
Reflect on the resolution
Razi says that once this definition is understood, the previous objections against possibility fall away. Why?
Because the definition shows imkan is not a real attribute but a capacity, so it doesn't require a real subject.

?

?

?

Ali's Proof from Frustration of Intentions
Ali gives a rational proof of divine power from failure of human efforts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ali ibn Abi Talib's rational proof for God's existence based on the failure of human intentions and the occurrence of undesired outcomes.

Core Idea
Human effort does not always produce intended results, pointing to a higher Power.

This lesson presents a classical rational argument attributed to Ali ibn Abi Talib. It uses a simple observation about human striving and its results to demonstrate that the world's affairs are governed by a power beyond human capacity. The argument is both accessible and profound, and it remains a staple of Sunni theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of the proof.
2
Identify the two key observations
The passage states two contrasting situations: a person strives hard but fails, and a person does not strive but succeeds. These observations form the foundation of the argument.
3
Understand the conditional reasoning
If human striving determined outcomes, then striven-for goals would always succeed and striven-against would never occur. Since reality is the opposite, a higher power must govern.
This logical step is the core of the proof.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that the management of the world and its events depends on a powerful, wise Being who overrules human capacities. Consider how this connects to Islamic theodicy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني عشر : سئل علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه عن الدليل فقال : الدليل عليه نقص العزائم ، وفسخ الهمم ، وتقرير هذا الدليل : هو أن الإنسان يسعى في تحصيل شيء من المطالب على أقصى الوجوه ويتعذر عليه ذلك وقد لا يسعى في تحصيله البتة فيحصل . فلو كان حصول هذه الأحوال بسعيه ، لوجب أن يحصل ما سعى في تحصيله ، وأن يمتنع ما سعى في امتناعه ، ولما كان الأمر بالعكس من ذلك . علمنا : أن تدبير أحوال هذا العالم ، وتقدير حوادثه يتعلق بحكمة موجود قهر بقدرته قدر العباد .
The twelfth: Ali ibn Abi Talib, may God be pleased with him, was asked about the evidence. He said: The evidence for Him is the frustration of intentions and the dissolution of determinations. The explanation of this evidence is that a person strives to obtain something of the goals in the utmost ways, and it eludes him, and sometimes he does not strive at all to obtain it, yet it is attained. So if the attainment of these states were by his striving, then necessarily what he strove for would be attained, and what he strove to prevent would be prevented. But since the matter is the opposite of that, we know that the management of the states of this world and the decreeing of its events are dependent upon the wisdom of an Existent who has subdued the capacity of the servants by His power.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof for the existence and power of God, attributed to Ali ibn Abi Talib. It is an argument from the frustration of intentions (naks al-'aza'im) and the dissolution of determinations (faskh al-himam). The argument begins by observing that a person may strive intensely to achieve a goal yet fail, while at other times a goal is achieved without any effort. If the occurrence of events were solely due to human striving, then striven-for goals would always be attained and striven-against outcomes would never occur. But experience shows the opposite. Therefore, the governance of the world and its events must be in the hands of a powerful, wise Being who overrules human capacities.

Key Terms
نقص العزائم
فسخ الهمم
تقدير
قهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies human causality altogether.
Correction: The argument does not deny that human efforts have some effect; it only shows that outcomes are not entirely determined by human striving, indicating a higher power that overrules.

Checkpoint
According to Ali's argument, why does the frustration of intentions prove God's existence?
A) Because all human efforts fail ultimately.
B) Because if human efforts determined outcomes, they would always succeed, but they don't.
C) Because God directly causes every human action.
D) Because humans never achieve what they strive for.

Key Takeaway
The frustration of human intentions reveals that the world's governance belongs to a powerful, wise Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you worked extremely hard for something and it didn't happen. When you realized your effort was not enough, did you turn to God or did you feel despair? How can this argument help you surrender your plans to Him?
?
You often act as if your success is entirely your own doing. After reading this proof, what specific change can you make in your daily attitude to acknowledge God's power over every outcome?
?
If the frustration of intentions points to a higher Power, then when your plans fail, what is that failure telling you about your reliance on God? Will you now invite Him into your planning before you even begin?

Umar's Proof from the Diversity of Human Faces
Umar uses a chessboard analogy to argue for a powerful Creator from facial diversity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Umar ibn al-Khattab's argument from the diversity of human faces and how it demonstrates the infinite creative power of Allah.

Core Idea
A small fixed face with endless variation points to an all-powerful Creator.

This lesson explores a beautiful proof attributed to Umar ibn al-Khattab. He uses an analogy with chess to highlight the astonishing diversity in human faces, despite their small, fixed structure. The passage invites us to reflect on a familiar phenomenon that reveals the boundless creativity of the Fashioner.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Get a sense of Umar's logical flow and the analogy he uses.
Pay attention to the key terms: chessboard, face board, Creator.
2
Grasp the chessboard analogy
Umar compares the human face to a chessboard: both are small boards, but unlike chess pieces, facial features are fixed and cannot move. Yet the diversity is greater.
This contrast is crucial for the argument.
3
Identify the conclusion
Despite the limitations, no two faces are identical anywhere. This points to the perfect creative power of Allah, who manifests endless variety from limited components.
The conclusion is stated explicitly in the passage.
4
Extend the point beyond faces
Umar extends the argument: no two persons are alike in any aspect—temperament, character, handwriting, fortune. The diversity is a vast ocean of divine wisdom.
This shows the argument is about whole individuality, not just appearance.
5
Reflect on the implication
Think about how this everyday observation serves as a profound proof. It opens a 'wide door' to recognizing the Creator's attributes.
This proof is an example of teleological argument (design).

Source Text

الثالث عشر : سئل عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه عن الدليل . فقال : إن الناس يبالغون في تعظيم الأعاجم حيث استخرجوا علم الشطرنج فإنهم وضعوا طريقا عجيبا ، يظهر في الرقعة المختصرة أنواعا من اللعب والمماسات غير متناهية . ثم قال : وإن رقعة وجه الإنسان أصغر من رقعة الشطرنج، وإن تلك المماسات الموضوعة في بيوت الشطرنج قد تنتقل من بيت إلى بيت ، وأما الأعضاء الموضوعة في رقعة وجه الإنسان فإنها لا تنتقل عن أمكنتها . ثم مع هذه الأحوال ، فإنك لا ترى إنسانين في المشرق ، والمغرب يتشابهان في صورة الوجه البتة . وهذا يدل على كمال قدرة هذا الخالق المصور حيث أظهر الأنواع التي لا نهاية لها من التفاوت، في هذه الأجسام القليلة الموضوعة في هذه الرقعة الصغيرة . واعلم أنك إذا وقفت على هذه الدقيقة علمت أنه لا يوجد إنسانان تتشابه صورتاهما البتة، ولا يوجد إنسانان تتشابه أحوالهما حتى أن كل واحد لا بد وأن يخالف صاحبه في الطبع والخلق والمشي وفي كسور العمامة . وفي الخط ، والكتابة ، والحظ من السعادة والشقاوة . وهذا باب واسع وبحر لا ساحل له.
The thirteenth: Umar ibn al-Khattab, may God be pleased with him, was asked about the evidence. He said: People exaggerate in magnifying the non-Arabs because they extracted the science of chess, for they devised an amazing method: there appear on the small board types of play and contacts without end. Then he said: Yet the board of the human face is smaller than the chessboard, and those contacts placed in the houses of chess can move from house to house, while the organs placed on the board of the human face do not move from their places. Then, despite these conditions, you do not see two persons in the East and the West who resemble each other in facial form at all. This indicates the perfect power of this Creator, the Fashioner, who manifested the endless types of variation in these few bodies placed on this small board. And know that if you grasp this subtlety, you will know that there exist no two persons whose forms resemble each other at all, and there exist no two persons whose states resemble each other, such that each one must differ from his companion in temperament, character, gait, and in the folds of the turban, and in handwriting, and in lot from happiness and misery. This is a wide door and a shoreless sea.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for God's power attributed to Umar ibn al-Khattab. It is an argument from the diversity of human faces, using an analogy with chess. Umar notes that people admire the game of chess for its infinite variety of moves on a small board. He then contrasts this with the human face: the facial 'board' is even smaller, and unlike chess pieces, the facial features are fixed in place. Yet despite these limitations, no two human faces are identical anywhere in the world. This immense and endless variation from such limited components demonstrates the perfect creative power of the Fashioner (al-Khaliq al-Musawwir). Umar extends the point: no two humans are alike in their entire state—temperament, character, gait, turban folds, handwriting, fortune—indicating a vast, shoreless sea of divine wisdom.

Key Terms
رقعة الوجه
الشطرنج
الخالق المصور
التفاوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof only concerns physical appearance or facial features.
Correction: The proof extends to all aspects of a person's individuality, including temperament, character, handwriting, and fortune, as explicitly stated in the passage (لا يوجد إنسانان تتشابه أحوالهما).

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Umar's chessboard analogy?
A) Chess is a greater miracle than the human face.

D) Facial features can move like chess pieces.

Key Takeaway
The endless diversity of human faces from a small fixed structure reveals the limitless creative power of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If every detail of your appearance, temperament, and even your handwriting is uniquely created by Allah, then what area of your life are you most prone to complain about? How should remembering His perfect wisdom in crafting your uniqueness transform that complaint into gratitude and trust?
?
Umar extends his proof to say no two people share the same 'lot from happiness and misery'. How does this truth challenge your default assumption that others have it better than you? What would it look like to fully accept your unique destiny as a gift from the All-Wise Creator?

The Leaf and the Dream: Wonder at Creation
A man marvels at a green leaf from dry wood and receives a dream answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a simple observation of nature can lead to the recognition of a Creator, using the story of the leaf as an example.

Core Idea
A green leaf from dry wood points to a wise Creator.

This short story from al-Matalib al-Aliyah illustrates a profound truth using a simple natural observation. A man wonders at the generation of a soft green leaf from dry wood, and his question is answered in a dream. The passage invites us to see the signs of creation in everyday things.

Guided Path
1
Read the story carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the narrative flow and wonder come through.
The story is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the marvel
Notice exactly what amazed the man: a green, soft leaf growing from dry, dense wood. This is the central observation.
The contrast between the leaf and the wood is key.
3
Understand the answer
The man asks 'Who caused the leaves to grow?' and the dream answers 'He who caused the leaves to grow.' The implied answer is the Creator.
The dream is a literary device, not the main proof.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Think about how this simple marvel points to a wise and powerful Maker. This is an argument from design.
The story teaches us to see signs of Allah in creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس عشر : كان رجل مضطجعاً في ظل شجرة فسقطت ورقة من تلك الشجرة على وجهه ، فتعجب من كيفية تولد هذه الورقة الخضراء الناعمة من تلك الخشبة اليابسة الكثيفة ، فقال : من الذي أنبت الورق على الشجر ؟ فنام فرأى في منامه كأن قائلاً يقول : الذي أنبت الورق على الشجر.
The sixteenth: There was a man lying down in the shade of a tree. A leaf from that tree fell on his face. He marveled at how this green, soft leaf was generated from that dry, dense wood. He said: Who caused the leaves to grow on the tree? Then he slept and saw in his dream as if someone said: He who caused the leaves to grow on the tree.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This short passage is a narrative illustration. A man lies under a tree, a leaf falls on his face, and he wonders at the generation of a soft, green leaf from dry, hard wood. He asks who caused the leaves to grow on the tree. He then falls asleep and in a dream receives a reply: 'He who caused the leaves to grow on the tree.' The implied answer is God, the Creator. This story serves as a simple argument from nature: the transformation of dry wood into a fresh leaf is a marvel that points to a wise and powerful Maker. The dream confirms that the answer is the Creator. This is a typical 'argument from design' simplified.

Key Terms
تولد الورقة
الخشبة اليابسة
أنبت الورق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dream itself is the main point or the proof.
Correction: The dream is a literary device; the real point is the natural marvel that points to the Creator.

Checkpoint

A) The tree's height.
B) The leaf falling on his face.
C) How a green leaf comes from dry wood.
D) The shade of the tree.

Key Takeaway
Observing the transformation of dry wood into a fresh leaf leads to the recognition of a wise Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see a green leaf on a dry branch every day. Have you ever stopped to ask 'Who did this?' If not, what does that numbness say about your relationship with the Creator?
?
The man in the story received an answer in a dream. What would it take for you to truly believe that the signs around you are from God? Are you waiting for a dream while ignoring the leaf that has already fallen?
?
If you were to die tonight, how many marvels of creation would you have passed by without a single thought of their Maker? Name one small sign you will commit to noticing tomorrow as an act of worship.

First Proof: Universal Esteem for the Spiritual
Author presents the first rational proof for the superiority of spiritual over physical beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational proof for the superiority of spiritual beings over physical beings, which relies on the universal instinctive judgment of all creatures.

Core Idea
Universal human and animal instinct honors the spiritual and disdains the merely physical.

The author presents a compelling rational argument grounded in observable human behavior: people everywhere instinctively honor those who are detached from worldly pleasures and disdain those absorbed in them. This proof is not limited to any particular culture or religion but is evident even in children and animals, revealing an innate hierarchy of worth. In this passage, we explore how this universal judgment establishes the superiority of spiritual beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument and the examples provided.
The passage is dense with examples; note the progression from human society to animals.
2
Identify the thesis and the first proof
Find the opening statement that declares the superiority of spiritual beings, and then locate the first proof that uses the universal human standard of honor and contempt.
The proof is not a scriptural quote but a rational observation.
3
Examine the examples
The author gives several examples: a person obsessed with physical pleasures, someone detached, then children and animals. Analyze how each example supports the claim that spiritual qualities are universally esteemed.
Notice the progression: intelligent people → all peoples → children → animals.
4
Understand the universality claim
The author emphasizes that this judgment is not merely cultural but is 'embedded in minds, implanted in souls.' Reflect on what this means for the argument.
This strengthens the proof by showing it is instinctive, not learned.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that magnifying spiritual things over physical things is agreed upon by all animals. Consider how this conclusion ties back to the initial thesis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقلاء الكاملين تشهد بأن الموجودات الروحانية موجودة . وأنها مع كونها موجودة فهي أعلى، وأكمل وأشرف من الموجودات الجسمانية . والذي يدل على ما ذكرناه وجوه اعتبارية . فالأول : إن كل إنسان كان استغراقه في حب الجسمانيات أكثر ، وكان شغفه بوجدانها والوصول إليها أشد ، كان عند العقلاء من الناس أكثر حقارة وأكمل خساسة، وكل من كان إعراضه عن الجسمانيات أكثر ، وكان التفاته إليها أقل ، وكان شغفه بالفكر في طلب المعارف أتم ، كان عند كل الخلق أعلى حالا ، وأكمل درجة . والذي يقرر هذا : أنهم إذا اعتقدوا في إنسان أنه جعل أيامه وذكره ، وفكره وقفا على تحصيل المطعوم والملبوس والمنكوح ، وصار بالكلية معرضا عن الأحوال الروحانية ، فإن كل عاقل يقضي عليه بأنه من البهائم ، وأنه خارج عن صفة الإنسانية ، وينظرون إليه بعين الحقارة والإهانة ، ولا يقيمون له في نظرهم البتة وزناً . وأما إذا اعتقدوا فيه أنه معرض عن طلب هذه الجسمانيات ، وقليل الميل إليها ، وعديم الالتفات إليها ، فإنهم يعظمونه ويعترفون له بوجوب التعظيم والاعتراف بالتقديم . واعلم : أن هذا الحكم غير مختص بالعقلاء والأكياس من الناس بل جميع طوائف أهل العالم مطبقون متفقون على هذا الحكم، وهذا يدل على أن جميع الخلق مطبقون على تحقير الجسمانيات ، وعلى تعظيم الروحانيات . بل نقول : إن الأجلاف من الترك والهند والزنج، مقرون بهذه الأحوال ، لأنهم يحترمون شيوخهم ويعظمونهم ، وما ذاك إلا أنهم اعتقدوا فيهم أنهم بسبب طول العمر عرفوا ما لم يعرفوه ، ووقفوا على ما لم يقفوا عليه ، فلأجل هذا الخيال يحترمونهم حتى إذا أحسوا من بعض الشيوخ بأفعال لا تليق بالمشايخ ، فإنهم يبالغون في إهانته واذلاله ، ويزيدون إهانته على إهانة الصبيان ، وكل ذلك يدل على أن إهانة الجسمانيات ، وتعظيم الروحانيات أمر مركوز في العقول ، مغزوز في النفوس ، يعترف به أهل الملل والنحل ، ويقر به جميع طوائف أهل العالم بل نزيد ونقول : إن الصبيان إذا رأوا الأكابر من الناس يخشونهم ، ويفرون منهم ، وما ذاك إلا لأجل إن روحانية الأكابر أقوى من روحانية الأصاغر . بل نزيد ونقول : إن السباع القوية ، والبهائم الشديدة إذا رأت الإنسان فإنها تهابه وتحتشمه ، مع أن الإنسان بالنسبة إليها في غاية الضعف ، وما ذاك إلا لأن الصفات الروحانية مهيبة معظمة بالطبع ، السباع والبهائم الشديدة القوية ، تهاب الإنسان وتحترمه وتعظمه وتفر منه لهذا السبب . فثبت بهذه التنبيهات : أن تعظيم جانب الروحانيات على الجسمانيات كالأمر المتفق عليه بين جميع الحيوانات.
The complete intelligent people testify that spiritual beings exist. And that while they exist, they are higher, more perfect, and more noble than physical beings. What indicates what we have mentioned are considered aspects. The first: Every human whose immersion in love of physical things is greater, and whose passion for finding them and reaching them is stronger, is among intelligent people more contemptible and more lowly. And whoever's turning away from physical things is greater, and his attention to them is less, and his passion for thinking in seeking knowledge is more complete, is among all creatures higher in state and more perfect in rank. And what establishes this: that if they believe of a person that he made his days, his remembrance, and his thought devoted to obtaining food, clothing, and sexual intercourse, and he became entirely turned away from spiritual states, then every intelligent person judges him as being among the beasts, and that he is outside the description of humanity, and they look at him with an eye of contempt and humiliation, and they give him in their view no weight at all. But if they believe of him that he is turned away from seeking these physical things, with little inclination toward them, and devoid of attention to them, then they honor him and acknowledge for him obligatory honor and recognition of precedence. Know: This judgment is not specific to the intelligent and clever among people, but all groups of the people of the world are agreed and unanimous upon this judgment. This indicates that all of creation are agreed upon belittling physical things and magnifying spiritual things. Rather we say: The rude among the Turks, Indians, and blacks acknowledge these states, because they respect their elders and honor them, and that is only because they believe that due to length of life, they know what they do not know, and have come to know what they have not come to know. For this imagination they respect them, so much so that if they sense from some elders actions unbecoming of elders, they exaggerate in insulting and humiliating him, and increase his humiliation over the humiliation of children. And all of that indicates that the humiliation of physical things and the magnifying of spiritual things is a matter embedded in minds, implanted in souls, acknowledged by the people of religions and sects, and affirmed by all groups of the people of the world. Rather we add and say: Children, when they see the great ones among people, they fear them and flee from them, and that is only because the spirituality of the great ones is stronger than the spirituality of the lesser ones. Rather we add and say: The strong beasts and powerful animals, when they see a human, they fear him and show reverence to him, while the human is in the utmost weakness compared to them, and that is only because the spiritual qualities are awe-inspiring and honored by nature. The strong beasts and powerful animals fear the human, respect him, honor him, and flee from him for this reason. So established by these indications: That magnifying the side of spiritual things over physical things is like a matter agreed upon among all animals.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two rational proofs for the superiority of spiritual beings over physical beings. The author begins by stating the thesis: spiritual beings are higher, more perfect, and more noble than physical beings. Then he introduces the proof: human beings universally judge those who are absorbed in physical pleasures as contemptible, and those who are detached from them as honorable. He elaborates by describing a person who devotes his entire life to food, clothing, and sexual gratification – such a person is considered beastly and outside humanity. Conversely, one who turns away from these things is honored. The author emphasizes that this judgment is not limited to the intelligent but is shared by all peoples, including the uncivilized Turks, Indians, and blacks, who respect their elders because they attribute to them spiritual knowledge. Even children fear the great, and animals fear humans due to spiritual qualities. The conclusion is that the magnification of spiritual things over physical things is an instinctive, universally agreed-upon matter.

عقلاء
حقارة
تعظيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument is based on cultural bias, so it is not universal.
Correction: The author insists that this judgment is universal and instinctive, even evident in animals, thus not merely cultural.

?

Second Proof: Stories and Games Show Spiritual Superiority
Author gives a second proof using child story-interest and gambler's neglect of food.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for the superiority of spiritual pleasures over physical ones, based on the examples of a child fascinated by stories and a gambler absorbed in a game.

Core Idea
Spiritual pleasure overpowers physical need, as seen in a child's love for stories.

The author continues his argument for the superiority of spiritual things by presenting a second proof based on observable human behavior. He draws on two everyday examples—a child absorbed in stories and a gambler immersed in a game—to show that the pleasure of the mind and spirit can completely overshadow physical desires. This passage invites us to consider what these common experiences reveal about the relative strength of spiritual versus physical pleasures.

The second aspect from the evidences indicating the preference of the side of spiritual things over the side of physical things: That the child, when he reaches the limit of understanding speech, then if an old woman sits and tells stories from the category of fables to that child, the child becomes absorbed in hearing those fables, and if one offers him the tastiest food and the most delicious drink, he turns away from it and does not pay attention to it, and remains engaged in hearing those stories. That indicates that the pleasure obtained by hearing those fables is stronger and more powerful than the pleasure obtained from that food and that drink, and that indicates that spiritual things are stronger in state than physical things. Similarly, the one occupied with backgammon and chess may remain turned away from eating and drinking for a long period, and he does not feel at all the pain of hunger and thirst, and that is only because the pleasure of victory, [the effect of pleasure]
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the superiority of spiritual things. The author uses two everyday examples. First, a child who understands speech will prefer listening to fables from an old woman over the tastiest food and drink. This shows that the pleasure from intellectual or imaginative engagement (a spiritual pleasure) is stronger than physical pleasure. Second, a person engrossed in backgammon or chess may ignore food and drink for a long time and not feel hunger or thirst, because the pleasure of winning (again, a mental/spiritual pleasure) overrides physical needs. Though the passage is cut off at the end, the argument is clear: these examples demonstrate that spiritual pleasures are more powerful, thus spiritual beings are superior.
