The First Argument: Universal Reverence for Spirituality
First proof: universal reverence for spiritual over physical among animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational argument for the superiority of spiritual matters over physical matters, based on the ingrained reverence observed in humans and animals.

Core Idea
All living beings instinctively honor the spiritual over the physical.

This lesson presents the first of four arguments from the author demonstrating that spiritual matters are naturally superior to physical matters. The argument draws from everyday observations: children fear elders, and even wild animals hold humans in awe. These phenomena point to a universal, instinctive recognition of spiritual superiority. As you read, notice the inductive reasoning from specific instances to a general principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the examples given: children and predators.
The author uses a repetitive structure to emphasize the point.
2
Identify the structure of the argument
Notice how the author moves from specific examples (children vs. elders, animals vs. humans) to a general conclusion. This is an inductive argument.
The conclusion is stated at the end: 'it is established...'.
3
Reflect on the key terms
Note the terms 'spiritualities' (روحانيات) and 'physicalities' (جسمانيات) and the phrases 'ingrained in minds, implanted in souls'.
These indicate that the recognition is not learned but innate.
4
Consider the common misunderstanding
The argument does not claim physical things are evil, only that spiritual things are naturally more revered.
This is important for avoiding misinterpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إهانته واذلاله ، ويزيدون إهانته على إهانة الصبيان ، وكل ذلك يدل على أن إهانة الجسمانيات ، وتعظيم الروحانيات أمر مركوز في العقول ، مغزوز في النفوس ، يعترف به أهل الملل والنحل ، ويقر به جميع طوائف أهل العالم بل نزيد ونقول : إن الصبيان إذا رأوا الأكابر من الناس يخشونهم ، ويفرون منهم ، وما ذاك إلا لأجل إن روحانية الأكابر أقوى من روحانية الأصاغر بل نزيد ونقول : إن السباع القوية ، والبهائم الشديدة إذا رأت الإنسان فإنها تهابه وتحتشمه ، مع أن الإنسان بالنسبة إليها في غاية الضعف ، وما ذاك إلا لأن الصفات الروحانية مهيبة معظمة بالطبع ، السباع والبهائم الشديدة القوية ، تهاب الإنسان وتحترمه وتعظمه وتفر منه لهذا السبب. فثبت بهذه التنبيهات : أن تعظيم جانب الروحانيات على الجسمانيات كالأمر المتفق عليه بين جميع الحيوانات.
Its insult and humiliation, and they increase its insult over the insult of boys. And all that indicates that the insult of physical things and the glorification of spiritual things is an ingrained matter in minds, implanted in souls, acknowledged by the people of religions and sects, and affirmed by all groups of the people of the world. Rather we add and say: Indeed, when boys see the elders among people, they fear them and flee from them, and that is only because the spirituality of the elders is stronger than the spirituality of the younger ones. Rather we add and say: Indeed, strong predators and fierce beasts when they see a human, they fear him and hold him in awe, despite humans being extremely weak in relation to them. And that is only because spiritual qualities are naturally awe-inspiring and glorified. The strong predators and fierce beasts fear the human, respect him, glorify him, and flee from him for this reason. So it is established by these indications that the glorification of the spiritual side over the physical side is like an agreed-upon matter among all animals.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents the first of four arguments (wujuh) for the superiority of spiritual matters over physical matters. The argument begins by noting that people naturally humiliate and demean physical things while honouring spiritual things, citing the example of boys who fear and flee from elders. This shows that spirituality is stronger in the elders. The argument then extends to animals: even predators fear humans, indicating that spiritual qualities inspire natural awe. The conclusion is that glorifying the spiritual over the physical is universally agreed upon among all animals, including humans. The argument proceeds inductively, drawing from observed behavior to establish a universal principle.

Key Terms

مغزوز في النفوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument implies that all physical things are evil or worthless.
Correction: The argument only demonstrates a natural inclination to honour spiritual qualities over physical ones, not that physical things are inherently evil. Physical things can be respected, but spiritual matters are inherently more sublime.

Checkpoint

D) Predators fear humans because humans are stronger.

Key Takeaway
The innate reverence for spirituality is a universal principle observed across all living beings, not just humans.

Second Argument: Spiritual Pleasures Outweigh Physical Pleasures
Second proof: spiritual over physical pleasures via child and gambler examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why spiritual pleasures are considered stronger than physical pleasures, as demonstrated by the examples of a child engrossed in stories and a gambler absorbed in play.

Core Idea
Spiritual pleasures triumph over physical ones, as seen in everyday human behavior.

The second argument for the superiority of spiritual matters appeals to everyday experience. We observe that a child will ignore delicious food to listen to a story, and a gambler will forget to eat while absorbed in a game. These examples show that the pleasure of the mind or spirit can outweigh physical desires. This passage presents these observations as empirical evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice that it presents two distinct examples and then draws a conclusion.
The examples are from common life, making the argument relatable.
2
Understand the examples
Reflect on why each example supports the point. The child's focus on stories over food shows mental pleasure is stronger. The gambler's focus on victory over hunger shows the same.
The gambler example even compares the pleasure of victory to that of eating, drinking, and intercourse.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Notice how both examples lead to the conclusion: spiritual pleasures are more noble (أشرف) and more effective (أثر) than physical ones. This is the main point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني : من الدلائل الدالة على ترجيح جانب الروحانيات على جانب الجسمانيات : أن الصبي إذا بلغ إلى حد أن يفهم الكلام، فإن المرأة الشيخة إذا جلست وذكرت الحكايات من باب الخرافات لذلك الصبي صار الصبي مستغرقاً في سماع تلك الخرافات ، فإذا عرض عليه أطيب الطعام وألذ الشراب ، فإنه يعرض عنه ، ولا يلتفت إليه ، ويبقى مشتغلاً بسماع تلك الحكايات ، وذلك يدل على أن اللذة الحاصلة بسبب سماع تلك الخرافات أشد وأقوى من اللذة الحاصلة من ذلك الطعام ومن ذلك الشراب ، وذلك يدل على أن الروحانيات أقوى حالا من الجسمانيات ، وكذلك المشتغل بالنرد والشطرنج ، قد يبقى معرضاً عن الأكل والشرب مدة طويلة مع أنه لا يحس البتة بألم الجوع والعطش، وما ذاك إلا لأن لذة الغلبة أثر عنده من لذة الأكل والشرب والوقاع ، وكل ذلك يدل على أن الروحانيات أشرف وأثر من الجسمانيات.
The second facet: from the evidences indicating the preference of the spiritual side over the physical side is that when a boy reaches the stage of understanding speech, if an old woman sits and tells stories from the category of fairy tales to that boy, the boy becomes engrossed in listening to those fairy tales. Then if the most delicious food and the tastiest drink are presented to him, he turns away from them and does not pay attention to them, and remains occupied with listening to those tales. That indicates that the pleasure resulting from hearing those tales is more intense and stronger than the pleasure resulting from that food and that drink, and that indicates that spiritualities are stronger in condition than physicalities. Similarly, the one occupied with backgammon and chess may remain abstaining from eating and drinking for a long time, while he does not feel at all the pain of hunger and thirst. That is only because the pleasure of victory has more effect on him than the pleasure of eating, drinking, and intercourse. And all that indicates that spiritualities are more noble and more effective than physicalities.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (الوجه الثاني) for the superiority of spiritual over physical matters. It provides two examples: first, a child will ignore delicious food to listen to stories, showing that mental/spiritual pleasure is stronger than physical pleasure. Second, a gambler absorbed in backgammon or chess forgets to eat, again showing that the pleasure of victory (a spiritual/psychological reward) outweighs the pleasure of food, drink, or even sex. The conclusion is that spiritual pleasures are both more noble (أشرف) and more effective (أثر) than physical ones. The argument is empirical, drawing on everyday observations.

The Shame of Physical Pleasures: A Proof of Spiritual Superiority
Third proof: shame of sexual pleasure shows it is not true happiness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument from al-Matalib al-Aliya that uses the universal shame of sexual pleasure to prove that spiritual states are superior to physical pleasures.

Core Idea
If true happiness, its display would be honored; physical pleasure is shameful, so it is not true happiness.

This argument takes a common human experience—the shame surrounding sexual pleasure—and uses it as evidence that physical pleasures are not the highest perfection. By contrasting this with the universal respect for spiritual states like knowledge and detachment, the author builds a powerful case for the superiority of the spiritual realm.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Read the opening premise: anything that is true happiness and perfection should be desirable to display. If its display is shameful, it cannot be from the category of perfections.
2
Apply to physical pleasure
Consider the strongest physical pleasure, sexual pleasure. Notice that even mentioning it is shameful, and using such words is the greatest insult. This shows it is not true happiness.
3
Contrast with spiritual states
Now look at spiritual states: knowledge, detachment from physicality, and devotion to the spiritual world. People delight in these and even those who lack them pretend to have them to gain respect.
4
Draw the conclusion
The argument concludes that physicalities are despised by consensus, while spiritualities are awe-inspiring and glorified. This proves the superiority of spiritual matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثالث : أن كل ما كان سعادة وغبطة وكمالاً ، فإن إظهاره يكون مطلوبا لكل أحد ، وإذا كان إظهاره مستقبحاً عند كل أحد ، فيدل على أن ذلك الشيء ليس من جنس الكمالات. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : أقوى اللذات الجسمانية : لذة الوقاع فلو كانت هذه اللذة من جنس السعادات والكمالات، لوجب أن يكون إظهارها مستحسناً في العقول ، ومعلوم أنه ليس الأمر كذلك ، بل العاقل يستحي من ذكره فضلاً عن إظهاره . وأيضاً : قد جرت العادات بأن الناس لا يشتم بعضهم بعضاً إلا بذكر الألفاظ الدالة على تلك الأحوال ، ولو كانت تلك اللذة من باب السعادات لما كان ذكرها أعظم أنواع الشتم والإهانة. وكل ذلك يدل على أن هذه الأحوال ليست من باب السعادات البتة. فأما الأحوال الروحانية ، وهي العلم بحقائق الأشياء ، والإعراض عن الجسمانيات ، والإقبال على عالم الروحانيات ، فإن كل أحد يبتهج بها ويستسعد بذكرها ، حتى أن من كان خاليا عنها ، فإنه يأتي بأفعال وأحوال توهم كونه موصوفاً بها ليتوسل بذلك إلى إقبال الناس على خدمته ، والانقياد الى طاعته. وكل هذه الاعتبارات دالة على أن الجسمانيات مستحقرة باتفاق الخلق وعلى أن الروحانيات مهيبة معظمة باتفاق جمهور الخلق.
The third facet: that everything which is happiness, felicity, and perfection, its display is desirable for everyone; and if its display is deemed ugly by everyone, then it indicates that that thing is not from the category of perfections. If you understand this, then we say: the strongest of physical pleasures is the pleasure of intercourse. So if this pleasure were from the category of happiness and perfections, it would be necessary that its display be considered good in minds. But it is known that the matter is not so; rather, the intelligent person is ashamed to mention it, let alone display it. Also, customs have established that people do not insult one another except by mentioning words indicating those states; and if that pleasure were from the category of happiness, its mention would not be the greatest type of insult and humiliation. And all that indicates that these states are not from the category of happiness at all. As for spiritual states—which are knowledge of the realities of things, turning away from physicalities, and turning towards the world of spiritualities—everyone delights in them and feels happy at their mention, such that even one who lacks them performs actions and states that suggest that he is described by them, in order to thereby obtain people's turning to his service and submission to his obedience. And all these considerations indicate that physicalities are despised by the consensus of creation, and that spiritualities are awe-inspiring and glorified by the consensus of the majority of creation.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument (الوجه الثالث) for the superiority of spiritual matters. It begins with a premise: anything that is true happiness and perfection should be something people wish to display publicly. If something is considered shameful to display, it cannot be from the category of true perfections. The strongest physical pleasure is sexual pleasure, yet displaying or even mentioning it is shameful; indeed, using such words is the greatest insult. Therefore sexual pleasure is not from the category of true happiness. In contrast, spiritual states such as knowledge, detachment from physical concerns, and devotion to the spiritual world are considered noble; even those who lack them pretend to have them to gain respect. The conclusion is that physical matters are despised by all, while spiritual matters are universally held in awe and reverence.

Key Terms
سعادة
كمال
الوقاع
الروحانيات
المستحسن
المستقبح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument says sexual pleasure is sinful or morally bad.
Correction: The argument does not condemn sexual pleasure as sinful. It only uses the fact that it is shameful to display to show that it is not the ultimate happiness or perfection.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Physical pleasures are not true happiness because they are shameful to display, while spiritual states are universally honored, indicating their superiority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Learning Objective

Worldly scheming instinctively earns contempt.

The fourth argument appeals to a universal human experience: deep down, people naturally disdain those who are overly obsessed with worldly gains and resort to trickery. This shared sentiment, the author argues, points to the lowliness of such pursuits. However, the argument is cut short, leaving us to ponder its intended conclusion.

The Fifth Proof: Spiritual Light Strengthens the Heart
Sanusi presents the fifth proof using experiential and anecdotal evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof for the superiority of the spiritual world over the physical, based on the transformative effect of spiritual light and the analogy of the physical world as a shadow of the spiritual.

Core Idea
When spiritual light enters the heart, worldly powers lose their sway.

Sanusi now presents the fifth proof for the superiority of the spiritual world. This proof draws on the observable effect of spiritual light on human hearts: it makes a person fearless of worldly kings and empowers them with divine strength. The passage also explains the relationship between the physical and spiritual worlds as that of a shadow to its origin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice the structure: first the effect of spiritual light, then examples, then conclusion, then an analogy.
The passage uses both experiential and analogical reasoning.
2
Understand the effect of spiritual light
Identify what happens when a light from the spiritual world enters the heart. Why does it make a person fearless of kings?
The text says strength is strengthened and power magnified.
3
Reflect on the examples
Consider the example of Ali pulling the gate of Khaybar with divine strength, and the general state of prophets and saints. How do these support the proof?
These are testimonial proofs from authoritative figures.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that the spiritual world is higher, greater, and more splendid. Notice the comparative adjectives used.
5
Consider the analogy
The passage ends with an analogy: the physical world is like an example or shadow of the spiritual world. What does this tell us about their relationship?
This reinforces the superiority of the spiritual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : في تقرير هذا المعنى أن الإنسان إذا حصل في قلبه نور من أنوار عالم الروحانيات ، قویت قوته ، وعظمت شوكته ، وصار بحيث لا يبالي بملوك الدنيا ، وكل ما سواه ، فإنهم يهابونه ويستعظمون القرب منه . قال على بن أبي طالب : والله ما قلعت باب خيير بقوة جسدانية ولكن بقوة إلهية . وأيضاً : فالأنبياء والأولياء لا يبالون بكثرة الأعداء ، ولا يقيمون لهم وزناً عند ظهور الأحوال الروحانية عليهم ، فدلت هذه الاعتبارات على أن العالم الروحاني أعلى وأعظم وأبهج من العالم الجماني . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إن أصحاب الرياضيات والمشاهدات ، قد انكشف لهم : أن العالم الجسماني كالمثال للعالم الروحاني ، وأن الأصل هو العالم الروحاني ، وأن العالم الجماني كالظل ، والرسم ، والخيال ، من العالم الروحاني .
The fifth aspect: in establishing this meaning, that when a light from the lights of the spiritual world occurs in a person's heart, his strength is strengthened, his power is magnified, and he becomes such that he does not care about the kings of this world and all besides them, and they fear him and deem closeness to him great. Ali ibn Abi Talib said: By Allah, I did not pull out the gate of Khaybar with physical strength but with divine strength. Also: the prophets and saints do not care about the multitude of enemies, and they do not give them any weight when spiritual states appear upon them. So these considerations indicate that the spiritual world is higher, greater, and more splendid than the physical world. If you know this, we say: the practitioners of spiritual disciplines and visions have discovered for themselves that the physical world is like an example of the spiritual world, and that the origin is the spiritual world, and that the physical world is like a shadow, a drawing, and an imagination from the spiritual world.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof for the superiority of the spiritual world over the physical. It starts by describing how a light from the spiritual world entering the heart strengthens a person, making them fearless of worldly powers. Two examples are given: Ali's statement about the gate of Khayyar (likely Khaybar) being pulled by divine power, and the general state of prophets and saints who disregard enemies due to spiritual states. The conclusion is that the spiritual world is higher, greater, and more splendid. Then an explanatory remark follows: the physical world is like an example or image of the spiritual world, which is the original; the physical is but a shadow, drawing, or imagination.

Key Terms
نور
قوة إلهية
روحانيات
جسمانيات
مثال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof denies the reality of the physical world, making it an illusion.
Correction: The passage affirms the physical world as a shadow or image of the spiritual, not as an illusion. It is real but dependent and lesser.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what happens when a light from the spiritual world enters a person's heart?
A) He becomes weak and fearful.
B) His strength increases and he fears no worldly kings.
C) He becomes arrogant and aggressive.
D) He loses interest in all physical activities.

Key Takeaway
The spiritual world is the origin; the physical world is its shadow, and spiritual light grants power that transcends worldly authority.

The Hierarchy of the Four Elements
Sanusi enumerates the four elements and ranks them by spirituality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the four elements demonstrate that nobility increases with spirituality.

Core Idea
Spirituality is the principle of nobility even in physical elements.

Sanusi begins his proof by examining the simplest physical entities: the four classical elements. He observes that earth, water, air, and fire form a clear hierarchy in terms of spiritual qualities. This observation sets the foundation for a broader argument about the superiority of spiritual beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to capture the overall argument.
Notice the logical flow from general statement to specific instances.
2
Identify the order of elements
Find the ranking from basest to noblest: earth, water, air, fire. Note the reasoning for each.
Sanusi uses terms like 'active power' and 'subtlety' to denote spirituality.
3
Understand the criteria
The criterion is the proportion of physicality (جسمانية) to spirituality (روحانية). More spiritual = nobler.
This principle is repeated throughout the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : فلنعتبر أحوال العالم الجسماني ، ثم ننتقل منها إلى اعتبار عالم الروحانيات ، فنقول : إنا إذا تأملنا في موجودات هذا العالم الجسماني ، وجدناها مختلفة الدرجات في مراتب الكمال والنقصان ، وذلك لأن الجسم المحض ، الخالي عن القوى النفسانية يكون في غاية النقصان ، مثل طبقات العناصر الأربعة . ثم إن هذه العناصر الأربعة أيضاً متفاوتة في الكمال والنقصان ، وكل عنصر كانت الجسمانية فيه أكثر ، والروحانية أقل ، كان أخس .. وكل عنصر كانت الروحانية فيه أكثر كان أشرف . فأخس العناصر هو الأرض لأنه ليس فيها إلا القبول والتأثير ، والقوة الفاعلة فيها ضعيفة جداً . وأما الماء فإنه بسبب ما فيه من اللطافة والحركة حصلت له قوة مؤثرة ، فلا جرم كان الماء بالنسبة إلى التراب كالروح بالنسبة إلى البدن . ولما كان الهواء أكمل لطافة ، وأصفى جوهراً من الماء لهذا السبب ، فإن الهواء مستولي على الماء . وأما النار فإنها لما كانت مشرقة عالية قوية على الفعل والتأثير ، لا جرم كانت أشرف العناصر وأعلاها . وظهر أنا لما اعتبرنا أحوال العناصر الأربعة وجدنا أنها كلما كانت أقل روحانية ، كانت أخس . وكلما كانت أكثر روحانية كانت أشرف وأعلى ، وهذا الاعتبار أيضاً يدل على أن الروحانيات أشرف من الجسمانيات . وأما الأجسام السفلية المركبة فهي ثلاثة أنواع : المعادن ، والنبات ، والحيوان . ولا شك أن القوة النفسانية الروحانية في المعادن في غاية القلة ، وفي النبات في المرتبة المتوسطة، وفي الحيوان في المرتبة العالية . فلا جرم كان أخس هذه الثلاثة هو المعادن ، وأوسطها النبات ، وأشرفها الحيوان . ثم إن الحيوان أنواع كثيرة ولها درجات متفاوتة في الخسية والشرف ، وأكثر الحيوانات روحانية هو الإنسان ، فلا جرم كان الإنسان سلطان الحيوانات ومتصرفاً فيها ، وسائر الحيوانات كالعبيد له . ثم نقول : وأصناف الناس فيهم كثرة إلا أنهم على اختلاف أصنافهم وتباين مراتبهم، مشتركون في حكم واحد وهو أن كل من كانت الروحانية عليه أغلب ، كان أشرف وأعلى
And if you know this, we say: let us consider the states of the physical world, then transfer from them to the consideration of the spiritual world. We say: when we reflect on the existents of this physical world, we find them varying in degrees of perfection and deficiency. This is because the pure body, devoid of psychic powers, is at the utmost deficiency, like the layers of the four elements. Then these four elements also vary in perfection and deficiency: every element in which physicality is greater and spirituality lesser is baser, and every element in which spirituality is greater is nobler. The basest of the elements is earth because it has only receptivity and influence, and its active power is very weak. As for water, due to its subtlety and motion, it has an influential power, so water is in relation to earth like the spirit in relation to the body. Since air is more perfect in subtlety and purer in substance than water, for this reason air dominates water. As for fire, since it is luminous, high, and powerful in action and influence, it is necessarily the noblest and highest of the elements. And it appears that when we considered the states of the four elements, we found that the less spiritual they are, the baser; and the more spiritual they are, the nobler and higher. This consideration also indicates that spiritual things are nobler than physical things. As for the lower compound bodies, they are three types: minerals, plants, and animals. There is no doubt that the psychic-spiritual power in minerals is at the utmost scarcity, in plants at the middle rank, and in animals at the high rank. So necessarily the basest of these three is minerals, the middle is plants, and the noblest is animals. Then animals are many types with varying degrees of baseness and nobility, and the most spiritual of animals is man, so necessarily man is the sultan of animals and controls them, and other animals are like slaves to him. Then we say: the types of people among them are numerous, yet despite their different types and varying ranks, they share one rule: that whoever has spirituality dominating him is nobler and higher.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents an empirical proof for the superiority of the spiritual over the physical by examining the hierarchy of physical beings. It begins with the four elements: earth (basest, least spiritual), water (more active, like spirit to body), air (more subtle, dominates water), and fire (most luminous and active, noblest). This shows that increased spirituality correlates with increased nobility. Then it considers compound bodies: minerals (least spiritual), plants (intermediate), animals (most spiritual). Among animals, humans are the most spiritual and thus the ruler. Finally, among humans themselves, those with greater spiritual dominance are nobler. The argument proceeds stepwise from simplest to most complex, using observable gradations to conclude that spirituality is the principle of nobility.

Key Terms
عناصر
روحانية
جسمانية
قبول
تأثير
لطافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The four elements are ranked by physical density or heaviness.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that ranking is by spiritual power, not physical qualities. Earth is basest due to weak active power, not heaviness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the four elements is the basest and why?
A) Fire, because it is destructive.
B) Earth, because it has only receptivity and weak active power.
C) Water, because it is passive.
D) Air, because it is changeable.

Key Takeaway
The four elements form a hierarchy where increased spirituality corresponds to increased nobility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that the noblest element, fire, is luminous, high, and powerful in action. Examine your own life: are you allowing your faith to become like fire—actively influencing others and shining brightly? Or have you settled into a passive 'earth-like' state, merely receiving without giving? What one change can you make today to increase your spiritual influence?
?
Sanusi says water is to earth as spirit is to body. Your body is like earth—it receives and is passive. Your spirit is meant to animate and direct it. Are your physical desires dominating your spirit, or is your spirit leading your body toward what is noble? What practical step can you take to let your spirit rule over your physical impulses today?
?
The hierarchy of elements shows that every increase in spirituality brings greater nobility. Look at your daily habits: do you prioritize spiritual growth (prayer, charity, knowledge) over material pursuits? If not, what is holding you back? Commit to one concrete action this week that elevates your spirituality and moves you up the ladder of nobility.

The Hierarchy of Compound Bodies
Sanusi classifies compound bodies into three types and ranks them by spiritual power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hierarchy of compound bodies (minerals, plants, animals, humans) confirms that spirituality determines nobility.

Core Idea
From minerals to humans, greater spirituality means greater nobility.

Having established the pattern in the elements, Sanusi now turns to compound bodies. He shows that the same principle applies: the more spiritual a being, the nobler it is. Humans, with the most spiritual nature, are the pinnacle of the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the full argument.
Notice that the argument continues from the previous lesson on elements.
2
Note the three types of compound bodies
Identify minerals, plants, and animals. Understand the ranking of spiritual power among them.
The passage explicitly states the degree of psychic-spiritual power in each.
3
Understand the special rank of humans
Among animals, man is the most spiritual and thus the ruler. Then among humans, spirituality determines individual nobility.
The final rule applies universally: whoever has spiritual dominance is nobler.

Source Text

وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : فلنعتبر أحوال العالم الجسماني ، ثم ننتقل منها إلى اعتبار عالم الروحانيات ، فنقول : إنا إذا تأملنا في موجودات هذا العالم الجسماني ، وجدناها مختلفة الدرجات في مراتب الكمال والنقصان ، وذلك لأن الجسم المحض ، الخالي عن القوى النفسانية يكون في غاية النقصان ، مثل طبقات العناصر الأربعة . ثم إن هذه العناصر الأربعة أيضاً متفاوتة في الكمال والنقصان ، وكل عنصر كانت الجسمانية فيه أكثر ، والروحانية أقل ، كان أخس .. وكل عنصر كانت الروحانية فيه أكثر كان أشرف . فأخس العناصر هو الأرض لأنه ليس فيها إلا القبول والتأثير ، والقوة الفاعلة فيها ضعيفة جداً . وأما الماء فإنه بسبب ما فيه من اللطافة والحركة حصلت له قوة مؤثرة ، فلا جرم كان الماء بالنسبة إلى التراب كالروح بالنسبة إلى البدن . ولما كان الهواء أكمل لطافة ، وأصفى جوهراً من الماء لهذا السبب ، فإن الهواء مستولي على الماء . وأما النار فإنها لما كانت مشرقة عالية قوية على الفعل والتأثير ، لا جرم كانت أشرف العناصر وأعلاها . وظهر أنا لما اعتبرنا أحوال العناصر الأربعة وجدنا أنها كلما كانت أقل روحانية ، كانت أخس . وكلما كانت أكثر روحانية كانت أشرف وأعلى ، وهذا الاعتبار أيضاً يدل على أن الروحانيات أشرف من الجسمانيات . وأما الأجسام السفلية المركبة فهي ثلاثة أنواع : المعادن ، والنبات ، والحيوان . ولا شك أن القوة النفسانية الروحانية في المعادن في غاية القلة ، وفي النبات في المرتبة المتوسطة، وفي الحيوان في المرتبة العالية . فلا جرم كان أخس هذه الثلاثة هو المعادن ، وأوسطها النبات ، وأشرفها الحيوان . ثم إن الحيوان أنواع كثيرة ولها درجات متفاوتة في الخسية والشرف ، وأكثر الحيوانات روحانية هو الإنسان ، فلا جرم كان الإنسان سلطان الحيوانات ومتصرفاً فيها ، وسائر الحيوانات كالعبيد له . ثم نقول : وأصناف الناس فيهم كثرة إلا أنهم على اختلاف أصنافهم وتباين مراتبهم، مشتركون في حكم واحد وهو أن كل من كانت الروحانية عليه أغلب ، كان أشرف وأعلى
And if you know this, we say: let us consider the states of the physical world, then transfer from them to the consideration of the spiritual world. We say: when we reflect on the existents of this physical world, we find them varying in degrees of perfection and deficiency. This is because the pure body, devoid of psychic powers, is at the utmost deficiency, like the layers of the four elements. This consideration also indicates that spiritual things are nobler than physical things. As for the lower compound bodies, they are three types: minerals, plants, and animals. A) Animals

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the hierarchy from minerals to humans, where do you honestly see yourself in terms of spiritual dominance? Are you more like a plant just going through the motions, or are you actively exercising your spiritual authority over your lower desires? What one change can you make today to move higher in this spiritual rank?
?
The passage says man is the sultan of animals and controls them. How does this authority sit with you when you think about how you treat animals in your daily life—whether through diet, entertainment, or waste? Does your treatment reflect the spiritual responsibility of a sultan or a tyrant?
?
Among humans, spirituality determines nobility. If your life ended today, and Allah measured your spirituality, would you be among the nobler or the baser? What specific habit or attitude has been reducing your spiritual dominance, and what will you do to change it starting right now?

The Second Path: A Slap on a Child Proves Four Principles
Introduction to the second path: a slap proves four principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single everyday event—a slap on a child's face—can serve as a proof for the four core doctrines of Islam: God's existence, His commands, reward and punishment, and prophethood.

Core Idea
A child's slap reveals four theological truths.

This lesson introduces a remarkable argument from the classical kalam tradition. Some rational thinkers claimed that even a single slap on a child's face provides immediate evidence for the existence of God, divine commands, accountability, and prophethood. As we read the passage, consider how such a simple event could carry such profound meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the phrase 'some rational people used to say'—this is not a universal claim but a reported argument.
2
Identify the four principles
The passage lists four principles that the slap manifests. Find each one: existence of God, commands and prohibitions, reward and punishment, and prophethood. Write them down.
These four are called 'the noblest of quests and the most precious of goals.'
3
Reflect on the claim
Ask yourself: How can a single slap point to all these? The rest of the second path will explain the derivation. For now, just appreciate the boldness of the claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثاني : كان بعض العقلاء يقول : إن لطمة واحدة تضرب على وجه صبي ، تظهر أن لهذا العالم إلها وأن هذا الإله أمر بعض عباده بأشياء ، ونهاهم عن أشياء ، وأن ذلك الإله أعد للمطيعين ثوابا ، وللمذنبين عقابا ، وأنه بعث إلى الخلق رسلاً مبشرين ومنذرين. وهذه هي الأصول الأربعة التي هي أشرف المطالب وأعز المقاصد
The second path: Some rational people used to say: A single slap struck on the face of a child manifests that this world has a god, and that this god commanded some of His servants with things and forbade them from things, and that that god prepared reward for the obedient and punishment for the sinners, and that He sent to creation messengers as bringers of good news and warners. These are the four principles which are the noblest of quests and the most precious of goals.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage introduces the second of three paths (arguments) for establishing theological truths. It presents a remarkable claim: a single slap on a child's face is sufficient to prove four fundamental principles of religion. The author states that some rational thinkers used this example. The four principles are: (1) the existence of God, (2) that God issues commands and prohibitions, (3) that there is reward and punishment, and (4) that God sends prophets. These are described as the most noble and precious of all inquiries. The argument works by examining the immediate intuitive reactions of a child who is slapped, which reveal innate knowledge. The rest of the second path will then unpack how each principle is derived from this single event.

Key Terms
لطمة
الأصول الأربعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second path, how many principles does a single slap on a child's face prove?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
A single slap on a child's face, according to this argument, points to the four fundamental principles of religion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness an act of injustice, even a small one like a child being slapped, do you instinctively feel that there must be a just God and a future reckoning? If not, what does that reveal about the state of your faith in these four principles? Commit today to consciously recall God's commands and accountability whenever you see wrongdoing.
?
The author claims that a single slap can manifest the noblest of quests. Yet how many of your daily experiences pass by without prompting you to reflect on God's existence, His commands, reward, punishment, or the messengers? What will you do tomorrow to let everyday events remind you of these ultimate truths?

The Slap Proves the Necessity of Divine Command
Opening argument for divine obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of a single rational act, like a slap, leads to the recognition that all human actions require divine guidance and command.

Core Idea
A single injustice calls for a divine law.

The author begins with a powerful argument from ordinary human reason. He asks us to imagine a boy who is slapped without cause. The boy's immediate judgment that this act cannot be meaningless reveals a deeper truth: human reason itself demands that every action be adjudicated by a higher law. This lesson traces that first step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
Notice that the argument begins with a specific example (the slap) and then generalizes to all actions. Identify the premise about the boy's reason and the conclusion about all actions.
3
Understand the logical principle
The argument uses a 'first' (a fortiori) reasoning: if a single act demands divine law, then many actions demand it even more. Reflect on why this reasoning is valid.
4
Connect to divine attributes
Think about how this argument implies the existence of a lawgiver (Allah) who issues commands and prohibitions. This is the first of the four demands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمطالبات عليهم متوجهة ، ولما حكم صريح عقل ذلك الصبي بأن تلك اللطمة الواحدة لا يجوز خلوها عن التكليف ، والأمر والنهي ، فأفعال كل الخلائق مع كثرة ما فيها من المصالح والمفاسد ، أولى بأن لا يجوز خلوها من التكاليف . وأما دلالة هذه اللطمة على المطلوب الثالث وهو حصول ذات الثواب والعقاب فإنه فنقول : إن ذلك الصبي إذا عرف أن ذلك الإنسان لطمه بغير سبب ، يطلب منه القصاص . فإن عجز عن استيفائه ، استغاث بمن يعينه على تحصيل ذلك المطلوب ، وهذا يدل على أن صريح عقله حكم بأنه لا بد للحسنة من ثواب ، ولا بد للسيئة من عقاب ، ولما كان صريح عقله حكم بأن هذه اللطمة لا يمكن إخلاؤها عن الجزاء أو القصاص، فكيف يمكن إخلاء أفعال كل الخلق عن القصاص ؟ وأما دلالة هذه اللطمة على المطلوب الرابع وهو بعثة الأنبياء عليهم السلام فهو أن الصبي إذا قرر أنه لا بد من القصاص ، فعند ذلك يطلب إنسانا يبين له ذلك القصاص بحيث يكون خاليا عن الزيادة والنقصان . وهذا يدل على أنه تقرر في عقله أنه لا بد في الخلق من إنسان يبين لهم مقادير المرغبات ، ومقادير الزواجر ، وذلك الإنسان ليس إلا الرسول . فظهر بهذا البيان الذي بحثناه : أن هذه اللطمة الواحدة ، كافية في إثبات هذه المطالب الأربعة الشريفة العالية [ومن الله التوفيق]
And the demands are directed upon them [the people]. When the clear reason of that boy judged that that single slap cannot be free of divine obligation and command and prohibition, then the actions of all creatures, with the abundance of benefits and evils in them, are more deserving of not being free of divine obligations. As for the indication of this slap to the third demand, which is the attainment of the reward and punishment itself, then we say: That boy, when he knows that that man slapped him without cause, he seeks retaliation. If he is unable to exact it, he seeks help from someone who will assist him in attaining that demand. This indicates that his clear reason judged that there must be reward for good and punishment for evil. And since his clear reason judged that this slap cannot be devoid of requital or retaliation, how can the actions of all creatures be devoid of retaliation? As for the indication of this slap to the fourth demand, which is the sending of prophets (peace be upon them), it is that the boy, when he decides that there must be retaliation, then he seeks a person who will explain that retaliation to him such that it is free from increase and decrease. This indicates that it is established in his mind that there must be, among creation, a person who explains to them the measures of incentives and the measures of deterrents, and that person is none other than the messenger. So it became clear through this exposition that we have investigated: that this single slap is sufficient to establish these four noble, high demands. [And from Allah is success.]
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage presents an argument for four fundamental theological demands (المطالب) using the example of a single slap. The author begins by stating that the clear reason of a boy judges that a single slap cannot be free from divine obligation (تكليف) and command and prohibition (أمر ونهي). This shows that human reason recognizes the need for a lawgiver. The author then extends this to all human actions, arguing that since even a small act like a slap requires divine guidance, all actions are subject to divine command. Next, the passage shows that the same boy, when slapped without cause, seeks retaliation, indicating that reason demands reward for good and punishment for evil. This establishes the necessity of divine justice and retribution. Finally, the boy seeks someone to explain the exact retaliation, showing the need for a messenger or prophet to detail the divine laws. The author concludes that this one example is sufficient to prove the four noble demands: the necessity of divine obligation, command and prohibition, reward and punishment, and prophethood.

Key Terms
لطمة
تكليف
أمر ونهي

The Slap Proves the Necessity of Reward and Punishment
Proof for reward and punishment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the boy's demand for retaliation after being slapped reveals that human reason requires a system of reward and punishment.

Core Idea
Reason demands that good be rewarded and evil punished.

The argument now moves from the need for a command to the need for justice. The boy, knowing he was wronged, seeks retaliation. This innate sense of justice is not arbitrary; it reflects a universal rational judgment that every action must receive its due recompense. The author shows how this leads to the necessity of divine reward and punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the scenario: the boy knows he was slapped without cause and seeks retaliation.
2
Identify the rational judgment
The text says that the boy's 'clear reason' judges that there must be reward and punishment. This is a natural human intuition.
3
See the generalization
If one slap requires requital, then all actions of all creatures require requital. The author uses this to argue for divine retribution.
4
Reflect on the implication
This argument establishes the necessity of divine reward and punishment (ثواب وعقاب) as a demand of reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمطالبات عليهم متوجهة ، ولما حكم صريح عقل ذلك الصبي بأن تلك اللطمة الواحدة لا يجوز خلوها عن التكليف ، والأمر والنهي ، فأفعال كل الخلائق مع كثرة ما فيها من المصالح والمفاسد ، أولى بأن لا يجوز خلوها من التكاليف . وأما دلالة هذه اللطمة على المطلوب الثالث وهو حصول ذات الثواب والعقاب فإنه فنقول : إن ذلك الصبي إذا عرف أن ذلك الإنسان لطمه بغير سبب ، يطلب منه القصاص . فإن عجز عن استيفائه ، استغاث بمن يعينه على تحصيل ذلك المطلوب ، وهذا يدل على أن صريح عقله حكم بأنه لا بد للحسنة من ثواب ، ولا بد للسيئة من عقاب ، ولما كان صريح عقله حكم بأن هذه اللطمة لا يمكن إخلاؤها عن الجزاء أو القصاص، فكيف يمكن إخلاء أفعال كل الخلق عن القصاص ؟ وأما دلالة هذه اللطمة على المطلوب الرابع وهو بعثة الأنبياء عليهم السلام فهو أن الصبي إذا قرر أنه لا بد من القصاص ، فعند ذلك يطلب إنسانا يبين له ذلك القصاص بحيث يكون خاليا عن الزيادة والنقصان . وهذا يدل على أنه تقرر في عقله أنه لا بد في الخلق من إنسان يبين لهم مقادير المرغبات ، ومقادير الزواجر ، وذلك الإنسان ليس إلا الرسول . فظهر بهذا البيان الذي بحثناه : أن هذه اللطمة الواحدة ، كافية في إثبات هذه المطالب الأربعة الشريفة العالية [ومن الله التوفيق]
And the demands are directed upon them [the people]. When the clear reason of that boy judged that that single slap cannot be free of divine obligation and command and prohibition, then the actions of all creatures, with the abundance of benefits and evils in them, are more deserving of not being free of divine obligations. As for the indication of this slap to the third demand, which is the attainment of the reward and punishment itself, then we say: That boy, when he knows that that man slapped him without cause, he seeks retaliation. If he is unable to exact it, he seeks help from someone who will assist him in attaining that demand. This indicates that his clear reason judged that there must be reward for good and punishment for evil. And since his clear reason judged that this slap cannot be devoid of requital or retaliation, how can the actions of all creatures be devoid of retaliation? As for the indication of this slap to the fourth demand, which is the sending of prophets (peace be upon them), it is that the boy, when he decides that there must be retaliation, then he seeks a person who will explain that retaliation to him such that it is free from increase and decrease. This indicates that it is established in his mind that there must be, among creation, a person who explains to them the measures of incentives and the measures of deterrents, and that person is none other than the messenger. So it became clear through this exposition that we have investigated: that this single slap is sufficient to establish these four noble, high demands. [And from Allah is success.]

Commentary
This passage presents an argument for four fundamental theological demands (المطالب) using the example of a single slap. The author begins by stating that the clear reason of a boy judges that a single slap cannot be free from divine obligation (تكليف) and command and prohibition (أمر ونهي). This shows that human reason recognizes the need for a lawgiver. The author then extends this to all human actions, arguing that since even a small act like a slap requires divine guidance, all actions are subject to divine command. Next, the passage shows that the same boy, when slapped without cause, seeks retaliation, indicating that reason demands reward for good and punishment for evil. This establishes the necessity of divine justice and retribution. Finally, the boy seeks someone to explain the exact retaliation, showing the need for a messenger or prophet to detail the divine laws. The author concludes that this one example is sufficient to prove the four noble demands: the necessity of divine obligation, command and prohibition, reward and punishment, and prophethood.

The Slap Proves the Necessity of Prophethood
Proof for prophethood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the boy's need for someone to explain the exact measure of retaliation demonstrates the necessity of prophets.

Core Idea
Justice requires a messenger to define its terms.

The final step in the argument extends from the need for retaliation to the need for a precise definition of that retaliation. The boy looks for a person who can tell him the exact amount of requital, free from excess or deficiency. This shows that human reason recognizes the need for a divinely guided teacher to communicate the details of the law — in other words, a prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the progression from the need for retaliation to the need for someone to explain the exact measures.
2
Understand the boy's reasoning
After deciding that retaliation is necessary, the boy seeks a person who can define the retaliation precisely, without increase or decrease.
3
See the link to prophethood
The author argues that this shows the need for a messenger (رسول) who can explain the divine laws and their measures.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Thus, prophethood is a rational necessity: humans need prophets to teach them the details of divine commands, incentives, and deterrents.

The Three Rational Divisions of Existence
The author enumerates and explains the three rational divisions of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational divisions of existence and their definitions.

Core Idea
Existence is divided into three rational categories: self, other, or neither.

Before we can reason about God's existence, we need a clear classification of how things can exist. The author presents three rational divisions that form the backbone of later arguments. This lesson unpacks these categories and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition passage
Read the Arabic passage defining the three divisions. Notice the precise phrasing for each category.
The third division is described as possible in rational terms but actually invalid.
2
Understand each division
Identify what 'exists by itself,' 'exists by another,' and 'exists neither by itself nor by another' mean. The third is explained as existence without any cause.
3
Reflect on the third division
Though invalid in reality, the third division is considered in rational discussion. Ask why the author includes it.
It serves to prevent a common logical mistake.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقلي ثلاثة : الذي يكون موجودا لذاته، والذي يكون موجودا لغيره ، والذي يكون موجودا ، لا لذاته ولا لغيره وهذا القسم الثالث معناه : أنه تحصيل الوجود للشيء من غير سبب أصلا ، لا من ذاته ، ولا من غيره ويصح أيضا طريان العدم عليه ، من غير سبب أصلا لا من ذاته ، ولا من غيره وهذا القسم ، وإن كان باطلا ، إلا أنه قسم من الأقسام المعتبرة ، بحسب التقسيم العقلي الأولي. إذا ثبت هذا ، فما لم يظهر فساد هذا القسم ، إما بحسب بديهة العقل أو بحسب الدليل المنفصل ، لم يلزم من كون الشيء غنيا في وجوده عن السبب المنفصل ، كونه واجب الوجود لذاته، لأن على تقدير كون هذا القسم صحيحا ، يكون الشيء غنيا في وجوده عن السبب المنفصل ، مع أنه يكون في نفسه قابلا للعدم وللوجود . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : من الناس من لم يعرف هذه التفاصيل ، وقال إما أن يكون الموجود غنيا في وجوده عن السبب ، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك ، فإن كان غنيا في وجوده عن السبب [المنفصل] كان واجب الوجود الذاته، وهو المطلوب . وإن لم يكن غنيا في وجوده عن السبب كان متحاجا إلى السبب ، فوجوده يدل على وجود السبب . فيثبت أن الاعتراف بوجود موجود [ غني عن السبب ] يوجب الاعتراف بوجود موجود واجب الوجود لذاته. ولقائل أن يقول : إن هذا القدر لا يفيد المقصود ، لأنه يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه وجد ، لا لذاته ولا لغيره ، بل حصل لا لأمر أصلا مع أن ذاته وماهيته قابلة للعدم فهو لأجل أنه حصل لا لسبب أصلا ، كان غنيا عن السبب ، ولأجل أن حقيقته قابلة للعدم ، لم يكن واجب الوجود لذاته ، فما لم تبطلوا هذا القسم ، لا يحصل مطلوبكم
The rational [division] is three: that which exists by itself, and that which exists by another, and that which exists neither by itself nor by another. And this third division its meaning: that it is the attainment of existence for a thing without any cause at all, not from itself, nor from another. And it is also possible for non-existence to occur upon it, without any cause at all, not from itself, nor from another. And this division, even though it is invalid, is nevertheless a division among the considered divisions, according to the primary rational division. If this is established, then as long as the corruption of this division has not become apparent, either by the intuition of the intellect or by a separate proof, it does not follow from a thing being independent in its existence of a separate cause that it is necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd) by itself, because on the supposition that this division is correct, the thing would be independent in its existence of a separate cause, while being in itself receptive of non-existence and existence. If you understand this, then we say: Among people there are those who do not know these details, and they said: Either the existent is independent in its existence of a cause, or else it is not so. If it is independent in its existence of a [separate] cause, then it is necessary of existence by itself, which is the desired conclusion. And if it is not independent in its existence of a cause, then it is in need of a cause, so its existence indicates the existence of the cause. Thus it is established that the acknowledgment of the existence of an existent [independent of a cause] necessitates the acknowledgment of the existence of an existent that is necessary of existence by itself. And an objector may say: This amount does not yield the intended purpose, because it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that it existed neither by itself nor by another, but rather it occurred without any cause at all, while its essence and quiddity are receptive of non-existence? So because it occurred without any cause at all, it was independent of a cause, and because its reality is receptive of non-existence, it was not necessary of existence by itself. So as long as you have not rendered this division invalid, you will not attain your desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument followed by an objection. The author first defines three rational divisions of existence: (1) existent by itself, (2) existent by another, (3) existent neither by itself nor by another. The third is explained as existence without any cause, whether from itself or from another, and it is also possible for non-existence to affect it without cause. Although this division is invalid in reality, it is considered in rational discussion. Next, the author makes a conditional argument: if the invalidity of the third division is not demonstrated, then independence from a separate cause does not necessarily imply necessary existence (wājib al-wujūd), because if the third division were valid, a thing could be independent yet still be contingent. Then the author reports a simplified argument made by some people: either an existent is independent of cause (thus necessary) or dependent (thus pointing to a cause). The objector then raises a problem: the simplified argument ignores the third division. An objector can say that a thing may exist without cause at all, independent, but still be contingent because its nature admits non-existence. Therefore, without refuting the third division, the conclusion that a necessary existent exists is not proven. The passage thus highlights the need to refute the possibility of uncaused contingent existence in proving the existence of a necessary being.

Key Terms
تقسيم عقلي (rational division)
واجب الوجود (necessary of existence)
سبب (cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the third rational division of existence?
A) That which exists by itself
B) That which exists by another
C) That which exists neither by itself nor by another
D) That which exists both by itself and by another

Key Takeaway
The third rational division—existence without any cause—is a logical possibility that must be refuted before concluding that independence implies necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you learned that everything that exists is either necessary by itself or caused by another. When you look at your own life—your health, wealth, relationships—do you truly feel that every moment of their existence is a gift from God, or do you unconsciously live as if you are self-sufficient? Name one area where you act as if you exist 'by yourself' and commit to thanking God for it every day this week.
?
The third division—existence without cause—is logically possible but actually impossible. Yet we often fall into the delusion that we are the sole cause of our achievements. When was the last time you took full credit for something, forgetting that your very existence and every factor involved were provided by Allah? Write down one success you felt proud of and honestly reflect: who was the real cause?
?
If the only being that truly exists by itself is Allah, then your entire existence is dependent on Him every single second. Does your daily schedule reflect this truth? Do you start your day with a conscious acknowledgment of your contingency, or do you rush into tasks as if you are in control? What specific change can you make tonight to wake up tomorrow with awareness of your absolute need for Allah?

The Independence Argument and Its Hidden Flaw
The author presents a conditional argument and responds to an objection regarding independence and necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why independence from a cause does not automatically imply necessary existence, and how the objection using the third division undermines a common argument.

Core Idea
Independence from cause does not equal necessity if existence without cause is possible.

Some argue that an independent existent must be necessary. But the author shows this conclusion is premature without refuting the third rational division. This lesson walks through the conditional argument and the objector's challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional statement
Read the passage starting from 'إذا ثبت هذا' to see how the author sets up the condition.
Note the logical structure: if X is not refuted, then Y does not follow.
2
Analyze the simplified argument
Identify the two-part argument: independent → necessary; dependent → needs cause. Understand why this seems compelling.
3
Examine the objection
The objector says the third division blocks the conclusion. Understand how: a thing can be independent yet still contingent.
This shows why the third division must be addressed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقلي ثلاثة : الذي يكون موجودا لذاته، والذي يكون موجودا لغيره ، والذي يكون موجودا ، لا لذاته ولا لغيره وهذا القسم الثالث معناه : أنه تحصيل الوجود للشيء من غير سبب أصلا ، لا من ذاته ، ولا من غيره ويصح أيضا طريان العدم عليه ، من غير سبب أصلا لا من ذاته ، ولا من غيره وهذا القسم ، وإن كان باطلا ، إلا أنه قسم من الأقسام المعتبرة ، بحسب التقسيم العقلي الأولي. إذا ثبت هذا ، فما لم يظهر فساد هذا القسم ، إما بحسب بديهة العقل أو بحسب الدليل المنفصل ، لم يلزم من كون الشيء غنيا في وجوده عن السبب المنفصل ، كونه واجب الوجود لذاته، لأن على تقدير كون هذا القسم صحيحا ، يكون الشيء غنيا في وجوده عن السبب المنفصل ، مع أنه يكون في نفسه قابلا للعدم وللوجود . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : من الناس من لم يعرف هذه التفاصيل ، وقال إما أن يكون الموجود غنيا في وجوده عن السبب ، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك ، فإن كان غنيا في وجوده عن السبب [المنفصل] كان واجب الوجود الذاته، وهو المطلوب . وإن لم يكن غنيا في وجوده عن السبب كان متحاجا إلى السبب ، فوجوده يدل على وجود السبب . فيثبت أن الاعتراف بوجود موجود [ غني عن السبب ] يوجب الاعتراف بوجود موجود واجب الوجود لذاته. ولقائل أن يقول : إن هذا القدر لا يفيد المقصود ، لأنه يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه وجد ، لا لذاته ولا لغيره ، بل حصل لا لأمر أصلا مع أن ذاته وماهيته قابلة للعدم فهو لأجل أنه حصل لا لسبب أصلا ، كان غنيا عن السبب ، ولأجل أن حقيقته قابلة للعدم ، لم يكن واجب الوجود لذاته ، فما لم تبطلوا هذا القسم ، لا يحصل مطلوبكم
The rational [division] is three: that which exists by itself, and that which exists by another, and that which exists neither by itself nor by another. And this third division its meaning: that it is the attainment of existence for a thing without any cause at all, not from itself, nor from another. And it is also possible for non-existence to occur upon it, without any cause at all, not from itself, nor from another. And this division, even though it is invalid, is nevertheless a division among the considered divisions, according to the primary rational division. If this is established, then as long as the corruption of this division has not become apparent, either by the intuition of the intellect or by a separate proof, it does not follow from a thing being independent in its existence of a separate cause that it is necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd) by itself, because on the supposition that this division is correct, the thing would be independent in its existence of a separate cause, while being in itself receptive of non-existence and existence. If you understand this, then we say: Among people there are those who do not know these details, and they said: Either the existent is independent in its existence of a cause, or else it is not so. If it is independent in its existence of a [separate] cause, then it is necessary of existence by itself, which is the desired conclusion. And if it is not independent in its existence of a cause, then it is in need of a cause, so its existence indicates the existence of the cause. Thus it is established that the acknowledgment of the existence of an existent [independent of a cause] necessitates the acknowledgment of the existence of an existent that is necessary of existence by itself. And an objector may say: This amount does not yield the intended purpose, because it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that it existed neither by itself nor by another, but rather it occurred without any cause at all, while its essence and quiddity are receptive of non-existence? So because it occurred without any cause at all, it was independent of a cause, and because its reality is receptive of non-existence, it was not necessary of existence by itself. So as long as you have not rendered this division invalid, you will not attain your desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument followed by an objection. The author first defines three rational divisions of existence: (1) existent by itself, (2) existent by another, (3) existent neither by itself nor by another. The third is explained as existence without any cause, whether from itself or from another, and it is also possible for non-existence to affect it without cause. Although this division is invalid in reality, it is considered in rational discussion. Next, the author makes a conditional argument: if the invalidity of the third division is not demonstrated, then independence from a separate cause does not necessarily imply necessary existence (wājib al-wujūd), because if the third division were valid, a thing could be independent yet still be contingent. Then the author reports a simplified argument made by some people: either an existent is independent of cause (thus necessary) or dependent (thus pointing to a cause). The objector then raises a problem: the simplified argument ignores the third division. An objector can say that a thing may exist without cause at all, independent, but still be contingent because its nature admits non-existence. Therefore, without refuting the third division, the conclusion that a necessary existent exists is not proven. The passage thus highlights the need to refute the possibility of uncaused contingent existence in proving the existence of a necessary being.

Key Terms
غنيا (independent)
متحاجا (in need)
واجب الوجود (necessary of existence)
سبب (cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Independence from a cause automatically means necessary existence.
Correction: The third division shows that something could exist without any cause, be independent, yet still be contingent in its essence.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why does independence from a cause not prove necessary existence?
A) Because nothing is truly independent
B) Because the third division allows existence without cause while still being contingent
C) Because necessary existence is impossible
D) Because independence is the same as necessity

Key Takeaway
The naive argument for a necessary existent fails unless the third rational division—existence without any cause—is proven impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the existence of God, have you ever assumed that if something exists without a cause it must be self-sufficient and necessary? How does this passage challenge that assumption, and what difference does it make in your conviction?
?
The objector points out that something could exist without any cause at all yet still be contingent. Does your reasoning for God's existence rely on ignoring this third division? If so, how will you now address it?
?
The author says: 'As long as you have not rendered this division invalid, you will not attain your desired conclusion.' Have you ever accepted a conclusion without fully addressing every possible objection? How can you ensure your faith is built on rigorous reasoning, not on ignoring hard questions?

The First Proof of Necessity as a Positive Attribute
First proof that wajib al-wujud is a positive attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that the necessity of existence is a positive attribute, based on the idea that self-necessitation is a real relation independent of the mind.

Core Idea
Self-necessity is a real, positive relation, not a mental abstraction.

This lesson presents the first argument for considering wajib al-wujud (the necessity of existence) as a positive attribute. The author shows that self-necessity means the essence necessitates its own existence, a relation that holds regardless of mental consideration. Confusion arises when one fails to distinguish between different meanings of necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic source once to get the flow. Notice the opening statement and the structure of the proof.
The passage begins with the issue statement and then presents the first proof.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify terms like وجوب الوجود (necessity of existence), اقتضاء الوجود (necessitating existence), استحقاق الوجود (deserving existence), ثبوتي (positive), and عدمي (negative).
3
Follow the argument by analogy
The proof uses an analogy: if necessitation were not positive, then the body's occupying space would also not be positive. Grasp the reductio ad absurdum logic.
The author argues that denying positivity leads to absurdity.
4
Recognize the source of confusion
The author explains that confusion arises from mixing two meanings of necessity: dependence on another vs. intrinsic deservingness.
This distinction resolves the puzzle.
5
Reflect on why this matters
Ask yourself why establishing positivity is important for kalam. It grounds real attributes of Allah.
This connects to broader theological discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية في أن وجوب الوجود. هل هو من قوم برقي أم لا؟ من الناس من قال : هذا المفهوم : مفهوم وجودي محصل . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنا بينا أن المراد من الوجوب الذاتي كون تلك الماهية مقتضية لوجود نفسها وكونها مستحقة للوجود من ذاتها، وهذا الاستحقاق ليس حصوله بسبب فرض العقل واعتبار الذهن فقط لأن هذا المعنى حاصل سواء اعتبره العقل أو لم يعتبره ، ولو جاز أن لا يكون اقتضاء الوجود وصفا وجوديا مع أنه في نفسه نسبة متحققة محصلة ، لجاز أن يقال : إن نسبة الجسم إلى الحيز بالحصول في نفسه ، ليس أمرا ثبوتيا ، بل أمرا عدميا . وعند هذا يظهر للمنصف : أن الاشتباه الواقع في أن الوجوب هل هو أمر ثبوتي أم لا ؟ كان بسبب عدم التمييز بين المفهومات التي بحثناها وميزنا بين كل واحد منهما وبين الآخر. فتارة يسبق الذهن من الوجود إلى العدم توقفه على الغير ، فيحكم بكونه عدميا ، وتارة إلى كونه عبارة عن استحقاق الوجود ، فيحكم بكونه ثبوتيا . فإن اقتضاء الثبوت وإيجابه كيف لا يكون ثبوتيا ؟ لكن لعدم التمييز بين المعنيين ، ربما يتحير الذهن
The second issue: concerning that the necessity of existence: is it of positive attributes or not? Some people said: this concept is a positive, realized concept. And there are proofs for it: The first: we have explained that what is meant by self-necessity is that that essence necessitates its own existence and that it is deserving of existence from itself, and this deservingness is not obtained by the supposition of the intellect and the consideration of the mind only, because this meaning obtains whether the intellect considers it or does not consider it. And if it were permissible that the necessitation of existence not be a positive attribute, while it is in itself a realized, actual relation, then it would be permissible to say: the relation of the body to space by occupying it is not a positive matter, but a negative matter. And here it becomes apparent to the fair-minded person that the confusion that occurred concerning whether necessity is a positive matter or not was due to the lack of distinction between the concepts that we have investigated and distinguished each from the other. Sometimes the mind leaps from existence to non-existence due to its dependence on another, so it judges it to be negative; and sometimes it leaps to its being an expression of deservingness of existence, so it judges it to be positive. For how can the necessitation and requiring of positivity not be positive? But due to the lack of distinction between the two meanings, the mind may become perplexed.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage introduces the second issue and presents the first proof that wajib al-wujud (the necessity of existence) is a positive (thubuti) attribute. The author states the position: some say this concept is a positive, realized concept. He then begins the proofs. The first proof argues that self-necessity (al-wujub al-dhati) means that the essence necessitates its own existence and is deserving of existence from itself. This deservingness is not dependent on the mind's consideration; it is real whether the mind considers it or not. If this necessitation were not a positive attribute despite being a realized relation, then one could absurdly claim that the body's occupying space is also not positive. Thus the confusion arises from failing to distinguish between the different meanings of necessity: sometimes the mind focuses on the dependence on another and calls it negative, sometimes on the deservingness and calls it positive. The author emphasizes that a positive attribute like 'necessitating existence' must itself be positive.

Key Terms
وجوب الوجود
الوجوب الذاتي
اقتضاء الوجود
استحقاق الوجود
ثبوتي
عدمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The necessity of existence is a negative concept because it implies dependence on another.
Correction: The proof distinguishes between two senses: necessity as dependence (which may be negative) and necessity as intrinsic deservingness (which is positive). The self-necessity of the Necessary Being is the latter.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what is the consequence of claiming that the necessitation of existence is not a positive attribute?
A) It would lead to the absurdity that the body's occupation of space is also negative.
B) It would mean that the concept is purely mental.
C) It would make necessity contradictory.
D) It would invalidate the distinction between possible and necessary.

Key Takeaway
The first proof establishes that self-necessity is a positive attribute because it is a real relation that does not depend on the mind, and to deny that leads to absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat God's attributes as mere concepts in your mind, forgetting that they are real and independent of your acknowledgment? How does this neglect affect your worship and reliance on Him? Will you commit to affirming His attributes as real even when you are not thinking of them?
?
The proof shows that confusion arises from failing to distinguish between two meanings of necessity. In your own understanding of God's existence, do you ever confuse His self-sufficiency with dependence? How can you correct this intellectually and spiritually, so that your certainty in His necessity is unshaken?
?
If God's necessity is a real, positive attribute that does not depend on your thought, how should this change the way you seek certainty about His existence and attributes? Will you study these proofs diligently until they become a conviction in your heart, not just a theoretical acceptance?
