The Second Proof: Necessity as a Positive Concept
Second proof: analyzing wajib into two constraints and proving the first is positive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second proof demonstrates that necessity (wajib) is a positive concept by analyzing it into two constraints and proving the first constraint is affirmative.

Core Idea
The negation of impossibility is a double negation, making it a positive concept.

In the first proof, the author argued that necessity is positive because it necessitates existence. Here, a second proof is presented by decomposing the concept of necessity into two constraints. The first constraint—that existence is not impossible—is shown to be positive through logical analysis of negation. Understanding this proof reinforces the positive nature of wajub.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two constraints
Read the passage and locate the two constraints that compose wajib: (1) its existence is not impossible, (2) its non-existence is impossible. Notice that the author only analyses the first.
The second constraint is left aside because the first suffices.
2
Follow the argument for the first constraint
Trace the logic: impossibility is a negative constraint—if it were positive, its subject would exist. Pure non-existence cannot have a positive attribute. Thus, negating impossibility is a negation of non-existence, and negation of negation yields affirmation.
This shows 'not impossible' is a positive concept.
3
Understand why the second constraint is ignored
Ask yourself why the author does not need to analyze the second constraint. He states that the first investigation alone suffices to achieve the intended objective.
The proof does not require both constraints to be positive; one positive constraint already establishes that necessity is composite and positive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني : في بيان أن الوجوب مفهوم ثبوتي : أن الوجوب ماهية مركبة من قيدين ، أحدهما : أنه لا يمتنع وجوده . والثاني : أنه يمتنع عده. أما القيد الأول [وهو قولنا] إنه لا يمتنع وجوده ، فهذا مفهوم ثابت . لأن الامتناع قيد عدمي ، إذ لو كان موجودا ، لكان الموصوف به أولى أن يكون موجودا. ضرورة أن العدم المحض يمتنع كونه موصوفا بالصفة الموجودة ، وإذا ثبت أن الامتناع قيد عدمي ، كان نفيه نفيا للعدم ، ونفي النفي ثبوت ، فكان نفي الامتناع صفة موجودة ، وهذا يدل على أن هذا القيد مفهوم ثبوتي . وأما القيد الثاني ، وهو قولنا : إنه يمتنع عدمه، فلا يتعلق غرضنا ببيان كونه وجوديا أو عدميا ، لأن البحث الأول يكفي في إفادة المقصود
And the second proof: in clarifying that necessity is a positive concept: that necessity is a composite essence of two constraints: one: that its existence is not impossible. The second: that its non-existence is impossible. As for the first constraint [which is our saying] that its existence is not impossible, this is a fixed concept. Because impossibility is a negative constraint, for if it were existent, the attribute described by it would be more deserving of existence. Necessarily, pure non-existence cannot be described by an existent attribute. And when it is established that impossibility is a negative constraint, its negation is a negation of non-existence, and the negation of negation is affirmation, so the negation of impossibility is an existent attribute. And this indicates that this constraint is a positive concept. As for the second constraint, which is our saying that its non-existence is impossible, we have no purpose in clarifying whether it is positive or negative, because the first investigation suffices to achieve the intended objective.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof that wajub is positive. It analyses wajub into two constraints (qaydayn): (1) that its existence is not impossible, and (2) that its non-existence is impossible. The author focuses on the first constraint, showing it is positive. He argues that impossibility (imtina') is a negative constraint, because if it were positive, the subject described by it would be existent, but pure non-existence cannot have a positive attribute. Since impossibility is negative, its negation (i.e., 'not impossible') is a negation of non-existence, and negation of negation is affirmation (thubut). Thus the first constraint is a positive concept. The second constraint is left aside because the first alone suffices for the proof.

Key Terms
وجوب
قيد
امتناع
عدم
نفي النفي
ثبوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'not impossible' is a negative statement because it contains the word 'not'.
Correction: The author shows that 'not impossible' is a double negation of non-existence, which results in affirmation. Thus it is a positive concept.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the first constraint 'not impossible' to be a positive concept?
A) Because impossibility is a positive attribute, so its negation is negative.
B) Because it is a negation of a negative, which yields affirmation.
C) Because it is a mental concept only.
D) Because it refers to existence itself.

Key Takeaway
The second proof confirms that necessity (wajib) is a positive concept by showing that one of its constituent constraints—'existence is not impossible'—is an affirmation through the logic of double negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just studied a logical proof that necessity is a positive concept. When you reflect on your belief in God (the Necessary Being), do you merely think of Him as 'not impossible', or do you truly affirm His existence as a positive reality in your heart? How might this logical distinction affect the strength of your certainty (yaqeen)?
?
The proof hinges on the idea that a negation of a negative becomes an affirmation. Is there any area of your faith where you have settled for a double negative—like 'I don't doubt that Allah exists'—rather than actively affirming a positive conviction? What step can you take today to move from 'not doubting' to 'affirming without hesitation'?
?
If the very concept of necessity is positive, what does that imply about your duty to know and worship the Necessary Being? Does your level of earnestness in seeking knowledge of Allah match the weight of this truth, or do you treat it as a mere intellectual exercise? What will you change starting now?
